Jerusalem Talmud Demai
תלמוד ירושלמי דמאי
The Jerusalem Talmud, translation and commentary by Heinrich W. Guggenheimer. Berlin, De Gruyter, 1999-2015
https://www.nli.org.il/he/books/NNL_ALEPH001901012/NLI

Jerusalem Talmud Demai



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The easy ones for <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The definition of <i>demay</i> is given in the Introduction. The “easy ones” are those to which the laws of <i>demay</i> do not apply and which may be bought from any source without separating heave or tithe.</i> are <i>shitin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">S. Lieberman has convincingly argued that שיתין is a contraction of שחיתין “garbage fruit” in the Galilean dialect which had no ח sound (compare <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 2:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.2.4.2"><i>Peah</i> 2:5, Note 86</a>.) It will be explained that these are figs growing under the leaves. Since figs need much sun to ripen, normally figs grow on top of the leaves, exposed to the sun. Those growing under the leaves remain hard and inedible at harvest time and are not usually harvested. Hence, these figs are to be considered abandoned property after the harvest and are not subject to any laws of heave and tithes.</i>, lotus fruit<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Definition of Maimonides, Arabic אַלנַבַּק. In some Mishnah and Tosephta manuscripts the reading is דימין and <i>Caftor Waperach</i>, ed. Luncz, Jerusalem 1899, p. 526, notes that in Falastini Arabic the fruit is called אַלדוּם.</i>, the fruit of the service tree, white figs<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Identification of R. Joḥanan in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 40b" href="/Berakhot.40b">Babli (<i>Berakhot</i> 40b</a>).</i>, and sycamore figs, fallen dates<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Either fallen because they are rotten or wormy inside or knocked down by a windstorm.</i>, fennel<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See later, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 1:1:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.1.1.12">Note 55</a>; cf. I. Löw, <i>Flora der Juden</i> 3, p. 462. Maimonides declares it to be “a relative of dill.” The Arukh declares it to be wild grapes, Italian <i>lambrusco</i>. This follows the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 40b" href="/Berakhot.40b">Babli (<i>Berakhot</i> 40b</a>) which defines it to be grapes that ripen only after the harvest.</i>, and capers<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Concurrent definition of Arukh (<i>cappero</i>) and Maimonides. This refers to the fruit only.</i>. Also in Judea sumac, vinegar, and coriander. Rebbi Jehudah says, all <i>shitin</i> are free except those from a tree bearing fruit twice a year, all lotus fruit are free except lotus fruit from Shiqmanah<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Identified as a village Sycaminon at the foot of Mt. Carmel, South of Haifa.</i>, and all sycamore fruits are free except those cracked open<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Cracked open while still on the tree, they will become sweet enough to qualify as human food. The others are only considered animal fodder. The sweetness of cracked sycamores was already noted by Pliny, <i>Naturalis Historia</i>, Book XIII, §56. In all these instances, R. Jehudah insists that the fruits do not grow wild and/or are not abandoned.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Joḥanan said, the Sages enumerated them because the majority of these kinds are collected only from abandoned property<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Either they are leftovers after the harvest and actually abandoned, or they are from shrubs and trees that are never harvested and, therefore, the fruits can be treated as abandoned even though they grow on private property.</i>.
Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, only <i>demay</i> is mentioned, hence one is obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">If it is certain that the tithes were not given, one is obligated to give them in full in any case, including the kinds mentioned in the Mishnah. Only if it is <i>demay</i> can one get away with giving only 1%. (This argument appears anonymously in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 40b" href="/Berakhot.40b">Babli <i>Berakhot</i>40b</a>.)</i> if they are certain. Rebbi Joḥanan said, they are free whether <i>demay</i> or certain<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The kinds enumerated in the Mishnah are not subject to any tithes.</i>. It is difficult for Rebbi Joḥanan, didn’t they mention only <i>demay?</i> Hence if it is certain, is it not obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan’s position would be acceptable if the Mishnah were in one of the tractates <i>Terumot</i> or <i>Ma‘serot</i>, but here it refers explicitly to <i>demay</i>.</i>? Rebbi La said, since at every place<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In this tractate. The mention of <i>demay</i> is a mnemotechnic device which starts tractate <i>Demay</i>. It has no intrinsic meaning.</i> he considers only <i>demay</i>, here he quotes only <i>demay</i>.
The Mishnah supports Rebbi Joḥanan: “From Akhzib<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">A place whose ruins are on the seashore, just South of the Lebanese border, mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 19:29" href="/Joshua.19.29"><i>Jos</i>. 19:29</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 1:31" href="/Judges.1.31"><i>Jud</i>. 1:31</a>. Along the seashore, Kezib or Akhzib denotes the northernmost point of contiguous settlement by Jews during the Second Commenwealth. As explained in the Introduction, agricultural land outside this area is in principle free from all obligations imposed on the Land. (Exceptions are discussed in Chapter 2, Halakhah 1 and, for the Tithe of the Poor only, in Tractates <i>Ma‘serot</i> and <i>Ševiït</i>.)</i> and further on<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">To the North, into Phoenician territory.</i> it is free from <i>demay</i>.” Is not from Akhzib and further on no difference between <i>demay</i> and certainty? Here also there is no difference between <i>demay</i> and certainty<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Certainty that no tithes were given.</i>.
A <i>baraita</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 1:1</a>: “The easy ones for <i>demay</i>,<i>shitin</i>, lotus fruit, and the fruit of the service tree, may be trusted everywhere to be free (from obligation of tithes), but if they are kept in storage they are obligated.”</i> disagrees with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: “If they were kept in storage they are obligated.” Did they not essentially come from abandoned property? He explains it, if most people keep them in storage<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">At such a place, nobody abandons the fruits and they are not free from tithes.</i>. Come and see, if most people keep them in storage, it is everybody’s opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Both R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i> that they are obligated, be it <i>demay</i> or certain. If most people do not keep them in storage, it is everybody’s opinion that they are free, be it <i>demay</i> or certain. Half and half you cannot consider<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">This would be the only practical point of difference between R. Joḥanan, who frees the fruits from tithes, and R. Simeon ben Laqish, who submits them to tithes.</i> since we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Makhshirin 2:10" href="/Mishnah_Makhshirin.2.10">Mishnah <i>Makhshirin</i> 2:10</a>: “If someone finds produce on the road, if most people store it in their houses, it is free” (since, then, everybody must give heave and tithes at his barn and the few things lost on the road are included,) “if most people transport it to sell on the market, it is obligated” (since the seller may nibble from it occasionally without tithes and the future buyer, who has no relationship to the harvester, is obligated for tithes,) “half and half is <i>demay</i>.” Hence, there seems to be no way to relax the standard for a fifty-fifty doubt.</i>: “Half and half is <i>demay</i>.” Rebbi Zeïra said, not only the easy ones for <i>demay</i> but even <i>demay</i> itself<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah <i>Makhshirin</i> does not deal with our case, but with commercial produce; hence, it has no implications here and we may reduce the difference between R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish to this particular case.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">As R. M. Margalit points out, this passage seems to be Amoraic, not Tannaitic as usually associated with the expression “stated.”</i>: If one enters a town inhabited mostly by Gentiles, if there is a doubt whether the majority keep them in storage<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The kinds mentioned in the Mishnah.</i> or not, everybody agrees<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">In Halakhah 2:1, in reference to a list in the Mishnah about produce that must be treated as <i>demay</i> everywhere, even outside the Land of Israel, since usually these are produce of the Land. There R. Joḥanan asserts that the requirement to treat them as <i>demay</i> equally applies to fruits bought from Gentiles or from Jews, but R. Eleazar only treats produce bought from Jews as <i>demay</i> and insists that produce bought from Gentiles is certainly not in order and one also has to give the heave. Everybody agrees with R. Meïr that Gentile ownership of land in the domain settled by the returnees from Babylonia does not free the land from the obligation of heaves and tithes, against the ruling of the Babli (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 4:6:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.4.6.9"><i>Peah</i>, Chapter 4, Notes 129–131</a>). “Everybody” referred to here, however, are R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish, whose disagreement is explained in a second case.</i> that they are obligated, be it <i>demay</i> or certain<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Demay</i> always for R. Joḥanan, certain for R. Eleazar if bought from a Gentile. Even these are “easy ones”, since the Gentiles certainly did not give heave and tithes; the twofold doubt which is the basis of the leniencies of <i>demay</i>, as explained in the Introduction, does not apply. There is only a single doubt, viz., whether the produce is obligated for tithes or not, and this, being of Biblical origin, must in case of doubt be resolved in a restrictive way.</i>. If one enters a town inhabited mostly by Jews, if there is a doubt whether the majority keep them in storage or not, everybody agrees that they are free, be it <i>demay</i> or certain<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the preceding paragraph.</i>. If there is any doubt whether there is a majority of Gentiles or a majority of Jews, that is the disagreement between Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish.
These<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the detailed discussion of the Mishnah.</i> are the <i>shitim</i>: Rebbi Simeon, the son of Rebbi Abba<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">In the Rome manuscript: R. Ayvu. However, R. Ayvu was a later aggadic preacher and nothing is known of his family. R. Simeon bar Abba was a Galilean scholar of the second generation, a relative of Samuel of Nahardea; he was a great scholar and always very poor. When Nahardea was destroyed by Odenatus, two of Samuel’s daughters were taken captive and redeemed in Galilee. R. Simeon bar Abba married first one, then after the early death of his wife the other, who also died soon.</i>: Those appearing under the leaves.
“<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).1.3">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 1:3</a>: “Early and late fruits in a garden are obligated; on the field they are exempt. These are the early fruits, before one appoints a watchman, the late fruits, after they folded up the mats. Rebbi Illaï said in the name of Rebbi Eliezer, the early ones are obligated because of an assumption that they are being watched.”</i> Early<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">They are ripe before there is a market for them. The late ones are unripe at harvest time, so they are abandoned on the field. Here one speaks of valuable fruits, not those mentioned in the Mishnah.</i> and late fruits are exempt. These are the early fruits, before one appoints a watchman.” But does this depend on the watchman<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is an individual decision, it is not a legal standard.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, if they are not worth the attention of a watchman. “These are the late fruits, after they folded up the mats<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">To store the fruits on the field, to shield them from dirt and dust before they are transported to the farmhouse.</i>. Rebbi Illaï said in the name of Rebbi Eliezer, the early ones are obligated because of an assumption that they are being watched<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since there always is a market for <i>primeurs</i>.</i>.” Rebbi Yose ben Ḥalaphta said, <i>shitin</i> in Sepphoris are obligated because of an assumption that they are watched<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Probably, to make vegetable rennet. This statement is also Tannaitic; it is not in our Tosephta but may come from a Yerushalmi version of the Tosephta.</i>.
If the fig harvest was finished and he watches his field because of grapes, grape harvest and he watches his field because of vegetables, if a worker enters there and the owner is offended, they are forbidden because of robbery<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">(There is a similar text in Tosephta <i>Ma’serot</i> 3:12 and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 6b" href="/Pesachim.6b">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 6b</a>, but it deals with the obligation to give tithes.) By Biblical ordinance (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:25" href="/Deuteronomy.23.25"><i>Deut</i>. 23:25</a>), a worker is entitled to eat from the produce he is harvesting. This implies that without the owner’s consent he cannot eat from produce he is not harvesting. Hence, if the owner objects to his workers going through those parts of his property that are already harvested, the owner retains all property rights to the unripe produce still standing on the field and, <i>a fortiori</i>, the unripe produce is not abandoned, may not be taken by other people, and is not freed from the obligations of heave and tithes.</i>.
Ulla bar Ismael<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, he is simply called Ulla. A student of R. Joḥanan, he was a frequent traveller to Babylonia and an important link between the Galilean and Babylonian sages. He died during one of his trips there.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Rebbi and Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">A son of R. Jehudah bar Illaï, constant companion of Rebbi, and influential in the composition of the Mishnah.</i> entered to eat of late fruits; the watchman shouted at them, and Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah stopped. Rebbi told him: eat, because the owners already gave up their expectations for them<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">יאוש is the technical term for giving up hope to regain a lost article. Rebbi seems to imply that the law made them give up hope. (Arabic איאס “giving up hope”.)</i>! Rebbi Joḥanan asked: He shouted, and you say so<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>baraita</i> of the previous paragraph bars people from taking late fruits if they are guarded. How could Rebbi permit taking these fruits?</i>? Rebbi Jonah said, he objects rightfully. But does not a Mishnah disagree: “<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ma’serot</i> 3:9. The plants mentioned are weeds used as spices. If they are close to the house and tended, they are kitchen herbs and as private property obligated for tithes. In all other circumstances, they are treated as weeds and free from heave and tithes.</i> Calamint, hyssop, and thyme in the courtyard are obligated if they are tended.” Hence, in the garden they are free even if they are watched. There<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Spice-weeds are everywhere but late fruits are watched; Rebbi was in error when he applied the ruling of the Mishnah in <i>Ma’serot</i> to his case.</i>, he may say, the entire world is before you, but here, there is one basketful and I am watching it for my employer.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 1:1</a>. The next sentence there reads: “But the Sages say, they are free as long as they do not ferment; once they start to ferment they are obligated.” Fermented dates are used for date wine.</i>: “Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says, fallen dates sold with regular dates are free<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">“Free” means not under the laws of either heave and tithes or those of <i>demay</i>. “Obligated” means that they fall under both kinds of laws. The reading “free” is in all Yerushalmi manuscripts except that of R. S. Cirillo, but the latter most probably represents an emendation by its author. In all Tosephta texts, the reading is “obligated.” Since the reading is “free” in both the Leyden and the Rome manuscripts, it must be very old. Nevertheless, it must represent a scribal error, and the reading must be “obligated;” otherwise the statement would be superfluous and contradicting the following discussion.</i>.” Is it about two boxes, one beside the other or one on top of the other<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Is it restricted to a case where the buyer can directly check what he is getting (if good and fallen dates are in separate boxes)?</i>? No, here we deal with a mixed bunch. Did they start to ferment<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, the fallen dates can be used to make date liquor and are commercially valuable. If they are fallen and did not start to ferment probably they are not sweet enough to qualify as human food and are valueless.</i> or did they not start to ferment? Rebbi Mana said, we deal with fermenting ones. If they started to ferment, they should be obligated for themselves<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">And subject to <i>demay</i> even if not mixed with good dates.</i>! You cannot say that, since Rebbi Joḥanan said, the Sages detailed them because the majority of these kinds are collected only from abandoned property<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Abandoned property is exempt even if valuable.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina said, we deal with nonfermenting ones. If we deal with nonfermenting ones they should be free even when mixed<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Since they have no value.</i>. You cannot say that, since it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">This statement does not appear in any other collection.</i>: “Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose said in his father’s name that a bunch of grapes of which one berry ripened, is all connected for tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">One good grape makes the entire bunch subject to the laws of heave and tithes. Hence, one good fruit in a box makes the entire box subject to the laws of heave and tithes.</i>.” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rebbi Zeïra and Rebbi Illaï, one adopted the first opinion, the other the second opinion. But the Sages say one does not follow either of them; those fermenting are obligated, those not fermenting are free<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">An adaptation of the Tosephta text quoted at the start of this paragraph, turned into a statement of Amoraic practice, adopted by Maimonides (<i>Maäser</i> 13:1). The statement of the Mishnah is qualified to apply only to dates that are so devoid of sugar that they do not ferment.</i>.
<i>Gufnān</i> is <i>šāmērāh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic שַמרַה, fennel.</i>. What is the difference between Judea and Galilee? Since one has a popular saying in Galilee: <i>šāmērāh</i>,<i>šĕmar mārāh</i> (its owner should watch it), would anybody compare it to spices<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">That a weed can be used in the kitchen does not make it a spice. Hence, in Galilee where fennel grows wild, it is not a cultivated plant, in contrast to arid Judea where it needs tending.</i>? That means, in Galilee it is free but in Judea it is obligated.
Coriander is <i>kusbārātā</i><sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Formally, the feminine form of the word for coriander, but, as seen in the next Note, denoting a weed.</i>. What is the difference between Judea and Galilee? Since one has a popular saying in the South: Coriander is <i>kusbārātā</i><sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Taking the Arabic <i>kuzbara(t)</i> for a guide, this form means wild growing adiantum (or it may mean <i>kuzbarat alṯalab</i>, pimpernel, <i>kuzbarat ḵaḍra</i>’, chervil, <i>kuzbarat alṣaḵr</i>, haircap-moss, <i>kuzbarat alḥamām</i>, Fumaria officinalis). <i>Kusbārātā</i> is not considered cultivated.</i>, would anybody compare it to spices? That means, in Galilee it is obligated but in Judea it is free.
It was stated: “Rebbi Jehudah said, in earlier times, vinegar in Judea was exempt from tithes. Since they made all their wine in purity for Temple offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In the time of the Second Temple, all wine and oil for the Temple had to come from Judea since the two blocks of Jewish settlement, Judea and Galilee, were not contiguous. Hence it was impossible to transport wine or oil from Galilee to Judea without passing over impure Gentile territory, since even Samaritan Samaria was separated from Galilee by a strip of land occupied by Gentiles.</i>, it never got sour; they made vinegar from the skins of grapes. But now, since wine turns sour<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Wine-vinegar is really wine turned sour; it is subject to all rules of wine. A similar anonymous statement appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 1:2" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).1.2">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 1:2</a>: “In earlier times, vinegar was exempt in Jehudah since it was presumed to be made from after-wine, but now it is presumed to be from wine and is obligated.”</i>, it is obligated.” The argument of Rebbi Jehudah is inverted since we stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ma‘serot</i> 5:6.</i>: “If one makes after-wine and puts in a given volume of water, if he finds exactly the same volume it is free, but Rebbi Jehudah obligates him<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">According to the first Tanna, after-wine is water which absorbed some taste from the grape skins. According to Rebbi Jehudah, it is wine mixed with water. Since they always drank their wine mixed with water, this is regular wine and subject to its rules. This contradicts Rebbi Jehudah’s statement in the earlier <i>baraita</i>.</i>.” And here he says so! Rebbi La said, in earlier times when grapes were plentiful, their skins were not counted as anything<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Afterwine was worthless and thus not subject to tithes; now it is traded as merchandise.</i>, but now that grapes are not plentiful, the skins are important.
Rebbi Huna said: Rebbi Jeremiah asked, does this not contradict Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">This question seems directed both to the problem of vinegar just discussed and the statement of R. Jehudah in the Mishnah, that all lotus fruit is exempt except that of Shiqmana, since R. Jehudah and the anonymous Tosephta exempt all vinegar, even when it is known not to have been tithed. Similarly, a doubt whether lotus fruit is valued at some place should not make them obligated since there still is a twofold doubt: perhaps these fruits come from another place, and if they do come from Shiqmana, perhaps they are not gathered to be stored. This is exactly the case in which R. Simeon ben Laqish declares them to be free. There is no problem for R. Joḥanan since he equates the rules for untithed and <i>demay</i> in all these cases.</i>? Should not all lotus fruit be more than the lotus fruit of Shiqmana, should they not be free? Rebbi Yose said, explain it at a place where most people store it<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Not only do they store it but, since it is an object of trade only at this place. There is no doubt that the fruits in question are local.</i>. Rebbi Yose the Great<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Not known otherwise. Possibly it should read: Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi X. But the second name is also missing in the Rome manuscript. The question is not answered.</i> asked: Is not the entire world before him, should not all lotus fruit be more than the lotus fruit of this place and be free?

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <i>Demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The subject is the Second Tithe. How far the Mishnah might be applicable to the heave of the tithe will be discussed in the Halakhah.</i> is not subject to a fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">If the Second Tithe is redeemed by money so that the produce itself becomes profane, one fifth (from above, a quarter from below) must be added (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:31" href="/Leviticus.27.31"><i>Lev</i>. 27:31</a>). This does not apply to the Second Tithe of <i>Demay</i>.</i> or to elimination<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The Second Tithe must be consumed at the latest during the year when the Tithe of the Poor is due, i. e., the tithes of years 1, 2 of the Sabbatical cycle must be eaten before the end of year 3, those of years 4,5 by the end of year 6 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:13" href="/Deuteronomy.26.13"><i>Deut</i>. 26:13</a>).</i>, it may be eaten by a strict mourner<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The אונן, the strict mourner, is a close relative of a deceased person from the time of death to the end of the day of burial when he must concentrate on the duties of burial and is excluded from all other religious rites (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:14" href="/Deuteronomy.26.14"><i>Deut</i>. 26:14</a>). Since the Second Tithe of <i>demay</i> does not have the sanctity of Second Tithe, it is not deemed subject to that prohibition.</i>, it may enter Jerusalem and leave it<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Genuine Second Tithe may not leave Jerusalem after having entered but must be consumed there. This is the position of the anonymous Tanna in Mishnah <i>Maäser Šeni</i> 3:5. [According to Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, all Second Tithe may leave Jerusalem. In the Babli (<i>Baba Meẓia</i>‘ 54b) the prohibition is given rabbinic character only. Maimonides (<i>Maäser Šeni</i> 2:9) restricts this part of the Mishnah to <i>demay</i> itself, not the Second Tithe taken from <i>demay</i>, on the basis of a discussion in tractate <i>Maäser Šeni</i> 3:4, as explained by the commentators of Maimonides.]</i>, and one may destroy a small part of it on the road<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Genuine Second Tithe must be brought to Jerusalem intact. The Halakhah will define “small part”.</i>; he may give it to the <i>am haäreẓ</i> if he eats correspondingly<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>am haäreẓ</i> is a person who cannot be presumed to eat his food, other than sacrifices, in ritual purity; cf. the definition of <i>am haäreẓ</i> in the Introduction. The Second Tithe is not a sacrifice but nevertheless must be eaten in ritual purity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:16" href="/Deuteronomy.24.16"><i>Deut</i>. 24:16</a>); hence, genuine Second Tithe may not be shared with the <i>am haäreẓ</i>. For the Second Tithe of <i>demay</i>, if it is too little for a meal, the owner may give that to the <i>am haäreẓ</i> in preference to have it entirely wasted, but nevertheless the owner has to spend of his own money to buy pure food in the same amount to eat in purity (Maimonides <i>Maäser Šeni</i>3:9).</i>, he may redeem silver for silver, copper for copper, silver for copper<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Genuine Second Tithe must be redeemed with silver coins (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:25" href="/Deuteronomy.14.25"><i>Deut</i>. 14:25</a>); it may not be redeemed in copper, neither may the silver be exchanged for copper. [As a matter of practice, it is permitted to exchange silver coins for gold coins to reduce their weight (<i>Maäser Šeni</i> 2:7).]</i>, and copper for produce<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Outside of Jerusalem; R. Meïr permits these vegetables to be redeemed again; the Sages require them to be eaten in Jerusalem. By contrast, everybody agrees that it is possible to exchange coins many times for other coins.</i> if he again redeems the produce, the words of Rebbi Meïr. But the Sages say, produce should be brought up and eaten in Jerusalem.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Not following the High Priest Joḥanan who abolished the declaration of tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Maäser Šeni</i> 5:13: “The High Priest Joḥanan removed the declaration of tithes.” Since at the return from Babylonia the priests returned <i>en masse</i> but almost no Levites came, the returnees decreed to give the First Tithe to the Cohen (who automatically also is a Levite.) The declaration of tithes, to be recited when the Second Tithe was brought to Jerusalem, is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:13-14" href="/Deuteronomy.26.13-14"><i>Deut</i>. 26:13–14</a>: “You shall say before the Eternal, your God: I have eliminated the holy matter from my house, I gave it to the Levite (the First Tithe), the stranger, the orphan, and the widow (the Tithe of the Poor), following all Your ordinances that You commanded me, I did not transgress Your ordinances and I did not forget. I did not eat from it in my strict mourning, I did not eliminate it (by eating) in impurity, I did not give of it to the dead. I listened to the voice of the Eternal, my God, I did all that You commanded me.” The High Priest found it inappropriate to declare that tithes were given to the Levite when in fact they went to the Cohen.</i>. He abolished the declaration but one still has to eliminate, i. e., eliminate the sure tithes. But <i>demay</i> one does not have to eliminate<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">There is no contradiction to the Mishnah in <i>Maäser Šeni.</i></i>.
It was stated: It may be eaten in strict mourning but not in impurity. What is the difference between strict mourning and impurity? Rav Naḥman said, impurity is frequent, strict mourning is infrequent. They made a decree for a frequent situation, they did not decree for an infrequent situation<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">This principle is also invoked several times in the Babli, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 56b" href="/Ketubot.56b"><i>Ketubot</i>56b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 55a" href="/Nazir.55a"><i>Nazir</i> 55a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, they did not even make it similar to a doubt of <i>ṭevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ṭevel</i> is defined in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:5:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.5.7"><i>Peah</i>, Chapter 1, Note 303</a>. Produce that may be <i>Ṭevel</i> must be treated as if the holy parts were still in it.</i>. Since for a doubt of <i>ṭevel</i>, whether Second Tithe was given in order, or maybe Second Tithe was not given in order, is that not forbidden for the strict mourner<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Since a doubt in matters of Biblical prohibitions always must be treated as prohibition.</i>? But here, it is permitted to the mourner.
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Baba Meẓia</i>‘ 3:7. The Mishnah enumerates five cases in which one has to add a fifth to the redemption money.</i>: “Heave, the heave of the tithe, and the heave of the tithe of <i>demay</i>.” And here you say so<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">If the Mishnah here also deals with the heave of the tithe of <i>demay</i>, it would be an obvious contradiction of one Mishnah against the other.</i>! Rebbi Zeïra said, there we have stated heave of the tithe of <i>demay</i> but here Second Tithe of <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah does not deal with heave at all.</i>. Rebbi Immi said, the Mishnah does not stop to deal with the heave of the tithe of <i>demay</i>. What is that? There it is Rebbi Meïr, but here the rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Usually, an anonymous Mishnah gives the opinion of R. Meïr. R. Immi asserts that as an exception, this Mishnah does not.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra said in the name of the rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">He gives the argument to support his own position. Since it is undisputed, it represents practice.</i>: It would have been logical that for the heave of the tithe of <i>demay</i> one would not have to add a fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Since one does not add a fifth for <i>demay</i> as stated in the Mishnah.</i>; why did they say that one has to add a fifth? Because of a fence for it<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbinic ordinances are always described as “a fence around the Law” (Mishnah <i>Abot</i> 1:1). Babylonian גזירה “rabbinic ordinance” is a derivative of Galilean גדירה “fence”, <i>z</i> for ð.</i>, for if you tell him that he does not have to add, he will not treat it as holy. It would have been logical that for the Second Tithe of <i>demay</i> one would have to add a fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">If one adds one for the heave of the tithe, since the Second Tithe also has to be consumed in purity.</i>; why did they say that one does not have to add a fifth? Because of a fence for it, for if you tell him that he has to add, he will not separate it at all<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">One requires the separation of Second Tithe of <i>demay</i>, in contrast to the First Tithe and the tithe of the poor, since the Second Tithe will not burden the buyer. Anything that makes it burdensome (and, in the absence of the Temple, when the coin used for redemption has to be destroyed, also costly) will cause people to ignore their duty.</i>.
Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked before Rebbi La<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the Mishnah: “one may destroy a small part of it on the road.” The question is again whether this refers only to the Second Tithe or also to the heave of the tithe. It seems that the same interpretation given to the question of the fifth applies here also.</i>: Why <i>demay</i> and not also what is certain<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Heave or Second Tithe, which are unquestionably holy, being given straight from the harvest, or on information that the produce is still <i>ṭevel</i>.</i>, why a little bit, maybe the greater part? Did we not state: One does not bring the heave from the threshing floor to town, nor from the prairie to cultivated land<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The recipient of heave is required to collect it where it is due and remove it himself.</i>, except in a place where [an animal<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">While there is no direct manuscript evidence, it seems that היתה גררתה is a misspelling for חיה גררתה. The reading היתה is an old one since R. Simson of Sens reads היתה גדודתה and explains “it was in danger of armies (or groups of robbers).” גררתה seems to be the better reading.</i>] would drag it away; in that case he brings it and takes money from the tribe<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The Cohen who took the heave has to pay the cost of transportation. The question presupposes that the Mishnah also applies to the heave of the tithe from <i>demay</i>.</i>. He<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi La to Rebbi Abun. If the heave is abandoned until some Cohen will come to pick it up, it probably will be lost.</i> said to him, is it not a commandment to return a find? Rebbi Yose said, it is a commandment to return a find in small matters<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">If presenting the heave to the Cohen involves little effort and little expenditure, one is certainly obligated to hand over the heave to the Cohen to prevent it from being lost. However, if the effort or expenditure were large, one has no such obligation. (Explanation of R. Eliahu Wilna.)</i>; it is not a commandment to return in large matters, except if it is certain; it is not a commandment to return <i>demay</i> since we have stated: “one may destroy a small part of it on the road”. So far, if he had no money in hand<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">If someone has difficulty handling the Second Tithe, rather than let some of it get lost as authorized in the Mishnah, would it not be better to have the owner redeem it for money which will not get spoiled or lost? Should we restrict the Mishnah to the most unlikely case where the owner has no money for redemption?</i>. If he had money in hand? Rebbi Neḥumai<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Nothing else is known about him. Neḥumai is a Babylonian name; the family of R. Ḥiyya bar Abba was Babylonian.</i>, the son of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba, said, my father had money in a double sack<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Byzantine Greek δισάκκιον “double sack”, hanging down on both sides of a donkey; cf. <i>Berakhot</i> p. 319.</i> and he did not redeem. It turns out that Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba holds with Rebbi Zeïra<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Since he permits the Second Tithe of <i>demay</i> to go to waste, he explains the Mishnah here to refer exclusively to Second Tithe. Since in the times of R. Ḥiyya bar Abba heave already had to be burned because all Cohanim were ritually impure in the absence of the ashes of the red heifer, the reference could not possibly be to heave.</i> and Rebbi Aḥa<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Since Rebbi Aḥa is not mentioned in the entire Halakhah, this must be a scribal error for Rebbi La, who explains the Mishnah in <i>Baba Meẓia‘</i> by the duty of conserving other people’s property in the same way as something found. Hence, R. La accepts that the Mishnah here also speaks of the heave of the tithe of <i>demay</i>, as proposed by R. Immi.</i> with Rebbi Immi.
In the house of Rebbi Yannai they said, if it is in crumbs one is permitted to waste in amounts less than a meal<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Generally taken to be the volume of a chicken egg.</i>, but whole foods only up to the volume of a dried fig. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of R. Simeon ben Yoẓadaq: If it is whole, up to the volume of a dried fig, in crumbs any amount<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">In this version, R. Joḥanan is more lenient than the House of R. Yannai; in the alternative version he is more restrictive. Maimonides interprets “up to” as “less than,” R. Abraham ben David as “not more than.”</i>. Is that their difference? Rebbi Mana said, is not the following their difference: In the house of Rebbi Yannai they said, if it is in crumbs one is permitted to waste in amounts less than a meal, whole foods only up to the volume of a dried fig. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of R. Simeon ben Yoẓadaq, whether it be whole or in crumbs, up to the volume of a dried fig.
Rebbi Hoshaia asked: May one allow a small amount to go to waste<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">If one has to give only a small amount, less than the volume of a dried fig, and he has no other use for it, may he give it to the <i>am haäreẓ</i> or does he have the right to directly throw it away? (R. Eliahu Fulda and his followers change לאבד into להפריש, “to separate”, but this emendation is unnecessary.) Since the question is not answered it should be answered in the negative (Maimonides <i>Maäser Šeni</i> 3:9, with concurrence of R. Abraham ben David.)</i>? One may give it to the <i>am haäreẓ</i> and eat from <i>demay</i> accordingly. But not for certain food<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Food for which it is certain that it has to be consumed in ritual purity.</i>, since one does not hand over certain food to the <i>am haäreẓ</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who buys as seeds or animal fodder, flour for tanning, oil for lighting, or oil to rub on vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">However, oil used as lotion is subject to <i>demay</i>, since rubbing on is everywhere equated to drinking.</i>, is free from <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The obligation of heave and tithes falls only on foodstuffs, not on industrial materials.</i>. From Akhzib and further he is free from <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Explained in Halakhah 1.</i>. <i>Ḥallah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">The heave of the dough made for bread, which is given to the Cohen.</i> of the <i>am haäreẓ</i>, food containing heave<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Which either has to be consumed by the priest or heave has to be taken from it as certain heave. Heave is called דמע in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:28" href="/Exodus.22.28"><i>Ex</i>. 22:28</a>.</i>, food bought with money of the Second Tithe, and the remainders of flour sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">The offering of a handful for the altar was taken and the rest eaten by male Cohanim in the Temple precinct under the strict rules of sacrifices. For all of these, <i>demay</i> was never intended as it would make no sense.</i> are free from <i>demay</i>. Sweet oil<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Perfumed oil for the House of Shammai is a lotion, for the House of Hillel it is a perfume. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 1:3:16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.1.3.16">Note 155</a>.</i>, the House of Shammai declare it obligated but the House of Hillel declare it free.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Since he bought it as seeds there is no difference whether it is something of which the seed disappears or something of which the seed does not disappear<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to certain heave, where seeds which do not disappear in the new growth never lose their status as heave (Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 9:5).</i>. Rebbi Johanan said, we have stated this<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 1:18" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).1.18">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 1:18</a>, the first part of the statement discussed in the next paragraph.</i>: “If he bought as seeds and then changed his mind to use them for consumption, they enter<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">They enter into the obligation of <i>demay</i> by his thought.</i> by thought. If he bought for consumption and then changed his mind to use them as seeds, not all is from him<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning, nothing results from his thought; his intentions may introduce the obligation of <i>demay</i> but cannot remove it.</i>.”
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 1:18" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).1.18">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 1:18</a>: “One does not sow <i>ṭevel</i>, nor does one cover <i>ṭevel</i>; one does not work <i>ṭevel</i> with a Gentile. A Jew who forgot and sowed <i>ṭevel</i>, before it sprouted he is obligated, after it sprouted he is free because it already is lost.” The language of the Yerushalmi (Rome ms.) is preferable; since if one does not work on <i>ṭevel</i> with a Gentile, it is obvious that one cannot do it alone. [Maimonides (<i>Maäser</i> 3:6) omits the mention of the Gentile, possibly because of the two contradictory sources.]</i>: One does not sow <i>ṭevel</i>, one may not cover <i>ṭevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Produce lying on the ground (or in a ditch) may not be covered with earth before heaves have been given.</i>, but one covers <i>ṭevel</i> with a Gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">In joint ownership.</i>. If one forgot and sowed <i>ṭevel</i>, he is free<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">From giving heave and tithes from the seeds, since they no longer exist.</i>, for it is already lost if it is something that usually is not collected; but for something that usually is collected<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">Which can be recovered from the soil in usable (edible) state. In that case, he must remove the produce from the soil, give heaves, and only then may he return the remainder to the ground.</i>, he is fined to collect it before it sprouts. But if it sprouted, it turns into something that usually is not collected.
They said that Rebbi Joḥanan was putting in order<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">If anything of <i>demay</i> was used in cooking, he gave heave of the tithe and redeemed the Second Tithe for everything. “Putting in order” everywhere means giving heave and tithes to permit the food for consumption.</i> everything he ate, even red meat, even an egg. His students said to him: Did our teacher not teach us (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:22" href="/Deuteronomy.14.22"><i>Deut</i>. 14:22</a>): “You shall certainly tithe all the yield of your field<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Excluding anything not vegetal.</i>.” He was worried about the fluids<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Wine and oils used in cooking; because of the part absorbed in the meat, he gave heave from everything.</i> in it.
Rebbi Jeremiah sent Rebbi Zeïra a basket of figs that were not put in order. Rebbi Jeremiah said to himself, would Rebbi Zeïra eat anything that was not put in order? Rebbi Zeïra said to himself, it is impossible that Rebbi Jeremiah would send me anything that was not put in order. In the meantime, <i>ṭevel</i> was eaten. The next day, when he was with him, he asked him: Was that basket you sent me put in order? He answered him, I said, would Rebbi Zeïra eat anything that was not put in order? He answered, I also said, would Rebbi Jeremiah send me anything that was not put in order<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">The moral of the story is that among observant Jews it is necessary to spell out whether food is tithed or not. Food originating with the <i>am haäreẓ</i> is automatically put in order by the rules of <i>demay</i>; this story extends the duty of<i>demay</i> to food from the observant unless there is a declaration.</i>? Rebbi Abba bar Zemina<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">An Amora of the Fourth Generation, student of R. Zeïra.</i> said in the name of Rebbi Zeïra: If the earlier generations were angels, we are men; if they were men, we are donkeys<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 112b" href="/Shabbat.112b">Babli <i>Sabbat</i> 112b</a>. The parallel is <i>Šeqalim</i> fol. 48c, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 60" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.60">Midrash <i>Gen. rabba</i> 60</a>.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, at that moment<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">When <i>ṭevel</i> was eaten and nobody suspected that it was not in order.</i> they said, we are not even comparable to the she-ass of Rebbi Phineas ben Yaïr<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna of the Fifth Generation, son-in-law of R. Simeon bar Ioḥai. No halakhic statements of his are known. He is the paradigm of a holy man.</i>.
Thieves stole the she-ass of Rebbi Phineas ben Yaïr at night. She was hidden with them for three days during which she did not eat anything. After three days, they took counsel to return her to her master. They said, let us send her to her master lest she die with us and foul up the cave. They let her out; she stood at her master’s door and started braying. He said to them<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">“He” is always R. Phineas, “they” his family and servants.</i>, open to that poor creature because for three days now she did not taste anything. They opened for her and she entered. He said to them, give her something to eat. They put oats before her but she did not eat. They said to him, rabbi, she does not want to eat. He asked them, did you put it in order? They said: Yes. He asked them, did you remove its <i>demay</i>? They said, did the rabbi not teach us: “He who buys for seeds or animals, flour for tanning, oil for lighting, or oil to rub vessels, is free from <i>demay</i>.” He said to them, what can we do with this poor creature since she is very restrictive for herself<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">The she-ass refused to eat anything she could not eat if she were a Jewish human. A Babylonian version of the second part of the story is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 7" href="/Chullin.7">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 7a/b</a>; the first part of the story is told of the she-ass of R. Ḥanina ben Dosa in <i>Aboth de Rabbi Nathan</i>, Version 1, end of Chapter 8. Both stories are referred to in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 112b" href="/Shabbat.112b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>112b</a>. The Yerushalmi version is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 60" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.60"><i>Gen. rabba</i> 60</a>.</i>? They removed the <i>demay</i> and she ate.
Two poor people entrusted two <i>se’ah</i> of barley to Rebbi Phineas ben Yaïr; he sowed them and harvested them. They came to him and wanted to take their barley. He said to them, bring camels and donkeys to carry their barley<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">This story and the following one are told, in Hebrew, also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Devarim Rabbah 3:4" href="/Devarim_Rabbah.3.4">Midrash<i>Deut. rabba</i> 3(4</a>). There, he replanted the crop for seven years.</i>.
Rebbi Phineas ben Yaïr went to a place where they<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">The farmers of that place.</i> came to him and said, rats are eating our grain. He commanded them<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">The rats.</i>; they assembled and started to whistle. He asked them, do you understand what they are saying? They said, no. He said to them, they say that it is not put in order. They said to him, be our guarantor. He promised<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">That the rats would leave if all tithes were taken. It is implied that the townspeople complied and correctly gave all heave and tithes.</i> and they had no further losses.
A pearl of the king of Saracens<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear whether Arabs were already called Saracens at that time; related words arc Arabic שרק “Eastern” and סרּאק “big robber.”</i> fell and was swallowed by a rat. He came to Rebbi Phineas ben Yaïr, who said to him, am I a sorcerer? He answered, I came because of your good name. He commanded them<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">The rats.</i>; they assembled. He saw one coming that was hunchbacked. He said, it is in this one; he gave the rat a command and it spat out the pearl.
Rebbi Phineas ben Yaïr went to a place where they<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">The farmers of that place.</i> came to him and said, our water source is not sufficient for us. He said to them, perhaps you do not put in order? They said to him, be our guarantor. He promised them<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">That the rats would leave if all tithes were taken. It is implied that the townspeople complied and correctly gave all heave and tithes.</i> and it became sufficient for them.
Rebbi Phineas ben Yaïr went to the House of Assembly<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Probably the place at Ḥamat Gader at which the intercalation of months was decided.</i> when Ginai<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">A river, called “river Ginai” in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 7a" href="/Chullin.7a">Babli, <i>Ḥulin</i> 7a</a>, probably <i>Wadi Jenin</i>.</i> overflowed. He said: Ginai, Ginai, why do you prevent me from going to the House of Assembly? It split before him and he crossed it. His students asked him, may we cross also? He said to them, he who knows of himself that he never slighted a Jew may cross and will not be harmed.
Rebbi wanted to permit the Sabbatical year<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Since in the absence of a Temple and the distribution of land as ordered in the Torah, the Sabbatical year is observed as a Rabbinic ordinance, Rebbi wanted to allow cultivation of the land to help pay real estate taxes that were imposed irrespective of the yield. A generation later, R. Yannai did permit the cultivation of land for the payment of taxes and delivery of requisitioned produce to the Roman army (Yerushalmi <i>Ševiït</i> 4:2, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 26a" href="/Sanhedrin.26a">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i>26a</a>, cf. Rashi there.) The parallel to the entire story is in <i>Taäniot</i> 3:1.</i>. Rebbi Phineas ben Yaïr went to him. He said to him: how is the grain doing? He<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">R. Phineas ben Yaïr.</i> answered him: endives<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">They are irrelevant for taxes or the army.</i> are doing fine. He said to him: how is the grain doing? He answered him: endives are doing fine. From this, Rebbi understood that he did not agree with him. He said to him: Would the rabbi care to eat a bite with me today? He said to him, yes. When he came, he saw the she-mules<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>mulus. Yoma</i> 8:5, it is stated that a white mule’s kick is life-threatening. Hence, white mules should not be kept by Jews.</i> of Rebbi standing. He said, are all these fed by Jews? He will not see me again! They went and told this to Rebbi. Rebbi sent and wanted to pacify him. They found him in his city. He said, the people of my city should come close to me. The people of his city came and surrounded him. They said to them, Rebbi wants to make peace with him. They left him and went away. He said, my cousins<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Taäniot</i>: “My sons.”</i> should come close to me. Fire descended from Heaven and surrounded him. They returned and told Rebbi. He said, since we did not have merit to eat our fill from him in this world, may we be worthy to eat our fill from him in the World to Come.
Rebbi Ḥaggai in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">The text also appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 5:1:11-22" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.5.1.11-22"><i>Šeqalim</i> 5:2</a> and in slightly different form in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Devarim Rabbah 3" href="/Devarim_Rabbah.3">Midrash <i>Deut. rabba</i> 3</a>.</i>: It happened that a pious person used to dig cisterns, ditches, and caves for travellers. Once, his daughter travelled to marry and she was swept away by a river. All people came to him and wanted to console him, but he refused to be consoled. Rebbi Phineas ben Yaïr came to him and wanted to console him, but he refused to be consoled. He told them, is that your pious man? They said to him: Rabbi, such and such he did, such and such happened to him. He replied: It is impossible that he would honor his Creator by water and He hurts him by water! Immediately, there started a rumor in the town that the pious man’s daughter had returned. Some say, she got trapped in a hedge of thorns, others say that an angel came in the appearance of Rebbi Phineas ben Yaïr and saved her.
Rebbi Ḥanina ben Dosa was sitting down to eat on Friday night when the table sank before him. They said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Yerushalmi is notoriously inexact with genders and numbers, it might mean: He asked her (his wife).</i>, what is that? She said to him, I borrowed spices from my neighbor and forgot to give tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">It follows that the Yerushalmi requires tithing of spices, whereas the Babli requires it only when the spice may serve as food in itself; cf. <i>Tosafot</i> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 87b" href="/Yoma.87b"><i>Tosafot Yešenim</i> to <i>Yoma</i> 87b</a>.</i> from it. He mentioned a condition<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">He mentioned the place from which he would give tithes after the Sabbath; this is an acceptable form of tithing of <i>demay</i> during the twilight hours of Friday night (Mishnah <i>Sabbath</i>2:6). The story is inserted to show that a just person will not stumble unwittingly over a prohibition.</i> and the table rose by itself.
Rebbi Tarphon sat down to eat when he dropped his bread. They asked him, what is this? He said to them, I borrowed an axe and I prepared food in purity with it<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">Apparently the axe was not ritually pure and the bread that was made impure by this fell before he could eat it. As a Pharisee, R. Tarphon ate all his food in purity.</i>.
“<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 1:24" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).1.24">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 1:24</a>. There, the text continues: “If one buys wine and oil to put on abscesses, black peas to make powder, they are subject to <i>demay</i> but R. Simeon ben Eleazar declares it free.”</i> Wine for brine<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>muries</i>,<i>muria</i>, shortened from Greek ἁλμυρίς, ίδος, ἡ, “salty thing; salt land; saltiness” (E. G.).</i>, wine for perfume<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>olentia</i>, cf. <i>Berakhot</i> pp. 87, 510. According to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 140" href="/Shabbat.140">Babli (<i>Šabbat</i> 140 a</a>) a mixture of olive oil, wine, and balsamum.</i>, legumes to make into powder<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Pea or bean meal to be used as powder.</i> are subject to <i>demay</i>, not to mention if they are certain. They themselves<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">If one buys them ready-made.</i> are free from <i>demay</i>.” Where are we standing? According to Rebbi, they should be subject to <i>demay</i>, according to Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon, even if certain they should be free<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">The disagreement between Rebbi and R. Eleazar ben R. Simeon is stated in <i>Terumot</i> 11:1 (fol. 47c) “Wine of <i>terumah</i> {heave} to use for brine, Rebbi permits it {since the wine remains wine}, R. Eleazar ben R. Simeon forbids it {since it is a use of the heave not for direct consumption.} Hence, Rebbi forbids such brine for non-Cohanim {since the sanctity of the heave permeates all}, R. Simeon ben Eleazar permits it to non-Cohanim {since the wine has disappeared.}” Here also, if the wine etc. has disappeared, and if brine bought on the market is not subject to <i>demay</i>, for R. Eleazar ben R. Simeon even wine bought expressly for the production of brine cannot be subject to <i>demay</i>.</i>. But we follow Rebbi, we say we are dealing with the easy ones, for which they eased the rules of <i>demay</i>.
“Wine for eye medication<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κολλύριον. The text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 1:25" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).1.25">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 1:25</a> reads: “He who buys wine to put into eye medication or flour to put into wound dressing, is free from <i>demay</i> and obligated if it is certain. They themselves are free from <i>demay</i>.” It seems that this is a Babylonian text.</i>, flour to make into wound dressing<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">Greek μάλαγμα. If wine and flour are produced, they are subject to the duties of heave and tithes, no matter what the final use is. But if one buys medication which definitely is not food, no obligation exists even if heave was not given from grain or wine.</i>, are obligated if they are certain and one does not have to mention that they are under the rules of <i>demay</i>. They themselves are free if certain.” Here you say, free if certain, and there<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">In the previous paragraph, where wine and flour are used for exempt foods, but foods nevertheless. In medication, wine and flour are denatured; in foods, only their status is changed.</i> you say, free from <i>demay</i>. Here it disappears by itself, there it disappears when used.
Did we not state: “Eye medication from a pagan temple is forbidden to use?”<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">This implies that eye medication is valuable; why should it be free from heave and tithes?</i> There is a difference, since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 13:16" href="/Deuteronomy.13.16"><i>Deut</i>. 13:16</a>): “Nothing banned should stick to your hands.” What is the difference between this and grape skins from Gentiles? Are these not also forbidden to use<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">Since the pagan Gentile is assumed to have offered a libation from his newly pressed wine to his gods, all pagan wine is forbidden for any use whatsoever. (This is different from the prohibition to drink any Gentile wine, even from non-pagans.)</i>? But dried skins are not forbidden because of idolatrous use<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Mishnah <i>Avodah zarah</i> 2:4, skins remaining after pressing grapes for wine are forbidden to use if moist but not if completely dry. The question is not answered, probably because the answer is obvious: totally dry eye medication is unusable.</i>.
“<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Continuation of the discussion of the Mishnah.</i> From Akhzib and further on it is free from <i>demay</i>.” What is the status of Akhzib itself? It was stated (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).1.4">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 1:4</a>): “Akhzib itself is free from <i>demay</i>.”
“He who buys from a donkey caravan at Tyre and from the chest<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">The drawer of a trader, who sells staple goods which may come from any nearby region that is a major producer, including Galilee. It seems that in Tyre, which is much closer to Galilee, Galilean grain was usually sold directly by the transporter who brought the grain, not by the local merchant (Maimonides <i>Maäser</i> 13:6).</i> at Sidon is obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from a Tosephta. In our <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 1:10" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).1.10">Tosephta, <i>Demay</i> 1:10</a>, the statement is inverted: “He who buys from a donkey caravan at Tyre and from the chest at Sidon is free, from the chest at Tyre and the donkey caravan at Sidon is obligated. R. Yose ben R. Jehudah says, he who buys from the chest at Tyre is free, and certainly from the donkey caravan. From one donkey driver at Tyre he is obligated. R. Jehudah says, the donkey caravan that descends to Akhzib is obligated because it may be assumed that it comes from Galilee. {If it “descends”, it comes either from Galilee or from Tyre.} But the Sages say, it is in its prior state, free until you have ascertained from where it comes.”</i>.” Hence, from the donkey caravan at Sidon and from the chest at Tyre it is free. From a single donkey at Tyre<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">The status is not indicated. Since the Yerushalmi contradicts the Tosephta the inference is that it is free (Maimonides <i>Maäser</i> 13:6).</i>. A donkey caravan that entered Tyre through Akhzib? Come and see, if it were standing at Akhziv it would be free, now that it entered Tyre would it be obligated?
Rebbi Joḥanan said: When they decreed <i>demay</i>, they did not decree about these matters<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">The cases enumerated at the end of the Mishnah, “<i>Ḥallah</i> of the <i>am haäreẓ</i>, food containing heave, food bought with money of the Second Tithe, and the remainders of flour sacrifices are free from <i>demay</i>” were never included in a duty of <i>demay</i>; there is no need for an explanation why these are excluded.</i>. Rebbi Hoshaia said, the fear of sacred things is on him and he will not give to the Cohen anything that is not in order<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">Any one of these is connected with the Sanctuary and their consumption in violation of the rules is a deadly sin, the farmer’s sin if he tricks the Cohen into thinking that the produce was brought in order. Hence, it is rational to exclude these items from <i>demay</i> even if the original decree did not mention this exclusion.</i>. “<i>Ḥallah</i> of the <i>am haäreẓ</i>,” according to R. Hoshaia, the Mishnah deals with the <i>ḥallah</i> of an <i>am haäreẓ</i>. But a <i>ḥaver</i> who bought dough from an <i>am haäreẓ</i> and gave <i>ḥallah</i> is not covered<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">His heave of the tithes must include 10% of the amount given as <i>ḥallah</i>, since his case is not mentioned in the Mishnah.</i>. According to R. Joḥanan, the two cases are identical. “And food containing heave,” according to R. Hoshaia, the Mishnah deals with produce of an <i>am haäreẓ</i>. But if a <i>ḥaver</i> bought produce from an <i>am haäreẓ</i> and it became mixed with heave, he is not covered. According to R. Joḥanan, the two cases are identical. What is the difference between them<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">Is there a case where the difference is in the opposite direction, that R. Joḥanan requires putting in order and R. Hoshaia does not?</i>? One <i>seah</i> that was lifted from among 100 <i>seah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">If heave fell into profane food (of the same kind, so that the heave is no longer recognizable) the entire food becomes מדומע and is no longer usable by laymen. But if the volume of the profane food is at least 100 times that of the heave, one may lift food in the volume of the heave out of the mixture and declare this now to be the heave; the rest is profane and may be used by everybody. This is true only if the rest is truly profane food which may be used by everybody. Hence, before designating the lifted amount as heave, the owner has to be sure that the rest is profane, so he has to remove the heave of the tithe from it first. According to R. Hoshaia, the owner will do that, so that the remainder is in order even in the hands of the unobservant. R. Joḥanan does not accept the argument and any produce in the hands of the unobservant remains <i>demay</i>.</i>, according to R. Joḥanan it is obligated, according to R. Hoshaia it is free.
It was stated: “All of these<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">Mentioned in the Mishnah as not under the laws of <i>demay</i>.</i>, if he gave a name to their heave of the tithe or Second Tithe, what he did is done.<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 1:28" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).1.28">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 1:28</a> which, however, as noted by R. S. Lieberman, deals exclusively with matters of the Temple and, therefore, excludes the heave of the tithe.</i>” According to R. Joḥanan, this is fine<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">He is not required to give, but if he gives there is no objection.</i>. According to R. Hoshaia, they are in order, and you say so<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">Since one cannot give heave from produce that was already tithed and if he is sure the produce was tithed, he should declare that calling anything from it heave of the tithe is invalid.</i>? Because of one who is unscrupulous about sacred things<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Hoshaia, his assertion is one of probability that the produce will be in order, so that one may base one’s actions on it, not of total certainty.</i>. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>baraita</i> was found expressing the last argument.</i>: “All of these, if he gave a name to their heave of the tithe or Second Tithe, what he did is done, because of one who does not put in order.”
Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">He asked whether the exemption of food bought with money of the Second Tithe refers only to money from <i>demay</i> or also includes regular tithe. The answer is that all tithe money is exempt (R. Simson.)</i> before Rebbi Ze'ira: So far with money of tithes of <i>demay</i>? Even if it is certain!
It<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">The entire paragraph is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:7:2-8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.7.2-8.4"><i>Qiddušin</i> 2:8,</a> fol. 63a, which is its main place.</i> was stated: “All of these, if he gave a name to their heave of the tithe or Second Tithe, what he did is done.” Rebbi Eleazar said, with the exception of the remainders of flour sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">Since the flour sacrifice must be eaten by male priests in the Temple precinct but heave of the tithe can be eaten by all members of the Cohen’s family anywhere, it is clear that strict sanctity cannot be replaced by a less stringent one.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah said, the rest is in dispute. Rebbi Yose asked, what dispute? Where are we standing, if according to Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">The dispute is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.7.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 2:8</a>: “If a priest marries by his share in the holiest sacrifices or simple holy sacrifices, the marriage is not valid. With Second Tithes, be it with or without knowledge, the marriage is not valid, the words of R. Meïr. Rebbi Jehudah said, if in error, the marriage is not valid, if with knowledge, the marriage is valid.” In order to marry, the groom has to give the bride something valuable of his property while declaring before two witnesses that by accepting the gift she is married to him (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 6:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.6.2.6"><i>Peah</i> Chapter 6, Note 46</a>). Rebbi Meïr declares that holiest sacrifices, the Cohen’s share of simple sacrifices, and the Second Tithe are all Heaven’s property offered, as the case may be, to the Cohen, his family, or the layman and his family for consumption in Jerusalem. Hence, for R. Meïr, the basic conditions for a valid marriage are not satisfied.</i>, both for tithes and for the remainders of flour sacrifices, he did not do anything. If according to Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jehudah agrees that under normal circumstances, Second Tithe in Jerusalem cannot be redeemed. However, since it must be redeemed if it became ritually impure, it can also be redeemed unlawfully. His position is explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 7:5:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.7.5.10"><i>Peah</i>, Chapter 7, Note 135</a>.</i>, what he did is done. Rebbi Mana said, I went to Caesarea and heard Rebbi Ḥizqiah who was sitting there, stating: “If a priest marries by means of his share in the holiest sacrifices or simple holy sacrifices, the marriage is not valid.” Rebbi Eleazar said, that is everybody’s opinion. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is in dispute<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Jehudah states that the Cohen’s share of the sacrifices is his personal property. Hence, at least as far as simple sacrifices are concerned, the woman becomes his wife through the marriage and can legally consume the meat given to her. In that case, R. Jehudah also asserts that Second Tithe is always the owner’s property, even before redemption. However, it cannot be used as a marriage gift as is since there is a lien on it that it should be used only for consumption, and that lien must first be removed by redemption.</i>. I said to him, from whom did the rabbi hear this, and he said, from Rebbi Jeremiah. I said, this explains the matter! Rebbi Jeremiah, who heard that Rebbi Eleazar said, it is everybody’s opinion, he says it is in dispute<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">Transferring R. Joḥanan’s opinion from the Mishnah in <i>Qiddušin</i> to <i>Demay</i>.</i>. Rebbi Yose, who did not hear that, asked which dispute? If according to Rebbi Meïr, both for Second Tithe and for the remainders of flour sacrifices, he did not do anything, if according to Rebbi Jehudah, what he did is done.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">This is the discussion of the last statement in the Mishnah. A different text, in the name of R. Nathan and not mentioning iris, is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 1:26-27" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).1.26-27">Tosephta <i>Demay</i>1:26–27</a>. As R. S. Lieberman points out, it follows that putting some (not too much) natural perfume in oil does not make the oil unfit for human consumption even though it is destined for rubbing into the skin.</i>: Rebbi Jehudah said, the House of Hillel freed only spikenard oil<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>foliatum</i>, cf. Pliny, <i>Hist. Nat</i>. XIII, 2.</i>. Others say in the name of Rebbi Nathan, the House of Hillel obligated oil perfumed with rose or iris<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἴρινον “made from the iris”.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> With <i>demay</i>, one may make <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">There are two kinds of <i>eruv</i> (“mixing”) on the Sabbath. If many houses open into a common courtyard, in order to carry from the houses to the courtyard it is necessary to turn the courtyard into common property. This is done by putting into the courtyard some food to which all houses contributed. The second kind is “mixing of domains”, explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 8:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.8.4.4"><i>Peah</i>, Chapter 8, Note 56</a>.</i>, participate<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">In order to turn a dead-end street into a common domain in which one may carry on the Sabbath, the residents of the courtyards opening into the dead-end street have to <i>participate</i> in giving food for a common meal. Since the rules for the dead-end street are different from those for a courtyard, the word <i>eruv</i> is not used.</i>, recite grace<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">Even if <i>demay</i> wine has not been put in order and one wants to use it for the cup of blessing. If a transgression of a Biblical commandment were involved, using it for a benediction would constitute blasphemy.</i>, and recite grace in a group<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">The longer form of Grace with an additional invocation.</i>. One may separate it while naked<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to separating heave and tithes from certain produce, <i>demay</i> does not require a benediction and no invocation of the Name, and, hence, may be separated while one is naked (Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 8:2:2-13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.8.2.2-13"><i>Berakhot</i> Halakhah 8:2</a>).</i> or at twilight<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">Friday night after sundown when it is still light but possibly already Sabbath. Certain food cannot be put in order then because this would make it usable now when before it was not at the start of the Sabbath. But <i>demay</i> which in any case is food of the poor, may be put in order during twilight (Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 2:7).</i>. And if one lifted the Second Tithe before the First, it is also acceptable<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">For untithed food, lifting the Second Tithe before the First is forbidden; here it is permitted. Maimonides points out that in any case the heave of the tithe must be a full one percent of the original amount.</i>.
