The oil the weaver puts on his hands<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 2:7: “If it is doubtful whether it is night or not, one does not tithe the certain, one does not immerse vessels (to remove impurity), one does not kindle lights, but one may tithe <i>demay</i>, make an <i>eruv</i>, and cover warm food (to keep it warm for the next morning.)”</i> is obligated for <i>demay</i>, that which the carder puts on wool is free from <i>demay</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> One may separate it while naked because it does not need a benediction.
Around twilight, that is what we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 2:7: “If it is doubtful whether it is night or not, one does not tithe the certain, one does not immerse vessels (to remove impurity), one does not kindle lights, but one may tithe <i>demay</i>, make an <i>eruv</i>, and cover warm food (to keep it warm for the next morning.)”</i>: “If it is doubtful whether it is night or not.”
Rebbi Ḥalaphta ben Shaul stated: One may redeem <i>demay</i> in the bathhouse<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">Where most people are naked, even if he himself is not.</i> since it does not need a benediction. Hence, if the produce is certain it needs a benediction. Rebbi Mana asked before Rebbi Judan, what does he recite? If it was produce, “on the redemption of Second Tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">Praised are You, Eternal, our God, King of the Universe, Who sanctified us by His commandments and commanded us on the redemption of Second Tithe.</i>,” if it was money<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">Money from the redemption of Second tithe which is sacred and, therefore, cannot be exchanged for other coins, or for produce in Jerusalem to consume in purity, without a ceremony of profaning the first coins and substituting new ones as holy.</i>, “on turning Second Tithe profane.”
“If one lifted the Second Tithe before the First, it is also acceptable.” Hence, one should not do it on purpose<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is formulated as a conditional, if it happened, nothing bad has happened. It does not say that one is invited to do it.</i>. Rebbi Abba, son of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ḥiyya bar Abba.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, one may make the Second precede the First in <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">This is accepted by Maimonides, <i>Maäser</i> 9:6, even though for practical decisions one prefers R. Joshua ben Levi over R. Joḥanan, probably because the practical rule which the Halakhah gives, in the paragraph following the next, works only according to R. Joḥanan.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa bar Idi<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">He cannot be identical either with R. Jacob bar Aḥa of the fourth generation, or with R. Jacob bar Idi, companion of R. Joḥanan. He is quoted only here and nothing else is known about him.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: One should not do it, but if he did, what he did is valid.
May one determine the Second in place of the First<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">This is not a question about <i>demay</i> but about produce from which no tithes were taken. It is forbidden to change the order in which the tithes are to be taken; if one takes the Second before the First, one may not recite in the Temple the declaration of tithes, containing the assertion (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:13" href="/Deuteronomy.26.13"><i>Deut.</i> 26:13</a>); “I did not overstep Your commandments.” Here, the question is whether this also applies simply to the designation of the place from which the tithe will be taken in the future. This is important if somebody wants to consume fruits or wine for which he will give tithes in the future from some other batch, under circumstances that prevent the actual separation now, e. g., on the Sabbath. Then he must spell out the location from which tithes will be taken. Does this preliminary determination also have to follow the strict rules of precedence?</i>? His sharecropper brought fruits to Rebbi Yose ben Shaul. He said to him, go and fix a place for the Second. He returned, and he told him, go and fix a place for the First. Should he not worry that maybe the Second was fixed at a place reserved for the First<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">If the tithes had actually been taken, would some of the produce now designated as Second Tithe actually have become First and would then be forbidden to the owner? Note that the sharecropper understood Hebrew, not only Aramaic; he was educated and did not object to what he was told.</i>? This means that it is permitted to determine the Second in place of<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., prior to.</i> the First.
Ulla bar Ismael in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: A person may free his <i>ṭevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">Produce from which tithes were not yet given. In general, <i>ṭevel</i> denotes produce from which nothing yet was given but, as R. Abraham ben David points out in his notes to Maimonides, <i>Maäser</i> 7:3, one must assume that heave already was taken; otherwise, the entire operation would be impossible.</i> with one <i>seah</i> of <i>ṭevel</i>. How does he do it? He brings one <i>seah</i> of <i>ṭevel</i> and declares it to be Second (Tithe), redeems it<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">Since the heave of the tithe was not yet taken, the redeemed Second Tithe is now <i>ṭevel</i> for the First Tithe only and can be used as First Tithe or as heave of the tithe. Since this Second Tithe is different from the produce to be tithed, no transgression was committed in designating the Second Tithe first.</i>, and returns to make it heave of the tithe for any other place. Ulla descended there<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">Babylonia.</i> and said this in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, but they united against him. Rav Sheshet objected, does not a Mishnah contradict: “If there were before him two baskets full of figs<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Demay</i> 7:6: “If he had before him two baskets of <i>ṭevel</i>; if he said, the tithes of one of them are in the other, the tithes of the first one are given. Of the first in the second and of the second in the first, the tithes of the first one are given. The tithes of each of the baskets are in the other one, he named them correctly.” In the second case, the second basket is not in order since the first basket is in order and one may under no circumstances give tithes from produce from which tithes were already given. In the third case, however, the fixing of the places of tithes is simultaneous for both baskets and that is possible since it was permitted in the first case (and we have established that there is no sin in giving names or fixing places simultaneously as long as the actual lifting of the tithes is done in good order.)</i>.” And we have stated about this<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 8:15" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).8.15">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 8:15</a>: “If two baskets of <i>ṭevel</i> were before him, each containing 100 figs, if their heave was taken and the tithes of each, the first gives its tithes. If he takes the tithes of each from the other, the first does not give tithes (as explained in the previous note). He takes two figs, two tithes, and a tenth of a tenth.” If he takes the tithes from each basket, he takes from each basket 19 figs (10 for First Tithe, 9 for Second Tithe, since heave was already taken.) If he takes all tithes from one basket, he takes 20 for First Tithe, 16 for Second Tithe. Hence, he has to add another 2 figs to get to the required 38 figs. Then from the First Tithe, he takes one tenth as heave of the tithe.</i>: “He takes from the second basket two figs and two tenths, and a tenth of a tenth.” Why can he not take two figs, declare them to be Second (Tithe), redeem them, and return to make them heave of the tithe for any other place<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">Following the recipe of R. Joḥanan.</i>? Rebbi Mana said, is not the Second Tithe in the first basket containing First Tithe of the second basket<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">Since everything comes from the second basket, the second basket would have to be fixed first but then it will be unusable to fix any other. Hence, the method of R. Joḥanan is not applicable here.</i>? Rebbi Ḥananiah said, there<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of Ulla.</i> it is <i>ṭevel</i> for the First and Second. But here, Second Tithe that was put in order after First, can you return and make it First<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">Since First was already taken, one cannot take something that is exempt from further obligations in order to make it heave of the tithe anywhere. (The explanations to this paragraph given in the commentaries, from R. Simson of Sens to R. S. Lieberman, are unintelligible.)</i>?
“The oil that the weaver puts on his hands is obligated for <i>demay</i>, that which the carder puts on wool is free from <i>demay</i>.” What is the difference between one case and the other? The first disappears on his body<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">It supports the body and is titheable as food.</i>, the latter disappears on the wool.

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following must be tithed as <i>demay</i> everywhere<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Every place that ever belonged to the Biblical Land of the Twelve Tribes, but only if the produce is from a kind which grows in the Land of Israel. These kinds are export articles and one may expect to find them in all surrounding countries.</i>: Fig cakes, dates, carobs, rice, and cumin. Rice from outside the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Rice from strains not grown in the Land are free from <i>demay</i> even if imported and used in the land occupied by the returnees from Babylonia.</i> is free for everybody who uses it.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ma‘serot</i> 4:8. All these kinds are not subject to tithes since they are not produced in the Land of Israel.</i>: “Garlic from Baalbek, onions from Rikhpah<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This place has not been identified.</i>, split beans from Cilicia, and Egyptian lentils.” Because these kinds<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Garlic, onions, beans, and lentils, but not these sorts.</i> also grow in the Land of Israel, the Sages had to attach marks to them<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Greek σημεῖον “mark, symbol.”</i>. “But filberts, pistachios, and pine nuts, since these kinds do not grow in the Land of Israel, the Sages did not have to attach marks to them.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Ma‘serot</i> 4:10, from a statement of R. Yose. There (in all Tosephta manuscripts) it is implied that these nuts are also produced in the Land of Israel, but Baalbek onions etc. do not grow in the Land. R. Isaac ben Malchisedek Simponti notes that he has Yerushalmi manuscripts of both traditions.</i>” Rebbi Abun said, the Mishnah is the opposite regarding the obligation of these kinds; since these kinds do not grow outside of the Land, the Sages had to enumerate them<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">That they are subject to <i>demay</i> even outside the Land, in contrast to the first Mishnah which enumerated the kinds exempt from <i>demay</i> even if grown in the Land.</i>. But is there not fig cake from Bosra<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This probably is Bosra or Bostra, on the main road that goes South from Damascus, just outside the boundaries of the domain settled by the returnees from Babylonia.</i>? It is pounded. But dates from Alexandria? They are thin. But carob from Kiari<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This place has not been identified.</i>? It is cut. But rice from the Ḥolata<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Rice from the dunes (חולה) of Antiochia is mentioned at the end of this Halakhah (fol. 22d), but there it is implied that it is undistinguishable from rice grown in the Land, in opposition to the text here. (אכתר is Arabic אכת̇ר “more numerous”, there are more kernels to a stalk.) R. Simson of Sens reads: Rice from Ḥolata Aktar? It is reddish. No locality Ḥolata Aktar is known. It is also impossible to identify חולתה with Ḥuleh, since according to all versions of the Tosephta detailing the borders of the land settled by the returnees from Babylonia, Lake Ḥuleh is inside the Land.</i>? It has more numerous kernels and is reddish. But cumin from Cyprus? It is curved.
Rebbi Lazar said, they stated the Mishnah only for him who buys from a Gentile, but for him who buys from a Jew it is <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10a</sup><i class="footnote">This is the text in all Yerushalmi manuscripts but it cannot be correct. Since the Mishnah speaks of <i>demay</i>, if the text is allowed to stand there would be no difference between R. Eleazar and R. Joḥanan. So either R. Eleazar must say that produce of a Gentile is certain and only that of a Jew is <i>demay</i> (following R. Meïr who declares that a Gentile cannot acquire arable land in the Land of Israel to exempt it from heave and tithes) or that the produce of the Jew is certain and that of the Gentile is <i>demay</i>. The second version is impossible since only a Jew will be expected to give heave and tithes but not a Gentile. In light of the following it is also impossible to assume that the Gentile is only the seller, not the producer. So only the first possibility remains. (Since practice follows R. Joḥanan, as indicated in the next paragraph, the problem is not mentioned in Maimonides and we do not know his reading.)</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, there is no difference, whether one buys from a Jew or from a Gentile it is <i>demay</i>. Rebbi Eleazar holds that most of the Land of Israel is in the hands of the Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Any produce is assumed to be untithed except if it is known to come from a Jewish producer.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan holds that most of the Land of Israel is in the hands of Jews. But even if Rebbi Eleazar agrees with Rebbi Joḥanan that most of the Land of Israel is in the hands of Jews, Rebbi Eleazar is anxious about a minority. Like that fortification<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Palisades, Greek χάραξ (cf. <i>Berakhot</i> p. 663). In the Babli it appears as כרך. The garrison in the palisades is Gentile and lives off the <i>annona</i>, a tax to be paid in agricultural products. They will sell their surplus; if all surroundings are Jewish, one buys their surplus as if it were Jewish produce, but not if some of the farmers sending their <i>annona</i> to the garrison are Gentiles. Then a testimony that one day the supply came from a Gentile source makes all their surplus subject to all tithes for him who holds that produce grown by a Gentile in the Land is subject to heave and tithes.</i>, if for one day it got supplies from a forbidden source, that day will become proof for all days. Rebbi Yose asked: If that is so, a prospective proselyte who comes to convert should not be accepted<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">He can be accepted but would be prohibited from marrying a Jewish woman, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:4" href="/Deuteronomy.23.4"><i>Deut</i>. 23:4</a>. Since practice rules this out, according to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Yadayim 4:4" href="/Mishnah_Yadayim.4.4">Mishnah <i>Yadaim</i> 4:4</a>, every male proselyte is permitted to marry a Jewish woman and the alternative reason given for R. Eleazar is unacceptable.</i>; I say he is from Ammon and Moab, and one proselyte would become proof for all proselytes. Hence, Rebbi Eleazar must hold that most of the Land of Israel is in the hands of Gentiles and Rebbi Joḥanan must hold that most of the Land of Israel is in the hands of Jews.
Moreover from the following: Rebbi Zeïra sent to ask Rebbi Alexander from Ẓedoqa<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">An unidentified place in Galilee. Nothing is known about this R. Alexander.</i>, those Nicolaus dates from here, what you estimate about them, mostly from Gentiles or mostly from Jews? He said to him, we are not able to estimate about them<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">If there were a need, they certainly would have decided. Hence, practice follows R. Joḥanan.</i>.
One statement supports this and a second statement supports Rebbi Eleazar. “The trader’s produce everywhere is <i>demay</i>. When is that? If most of his supply is from Jews. But if he buys from Gentiles, it is certain<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">This supports our reconstruction of R. Eleazar’s text above, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.1.3">Note 11</a>.</i>.” A statement supports Rebbi Joḥanan: “Rebbi Neḥemiah stated: “Whether from a Gentile, a Jewish Samaritan<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">All commentators change the text here, to treat ישראל either as a separate entry or to move it as qualifier to עם הארץ. However, there is no manuscript evidence for such a change. Rather Rebbi Neḥemiah seems to take sides in the quarrel on whether to consider Samaritans as Jews and sides with Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:1:2-19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.1.2-19"><i>Ketubot</i> 3:1</a>, fol. 27a) against Rebbi; cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 3:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.3.4.3">Chapter 3, Note 98</a>. A Samaritan observes all Biblical laws. However, since his brand of Judaism was adopted by the Sadducees, we know that he takes the verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:14" href="/Leviticus.19.14"><i>Lev</i>. 19:14</a>): “Do not put an obstacle before a blind man” literally, not as a general injunction not to trap the unwary; he will not take heave and tithes from his harvest for sale. His produce is certainly subject to all the laws if we accept Samaritans as Jews.</i>, or an <i>am haareẓ</i> seller, if at times he buys from the Gentile, at times from the Jew, it is <i>demay</i>.”
Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada asked before Rebbi Mana: From whom did this man buy<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The trader who has no single source of supply.</i>? If from a Jew it is <i>demay</i>, from a Gentile certain. Explain that if the trader was a Gentile and both Jew and Gentile deliver to him<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The farmers bring him their produce and he does not care who it is.</i>, it is <i>demay</i>. Rav Hoshaia objected, does not the Mishnah support Rebbi Joḥanan? As it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 17:5" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.17.5"><i>Kelim</i> 17:5</a>. Badan (<i>Wadi Badyah</i>) and Geba were places in Samaria (Tosephta <i>Kelim Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 6:10). While these varieties were mainly grown in Samaria, some were grown by Jews. But since the Jews were a minority among the Samaritans, the produce must be treated as Samaritan, subject to tithes but certainly not tithed.</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah said, they only mentioned pomegranates from Badan and grain from Geba because they must be tithed as certain everywhere.” What are we talking about? If he bought from a Jew, can it be pronounced certain? But we must deal with a Gentile. Hence, all other things are tithed as <i>demay</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said, we deal with certain produce from a Jew. Explain it that there was a powerful overseer of markets<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἀγορανόμος, the market supervisor. Usually, his function was only to act as police for correct weights and measures and fair trading practices.</i> who exerted pressure to sell at low prices, and they<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The local rabbis, to save the livelihoods of the Jewish merchants. If the buyer buys below regular cost, it is common prudence to assume that the produce is untithed.</i> permitted him to sell <i>ṭevel</i> and let the buyer beware for himself.
Who is a trader<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of the <i>baraita</i> supporting R. Eleazar.</i>? Everyone who brought, repeated, and came a third time. Rebbi Jonah asked, if he brought three loads together, is he not a trader<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">“Three loads” means that he either brings three carriers or three donkeys with him, each with a full load. A rhetorical question to show that this would be an unreasonable restriction.</i>; one after the other, is he a trader? Rebbi Jonah asked, does he become a trader retroactively or only for the future? What is the difference? If someone came and brought his things in order, if you say only for the future, can he tithe from one on the other<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody bought before he became a professional trader, the produce is certain; after that, it is <i>demay</i>. Once he is a trader, one cannot tithe one <i>demay</i> for another since the one chosen may have been in order and the tithing is invalid. If the title of trader is not retroactive, he may give from the certain also for the <i>demay</i>. The question is not answered.</i>! Rebbi Mana asked: Can he, his son, and his worker combine for three loads<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Since they are all extensions of the trader himself, would R. Jonah agree that these three do not count separately.</i>? Rebbi Jonah asked, a ship that comes from Rome and carries many kinds, do you count this only as one<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">If the ship’s owner is the seller, does he have professional status already the first time around or not?</i>?
“For fruits they did not consider either smell, or looks, or taste, or value, but only plurality<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 4:11" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).4.11">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 4:11</a>. If fruits are mixed the obligations of tithes follow the status of the group which forms a plurality.</i>.” Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If it was wine, for example new wine and old wine, they judged according to taste.
Rebbi Mana said, I went to Caesarea<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">As shown in tractate <i>Berakhot</i>, Caesarea in the Yerushalmi is usually Caesarea Philippi near the source of the Jordan river. Caesarea here is Caesarea maritima, whose leading authority was R. Isaac ben Eleazar. The Land in which all duties on produce are due is the land occupied by the returnees from Babylonia, Judea and Galilee, and, if we accept Samaritans as Jews, their land, Samaria. In the plain East of Samaria, the region between the <i>via maris</i> connecting Damascus and Egypt and the sea was mostly malaria swamps, except for the healthy strip between the first dunes and the sea. Herod built an elevated highway from the <i>via maris</i> to the sea, where he built Caesarea maritima for a Gentile population, separate from the Jewish centers. Hence, the region of Caesarea and its sand marshes never fell under the laws attached to the Land.</i> and I saw them using fig cake as free. I asked Rebbi Isaac bar Eleazar; he said to me that Zeugos<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">An otherwise unknown sage. That the authority is such a minor figure again shows that we are not dealing with Caesarea Philippi. The fig cakes at Caesarea probably had a peculiar form so that they were immediately recognized.</i> used to permit it. Rebbi Isaac bar Eleazar in the name of Zeugos of Caesarea: All that sees the water<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">In the Rome manuscript: ימה, “the sea.” This might be the better reading.</i> is free. Some want to say, up to the salt tower, some want to say, up to the Telamon cave<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Probably the salt pans at the Northern end of the sands. The place of the cave is unknown; the only caves in the neighborhood are (burial) caves in the petrified first row of dunes North of the aqueduct from the Carmel range. Τέλαμων is a Greek mythological figure; also “base of στήλη”, Τελαμῶνες the name of colossal figures supporting pillars (E. G.).</i>. Rebbi Abba Mari said, the words of the rabbis imply that all kinds are forbidden in Caesarea. “Wheat, bread, oil, dates, rice, and cumin<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">First half of a <i>baraita</i> whose end is quoted later in the paragraph. The implication is that only these products are <i>demay</i> because they are imported; all others are free, since it does not simply say: everything in Caesarea is <i>demay</i>. In the synagogue mosaic from Reḥov, the text reads:<br>הפירות הללו מתעסרין דמיי בקסרין החטין והפת<br>חלה לעולם והיין והשמן והתמרין והאורז והכמן<br>הרי אלו מותרין בשביעית בקסרין ובישאר שני <br>שבוע הן מתקנין דמיי ויש אוסרין בולבסין<br>הלבנין מהר המלך.<br>“The following produce is tithed as <i>demay</i> in Cesarea: Wheat (bread always needs <i>ḥallah</i>), wine, oil, dates, rice, and cumin; these are permitted in the Sabbatical year, in the other years they must be fixed as <i>demay</i>. Some authorities forbid white bulbs (onions) from King’s Mountain.”</i>.” Fig cakes are not included? It is in the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Which requires <i>demay</i> “everywhere.” The sentence is the answer to the preceding question.</i>. But we have also mentioned rice and cumin; these are in the Mishnah and we have spelled them out! Those are the ones which are left out. “These are permitted in the Sabbatical year, in all other years of the Sabbatical cycle they are <i>demay</i>.” These are permitted in the Sabbatical year; why are they not Sabbatical fruits in the Sabbatical year? The Jews treat them as Sabbatical, the Gentiles are free, Jews and Gentiles outnumber Samaritans<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Since Samaritans will not hesitate to supply others for money with Sabbatical fruits that they themselves would not use; they are a minority and we may assume that all produce offered for sale at Cesarea is Gentile. Similarly, Jewish produce in the other years is <i>demay</i>; Gentile produce is <i>demay</i> because maybe it is locally grown and exempt; this outnumbers the produce that may be certain if produced by Samaritans.</i>. In all other years of the Sabbatical cycle they are <i>demay</i>, the Judean Jews put them in order, Gentiles are free, Jews and Gentiles outnumber Samaritans. How far? The inn of the pillars<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Its position is unknown. In the mosaic of Reḥov, the text reads: ועד איכן סביב לקיסרין עד צוורנה ופנדקה דטיבתא ועמודה ודור וכפר סבה ואם יש מקום שקנו אותו ישראל חוששין לו רבותינו שלום. “What is the extent of the vicinity of Cesarea? As far as Ṣavranah, the inn at Ṭaybeta, the pillar, Dor, Kefar Sabah, and any land which a Jew ever bought, our teachers worry about it; Peace (upon you).” R. Saul Lieberman (<i>Tarbiẓ</i> 45, 54–63) concludes from the last sentence that the principle of applying the Laws of the Land to the territory held by the returnees from Babylonia meant that any real estate ever held by a Jew within the territory of Biblical Israel after the return is forever subject to the Laws of the Land.</i>, the inn at Tayibeh<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">On the <i>via maris</i> North of Kefar Saba (which probably was near modern Juljulia), which in its turn was North of Antipatris, the Northern border fortress of Judea.</i>, as far as Kefar Saba. Their juices are certain in Caesarea.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina: bulbs (onions) sold in Cesarea are forbidden because most of them come from King’s Mountain<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Probably this is the hill region between Judea and Samaria proper.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada said, the white ones, but the rabbis of Cesarea say, the red ones.
Rebbi Zeïra, Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Rebbi permitted Beth Shean<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">He permitted produce from the entire valley of Beth Shean in the Sabbatical year for commercial use because it seems not to have been settled by the returnees from Babylonia. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 6b" href="/Chullin.6b">Babli (<i>Ḥulin</i> 6b</a>), the reading is that R. Meïr ate a leaf of vegetable without tithing for the same kind of argument.</i> on the testimony of Joshua ben Zeruz, the son of Rebbi Meïr’s father-in-law, who said, I saw Rebbi Meïr buy vegetables from a garden in the Sabbatical year. He permitted all of it!<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the testimony was only from one spot.</i> Rebbi Zeïra said, this implies that nobody should act in public; I say that this garden was a particular case for him<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Maybe that particular plot of land never had a Jewish owner since Bet Shean was a Philistine city.</i>; should he have permitted it completely<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">As the ruins of ancient synagogues with mosaic floors show, in the time of R. Zeïra the valley of Beth Shean was settled by Jews and should not have been permitted, but his authority was not sufficient to overturn Rebbi’s ruling.</i>? Rebbi permitted Beth Shean<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The Jews of Beth Shean (Scythopolis) were murdered in a progrom of 66 C. E. and we have no record of the re-establishment of a community in that city.</i>, permitted Caesarea<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Caesarea maritima.</i>, permitted Bet Guvrin<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Near Lakhish, Northwest of Hebron.</i>, permitted Kefar Ẓemaḥ<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Kafr Samekh just South of the Sea of Genezareth; it is counted in the neighborhood of Susita.</i>. Rebbi permitted to buy vegetables after the end of the Sabbatical year<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ševiїt</i> 6:4, Tosephta <i>Ševiït</i> 4:17. The reason is detailed in <i>Peah</i> 7:4, note 81.</i> and all were saying bad things about him<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: His family united against him.</i>. He said to them, come and let us discuss the matter. It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 18:4" href="/II_Kings.18.4"><i>2Kings</i> 18:4</a>): “He<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Hezekiah destroyed the brass snake Moses had made (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 21:9" href="/Numbers.21.9"><i>Num</i>. 21:9</a>), because the people worshipped it as a divinity. The argument given here is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 6a" href="/Chullin.6a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 6a</a>.</i> smashed the brass snake.” Was there no just person between Moses and Hezekiah to remove it? But the Holy One, praise to Him, reserved that crown to him so he could crown himself with it. We also had this crown reserved by the Holy One, praise to Him, to crown ourselves with it.
Rebbi Joshua ben Levi commanded his lads: Do not buy me vegetables except from Sisera’s garden<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Sisera’s headquarters were somewhere in the valley of Bet Shean. R. Joshua ben Levi wanted vegetables only from a place that was neither distributed by Joshua nor settled by the returnees from Babylonia; such a place certainly is free from all obligations on the Land.</i>. He, may he be remembered for the good<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">A common appellation of Elijah. Since he did not die, one cannot mention for him the blessing of the dead, “may his remembrance be a blessing.”</i> stood nearby and said to him: Go tell your teacher that this is not Sisera’s garden; it did belong to a Jew but he killed him and took it away from him. If you want to be restrictive for yourself, permit it to your friend<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Elijah rebuked R. Joshua ben Levi on two points: The names attached to plots are no guarantee for their past, and restrictive action should be taken in private, not declared publicly. The story shows clearly that R. Joshua ben Levi was living in Bet Shean valley, South of Galilee.</i>.
Rebbi Yose of Kefar Dan in the name of Rebbi from Meadiah<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Nothing is known about these two authorities nor about their localities. In the Rome manuscript, the last place name is spelled מעזיה, showing that the vowel under ע is long.</i>, the kinds forbidden in Beth Shean<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The kinds of vegetables forbidden as objects of trade in the Sabbatical year because they are imported from regions under the Sabbatical law. The text is now confirmed by the mosaic inscription from the synagogue of Reḥov in the Beth Shean valley:הפירות הללו אסורין בבית שאן ובשאר שני שבוע מתאסרין (!) דמי הקישואין והאבטיחין והממלפפונות והמינתה הנאגדת בפני עצמה ופול המצרי הנאגד בשיפה והקפלוטות מן העצרת עד החנוכה והזירעונין והקצע והשמשמין והחרדל והאורז והכמון והתורמסין היבשין והאפונין הגמלונין הנמכרין במידה והשום ובצלין בני מדינה הנמכרין במידה והבולבסין והתמרין אפסיות והיין והשמן בשביעית שביעית שני שבוע דמי והפת חלה לעולם. “The following are forbidden in Bet Shean and in the other years of the Sabbatical cycle they are tithed as <i>demay</i>: Green melon, water melon and sweet melon, mint {Greek μίνθα, μίνθη, Latin menta,<i>mentha</i>} bundled separately, Egyptian beans bundled with bast, leeks from Shavuot to Hanukkah, seeds of nigella, sesame, mustard, rice, cumin, dry lupines, large peas sold by measure {cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.3.2.1">Mishnah <i>Kilaim</i> 3:2</a>}, garlic, local onions, βολβός {perhaps truffles}, Ephesian dates; wine and oil are Sabbatical produce in the Sabbatical year, in the other years <i>demay</i>. Bread always needs <i>ḥallah</i>.”</i>: Nigella, sesame, mustard, garlic, bulbs, black peas, commercial onions<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Probably imported from Galilee or Samaria. The “local ones” must mean the ones of the same type as the local produce but since they are sold wholesale they are not considered local.</i>, local ones that are sold by weight, Egyptian beans bound with bast, mint bundled separately, carrot<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">In the Beth Shean inscription, אסטפליני, Greek σταφυλῖνος “carrot, <i>Daucus carota</i>”.</i> always, leeks<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Allium porrum L</i>., called <i>allium capitatum</i>, corresponding to Greek κεφαλωτός [Pliny XX. 6. 22]; cf. <i>Berakhot</i> p. 484.</i> from Pentecost to Ḥanukkah. Rebbi Zeïra said, from Pentecost to Ḥanukkah the forbidden ones outnumber the permitted ones, from Ḥanukkah to Pentecost the permitted ones outnumber the forbidden ones. In the matter of aftergrowth<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Produce that started growing by itself in the Sabbatical year and which is to be treated as Sabbatical in the following year (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:5" href="/Leviticus.25.5"><i>Lev</i>. 25:5</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:11" href="/Leviticus.25.11">11</a>).</i>, from New Year’s Day to Ḥanukkah prohibition of aftergrowth, from Ḥanukkah to Pentecost permission of aftergrowth, from Pentecost to New Year’s Day questionable. Zucchini, squash, water melon, apple melon<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>melopepo</i>, Greek μηλοπέπων. The modern meaning of the word, “cucumber”, is found in Maimonides and <i>Caftor waPeraḥ</i> (ed. Luncz, Jerusalem 1899 p. 485), see <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.2.1">Mishnah <i>Kilaim</i> 1:2</a>. The identification of all these fruits from the <i>cucurbitaceae</i> family is tentative; see the detailed discussion in the commentary to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.2.1">Mishnah <i>Kilaim</i> 1:2</a>.</i>, wine, oil, Ephesian dates, somebody also says lupines. Bread always requires <i>hallah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ḥallah</i> is an obligation of the Land (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.2.1.1">Mishnah <i>Ḥallah</i> 2:1</a>), but by rabbinical ordinance it has to be taken everywhere (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 4:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.4.4.1">Mishnah <i>Ḥallah</i> 4:8</a>). It seems from the repeated insistence on the obligation to give <i>ḥallah</i> in the Yerushalmi and the mosaic from Reḥov that this rabbinic ordinance was not generally observed by the population of districts outside the holdings of the returnees from Babylonia.</i>. These are Sabbatical in a Sabbatical year. In the other years of the cycle? Rebbi Jonah said, <i>demay</i>; Rebbi Yose said, certain. They have no disagreement over tithes; Rebbi Jonah said <i>demay</i> for him who buys from a person making house calls<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Explanation of A. Kohut in <i>Aruch Completum</i>, cf. Arabic בלנקע “paved road.” The produce bought on the road is of uncertain origin.</i>, Rebbi Yose said certain for him who bought from the garden<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Local produce is from a Gentile grower, certainly exempt.</i>.
How far<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">How far does the exemption of the Beth Shean valley extend? The text is garbled, the places have not been identified. The list is better preserved in the mosaic from Reḥov:אילוּ המקומות המותרין סביבות בית שאן מן הדרום שהיא פילי דקמפון עד חלקה חיורתה מן המערב שהיא פילי דזיירא עד סוף הרצפה מן הצפון שהיא פילי דסכותא עד כפר קרנוס וכפר קרנוס כבית שאן ומן המזרח שהיא פילי דזבלייה עד נפשיה דפנוקטייה וּפילי דכפר זימרין ופילי דאגמה לפנים מן השער מותר ולחוץ אסור. “These are the places permitted in the neighborhood of Beth Shean: In the South, from the Stadium (<i>campus</i>) gate (πύλη) to the white smooth place (the shale rocks at the foot of the Gilboa mountains). In the West, from the Ziara gate to the end of the paved road. In the North, from the Sakota gate to Kefar Qarnos (quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 17" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.17"><i>Lev</i>.<i>rabba</i> 17</a> as grazing place of Job’s cattle; ms. Rome also reads קרנוס); Kefar Qarnos has the status of Beth Shean. In the East, from the dung gate to the mausoleum of Panokatia (?), the gates of Kefar Zimrin and the pond gate; inside the gate it is permitted, outside it is forbidden.”</i>? (The cross roads of <i>Rẓyfta</i>, the mausoleum of <i>Pgvṭiah</i> and Kefar Karnaim, Kefar Karnaim being like Beth Shean.)
Rebbi Jonah in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Zachariah<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">An otherwise unknown author.</i>: The kinds forbidden in Paneas<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Banias, at the Jordan source, a Greek town just upstream from Caesarea Philippi. The text of Reḥov reads: הפירות הללו אסורין בפניס בשביעית ובישאר שני שבוע הן מתעסרין דמיי משלם האורז והאגוזין והשמשמין ופול המצרי יש אומרין אוף אחוניות הבכירות הרי אלו בשביעית שביעית ובשאר שני שבוע הן מתעסרין ודיי ואפילו מן תרנגולה עלייה ולחוץ. “The following produce is forbidden at Paneas in the Sabbatical year; in the other years their are tithed as complete <i>demay</i>: Rice, walnuts, sesame, Egyptian beans; some also say early ripening <i>atriplex</i>. These have sabbatical status in the Sabbatical year, in the remaining years they must be tithed as certain, even outside the Upper Rooster.” The identity of “complete <i>demay</i>” and “certain” is discussed by R. S. Lieberman in <i>Tarbiẓ</i> 45 (5776), 54–63. He quotes several passages in which “certain” means “to be treated as certain because of doubt.”</i>: Walnuts, rice, sesame, and Egyption beans. Gamliel the twin says, early ripening <i>atriplex</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.2.4"><i>Berakhot</i> 6:2, Note 125</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, that means, higher than the Rooster of Caesarea<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta on the borders of the Holy Land (<i>Ševiït</i> 6:1, fol. 36c), one of the border marks is “the Upper Rooster over Cesarea Philippi”; from there to the Northeast is the Trachonitis and Syria, to the Southwest is the Holy Land.</i>. But below the Rooster of Caesarea it has the status of the Land of Israel. Rebbi Jonah asked: If his field was sown with vegetables and he came and found it full of rice, is the vegetable permitted and the rice forbidden? If the rice is high and the vegetable low, is the vegetable permitted and the rice forbidden? In fact, most of these fields produce these species! Even if you say that most of these fields produce these species, most of these kinds only come from forbidden areas<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Since the imports outnumber local produce, we have to follow the majority in all cases, as established earlier.</i>.
It was stated: The rice in the sands of Antiochia<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Antakia on the Orontes. It seems that the rice from there was the same kind as that from the Holy Land; since the local production was enough and nothing or very little was imported, the decree of the Mishnah does not apply to Antiochia.</i> is permitted in its place. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose permitted it as far as <i>Kdvn</i><sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simson of Sens reads בורן. The place is unidentified.</i>. Rebbi Jonah asked: This distance<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Measured from the dunes.</i> in every direction?
“The following are the forbidden towns in the district of Tyre<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the Mishnah states that Akhzib is the Northern border of the Land, there are isolated villages that always had a Jewish population and, therefore, are obliged to follow the rules of the Sabbatical. The text of the Reḥov mosaic reads: העיירות האסורות בתחום צור שצת ובצת ופי מצובה וחנותה עלייתה וחנותה ארעייתה וביברה וראש מייה ואמון ומזה היא קסטלה וכל מה שקנו ישראל נאסר.. “The towns forbidden in the district of Tyre: <i>ṡẓt</i>, Al-Baṣa, the source at Ma‘aṣuba, upper and lower Ḥanuta, the house of Bara (or Badia), the water spring, Hamun, and Mazy, that is the water tower [<i>castellum</i>, cf. Y. Sussman, <i>Tarbiẓ</i>45 (5736), 213–257]; and all that was acquired by Jews becomes forbidden.”</i>: <i>ṡẓt</i><sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Unidentified; in the Rome ms. שיצת, in the Tosephta mss. אשנץ, שנץ.</i>, Al-Baṣa<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Just South of Rosh Haniqra.</i>, the source at Ma‘aṣuba<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Khirbet Ma‘aṣuba, SE of Al-Baṣa.</i>, upper and lower Ḥanuta<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Khirbet Ḥanuta, NE from Khirbet Ma‘aṣuba.</i>, Bet Badia<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Unidentified; in the Erfurt ms. of the Tosephta: בית כריא.</i>, the water spring, Hamun<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Khirbet Mazy is near Rosh Haniqra; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 19:28" href="/Joshua.19.28">Ḥamun (<i>Jos</i>. 19:28</a>) is North of it at the source Ḥamul, so maybe ראש מיא and עמון are one and the same. Rebbi Mana said, what you enumerate was earlier. But now there [are other towns that were held by Israel and they are forbidden. “<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The text in Tosephta <i>Ševiït</i> 4:10 is: “Towns obligated for tithes in the district of Susita.” R. Missing in the list of <i>Caphtor Waperaḥ.</i></i>, the heights of Barin<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">In the Rome ms. בריך, in the Vienna ms. of the Tosephta: ורומברך. “The following are the towns that were permitted in the district of Naveh<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Appears in the Yerushalmi also as Nineveh (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:1:2-18" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.1.2-18"><i>Taäniot</i> 1:1</a>), in the center of the Bašan plateau. The text is also in Tosephta <i>Ševiït</i> 4:8. In the Tosephta: “Towns that were permitted in the district of Nave and became forbidden.” As Arab village, Nowa. The text from Reḥov reads: העיירות שהן ספק בתחום נווה ציר וצייר וגשמיי וזיזון ורנב .וחרבתה ואיגרי חותם וכרכרה דבר הרג. “The towns that are questionable (whether Jewish or Gentile) in the district of Naveh: Ṣaria, Ṭeriya, Jasim, Zizun, Regeb (?), the ruin and wall of <i>ḥṭm</i>, the palisades of <i>Bar hrg</i>.”</i>: Ṣaria<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta mss.: ציר וצייר, Rome ms. צורן צייר. The identifications here are S. Klein’s. The place is E. of Nave, N. of Ṭeriya.</i>, Ṭeriya, Jasim<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of the Rome Yerushalmi and Vienna Tosephta mss. is גשמי וזיזיון, the Erfurt ms. has גושמי וזיזין. Jasim is N. of Naveh, Zizun to the SE.</i>, Zizun, the stone heaps of <i>ḥṭm</i><sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: ויגרי טב. For יגר, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 31:47" href="/Genesis.31.47"><i>Gen</i>. 31.47</a>. Identified by S. Klein as ‘Otman, E. of Zizun.</i>, Aldanba the ruin<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Rome ms. ונדב חרבותה, Tosephta mss. ודנב חורבתא. Aldanba is E. of Naveh (S. Klein).</i>, the palisades of <i>Bar hzrg</i><sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Rome ms.: דבר הורג. Tosephta mss.: דבית הרב. The place of this fortification has not been identified. S. Klein identifies the name <i>ḥzrg</i> as Nabatean (maybe from زرج “shoot of vine”).</i>.”
A load of raisins<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Either a full donkey load or a full camel load. Since it says “ascended,” it must have come up the Jordan valley from the Beth Shean valley, or from Transjordan. It seems from the following that the question was whether in a Sabbatical year one was permitted to trade in these raisins.</i> came up to Tiberias. Gamliel the twin asked Rebbi Abba bar Kahana<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Rome ms: Rebbi Abba bar Zavda. This reading is more likely since the latter was the Halakhic authority.</i>. He said to him: All of the Land of Israel does not produce a load of raisins. Does all of the Land of Israel not produce a load of raisins? But so he said to him: No single place in the land of Israel produces a load of raisins.
A person brought a back-pack<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Rome ms: מפשלתה; the <i>hif‘il</i> of פשל means: to let hang down (in Arabic: to fail, be unsuccessful.)</i> of leeks to Rebbi Isaac bar Tevele. He asked Rebbi Joḥanan. He said to him, go and ask Ḥananiah ben Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna in the academy of R. Joḥanan, whose task was to memorize all known tannaïtic statements. R. Isaac bar Tevele is otherwise unknown.</i> who knows statements about this. He went and asked. He said, they did not show me<sup class="footnote-marker">109a</sup><i class="footnote">The expression יחמי לי is discussed in detail by Y. Sussman in Mehqerei Talmud, vol. 3, Jerusalem 2005, pp. 219-224. (Addenda and Corrigenda by Guggenheimer)</i> a statement, but he told me an Amoraic tradition, since Rebbi Yasa said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Rebbi and Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon disagree: one says after its place of production, the other says, after its current stand. Rebbi Abbahu explained: Rebbi said, after its place of production and Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon, after its current stand. Rebbi Abbahu said that Rebbi said, after its place of production, and they taught him following Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon, after its current stand<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Produce that may be from the Land of Israel is under the laws of the Sabbatical everywhere in the Land if carried by a human.</i>. This means for back packs, but for animal loads everybody agrees after its place of production. If its place of production and of current placement are free but he is going to transport it through forbidden territory, that forbidden territory is as if he did not traverse it. Rebbi Abba bar Cohen asked before Rebbi Yose: Did not Rebbi Ḥiyya say in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, between Rebbi and his colleague, practice follows Rebbi. But Rebbi Jonah said, even Rebbi with Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Eleazar ben R. Simeon was Rebbi’s teacher; he could not be counted as a “colleague.” It is said that after the death of R. Eleazar, Rebbi, who at that time was a widower, asked his widow to marry him. She answered: “Should a vessel that was used in the Sanctuary ever be used for profane purposes?”</i>? He said to him, what do you want from Rebbi Joḥanan! Rebbi Joḥanan follows his own opinion, for Rebbi Joḥanan said, they were very lenient regarding the Sabbatical, which is of their words<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the observation of the Sabbatical year is among the duties that the returnees from Babylonia took upon themselves voluntarily (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 10:32" href="/Nehemiah.10.32"><i>Neh.</i>10:32</a>), it cannot be considered a Biblical commandment since it is intrinsically connected with the Jubilee year which presupposes that every family hold the grounds alotted to it in Joshua’s distribution. Since there can be no Jubilee, the Sabbatical stands only on popular acceptance.</i>. Some want to say<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">These object to R. Joḥanan and say that even if the Sabbatical cannot be considered a part of the Jubilee institution, at least it is to be kept in fulfillment of the vow registered by Nehemiah and, as such, it is Biblical.</i>, the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan is added to that of Rebbi and they form a majority against Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who takes it upon himself to be trustworthy<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">To guarantee that all his produce is tithed and free from the rules of <i>demay</i>.</i> gives tithes for what he eats, what he sells, and what he buys, and he is not a guest of an <i>am haäreẓ.</i> Rebbi Jehudah said, he who is a guest of an <i>am haäreẓ</i> may be trustworthy. They said to him, for himself he is not trustworthy<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is assumed that the <i>am haäreẓ</i> will serve him <i>demay</i> from which the heave of tithes was not taken, and by eating it he potentially commits a deadly sin, how can he be trusted by his customers that tithes were taken according to all rules?</i>, how can he be trusted for others?
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “What he buys,” to eat it. But did we not state: “What he eats?” Hence, “what he buys” in order to sell it. But did we not state: “What he sells?” Hence, “what he buys” in order to sell it and “what he sells” from his own gathering.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 2:1</a>. As usual, the opinion of the anonymous author of the Mishnah is attributed to R. Meïr. The position taken in the Mishnah and here by R. Jehudah in the Tosephta is the collective opinion of the Sages. The sentence here is R. Jehudah’s (or the Sages’) reply that one never objected to any private person’s being the guest of an <i>am haäreẓ.</i> (What the observant person has to do in this case is described at the end of this Halakhah. The Yerushalmi accepts this relaxed standard for private persons but not for traders.)</i>: Rebbi Jehudah said, private persons never refrained from being guests of private persons of their acquaintance. Nevertheless, they used to put in order all their produce in their own houses.
Rebbi Jonah said, <i>ḥaverim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the next Mishnah, a <i>ḥaver</i> is a person who takes it upon himself to observe the rules of ritual purity in his private life, away from the Sanctuary, and to avoid becoming impure if at all possible. With the disappearance of the last ways to cleanse oneself from the impurity induced by the dead (see the Notes to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.1">Mishnah <i>Berakhot</i> 1:1</a>), fellowship was slowly transformed into the fraternity of those who followed ritual law scrupulously. Since Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose are late Amoraïm, we have to assume that for them, fellowship and trustworthiness are practically identical; see the story about R. Simeon ben Laqish in the next Halakhah, Note 154.</i> are not suspected either to eat or to serve to others<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Jonah, a <i>ḥaver</i> who is seen eating at an <i>am haäreẓ</i>’s home or inviting an <i>am haäreẓ</i> to eat with him loses his standing. There is no imputation that the home of the <i>am haäreẓ</i> would not be kosher, but his food may be impure and eating it might break the vow of fellowship. That would not apply to fruits eaten dry, for example. But the food presented by the <i>am haäreẓ</i> is <i>demay</i> by definition and it will be made clear that the vow of fellowship is a step above that of being trustworthy for tithes; hence, eating <i>demay</i> breaks trustworthiness and, <i>a fortiori</i>, fellowship. If one gives of one’s food to an <i>am haäreẓ</i>, it becomes impure by contact with the impure person which is a sure break of the vow of fellowship.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, <i>ḥaverim</i> are suspected to eat but not to serve to others<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose holds that a <i>ḥaver</i> who is seen eating from a source considered as <i>demay</i> does not lose his standing since he might have put his food in order mentally as explained at the end of the Halakhah. In the next Halakhah, however, the statement of R. Yose is qualified to apply to trustworthy persons only, not to recognized <i>ḥaverim</i>.</i>. The Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Yose: “They said to him, for himself he is not trustworthy, how can he be trusted for others?” The words of the rabbis support Rebbi Yose, for Rebbi Ḥanina, Rebbi Yasa, said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Rebbi Jehudah spoke only of the end, but at the start even Rebbi Jehudah will agree.<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody takes the vow (publicly, as explained later) of trustworthiness, he must accept the rules spelled out in the Mishnah as the majority opinion. If later he is seen eating from the <i>am haäreẓ</i>’s, there is disagreement between R. Jehudah and the Sages.</i> If you say that <i>ḥaverim</i> are not suspected either to eat or to serve to others, what is the difference between start and end? As we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">A similar statement is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 2:3" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).2.3">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 2:3</a>, reproduced <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 30b" href="/Bekhorot.30b">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i> 30b</a>, but speaking of fellowship instead of trustworthiness. Again, the anonymous opinion here is R. Meïr’s in the Tosephta, and R. Jehudah’s here is that of the anonymous majority there. (Maimonides <i>Miškav Umošav</i> 10:9 follows the Babli and, hence, the opinion expressed here by R. Jehudah.)</i>: “He who takes it on himself to be trustworthy except for one thing cannot be accepted; he who is suspect in one thing is suspect in everything. Rebbi Jehudah said, he is only suspect in that thing alone.”
It was stated: “He who is trustworthy for purity is trustworthy for tithes.<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">This implies that one is not accepted for fellowship until he be trustworthy for tithes; see the next Halakhah.</i>” Rebbi Yannai ben Rebbi Ismaël stated it and explained his reason: That means, if one is a guest of his, but for the public he is not trustworthy until he accepts it publicly<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Usually, “publicly” means before at least ten adult males. Similarly, the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 2:14" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).2.14">Tosephta (<i>Demay</i> 2:14</a>) speaks of “him who accepts before the fellowship.” However, the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 20b" href="/Bekhorot.20b">Babli (<i>Bekhorot</i> 20b</a>) requires “before three people” (who form a court of law.)</i>. Rebbi Ammi in the name of Rebbi Yannai: Even I am not trustworthy until we accept it in public<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 2:13" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).2.13">Tosephta (<i>Demay</i> 2:13</a>) and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 30b" href="/Bekhorot.30b">Babli (<i>Bekhorot</i> 30b</a>) exempt a member of a Rabbinic court since he would not have been accepted into the court had he not given a vow of fellowship.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra, Rebbi Yasa, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Even a <i>ḥaver</i> who sent to another <i>ḥaver</i>, the recipient must tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">In order to avoid situations as that described in Halakhah 1:3 between R. Zeïra and R. Jeremiah, where each trusted the other to put the food in order.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra asked before Rebbi Yasa, for example, I to Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac, and Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac to me? He answered him, what do you want from Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac, because all he eats, he eats from the market<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">He was Babylonian in origin and, in contrast to R. Zeïra, never acquired land in Galilee. Hence, all he ate was bought from the market as <i>demay</i> and immediately put in order.</i>.
“If<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 3:9" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).3.9">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 3:9</a>, in slightly different language. The curse on the man whose wife is trustworthy while he is not is missing there.</i> he is trustworthy but his wife is not, one buys from him<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">On the market.</i> but one cannot be his guest. But they said, he is like one who lives with a snake in the same basket<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Since she will serve him <i>demay</i> that was not brought in order if he forgot to ask.</i>. If his wife is trustworthy but he is not, one can be his guest but not buy from him. But they said, a curse should fall on him whose wife is trustworthy while he is not.”
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Similar text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 3:6-7" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).3.6-7">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 3:6–7</a>.</i>: “A <i>ḥaver</i><sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. S. Lieberman, the “<i>ḥaver</i>” here is the trustworthy person, not necessarily a full member of the fellowship, but the language both here and in the Tosephta does not support this explanation. Maimonides, in Chapter 10 of <i>Maäser</i>, identifies trustworthy person and <i>ḥaver</i>, but he writes for practical use and in his time the difference between the two had disappeared since nobody was able to eat in purity. However, as will be explained in the next Halakhah, one facet of fellowship, the washing of one’s hands before a meal, is still valid and this has to be added to the conditions of trustworthiness in any case.</i> should not serve as waiter at the wedding feast of an <i>am haäreẓ</i> or the dinner of an <i>am haäreẓ</i> except when everything was in order and tithed under his control, even<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Including the wine that might be in the ladle separate from the amphora from which the heave of the tithe is taken.</i> the wine ladle. But if<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Since a <i>ḥaver</i> may not serve at a dinner of untithed food, his presence is a guarantee of the kosher status of the entire meal.</i> a <i>ḥaver</i> acted as waiter at the wedding feast of an <i>am haäreẓ</i> or the dinner of an <i>am haäreẓ</i> this is <i>prima facie</i> evidence for tithes. If one saw him<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>ḥaver</i> was not a waiter but a guest; his participation is not proof of anything since before the meal he could have made a mental stipulation to give the necessary tithes for what he ate from his own provisions at home when he returned; this is an acceptable way of tithing <i>demay</i> in emergencies (Mishnah 7:1). But he is not responsible for anybody else at the dinner, except for his dependents.</i> lying on a couch it would not be <i>prima facie</i> evidence, since I say that he is lying on the couch after making the required mental stipulations. If his son is lying down next to him, he has to tithe for him; he does not have to tithe for another <i>ḥaver</i>.”

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who accepts upon himself to be a <i>ḥaver</i> will not sell to the <i>am haäreẓ</i> wet or dry<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">The entire essence of fellowship is to preserve food grown or raised in the Land of Israel from ritual impurity. Since the <i>am haäreẓ</i> is characterized by his disregard for ritual purity (except in Jerusalem during the three annual pilgrimages when the Temple was standing, when “all of Israel are <i>ḥaverim</i>”), food sold to the <i>am haäreẓ</i> will certainly become defiled.</i> and will not buy wet from him<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">There is a rabbinic decree (explained in the next Note) that any fluid which becomes impure in any degree becomes impure in the first degree. In particular, unwashed hands are always impure in the second degree; the <i>am haäreẓ</i> can be assumed not to wash his hands before handling either drinks or wet food (such as olives); the food therefore is impure in the first degree and unfit for the <i>ḥaver</i>. (Fluids that cause impurity are water, human body fluids, grape juice or wine, olive oil, and date honey.)</i>, is not guest of an <i>am haäreẓ</i> and does not have him as a guest in his clothing<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">The impurities caused by certain human discharges, such as gonorrhea and menstrual blood, impart original impurity not only to the human himself but also to his clothes. Hence, the clothes of an <i>am haäreẓ</i> have to be considered sources of original impurity and a <i>ḥaver</i> can have an <i>am haäreẓ</i> person as a guest only if he sends him first to the ritual bath to be purified and then clothes him in his own pure clothing. The stages of impurity are, in order, 1) “grandfather of impurity,” a dead person and the house in which there is a corpse, 2) original impurity, anything in contact with a corpse or in a house in which there is a corpse (with a few exceptions), and any other impurity described in the Torah, 3) impurity in the first degree, imparted by contact with original impurity or any impure fluid (because fluids might be in contact with impure human discharges, as above), 3) impurity in the second degree, imparted by contact with impurity of the first degree, also the status of any unwashed hands unless the person was careful that his hands should not touch anything absentmindedly, 4) impurity in the third degree, imparted by contact with impurity of the second degree, applicable to sacrifices and heave only, not to profane food (including profane food eaten under the rules of the heave), and 5) impurity in the fourth degree, imparted by contact with impurity of the third degree, applicable to sacrifices only, but not to heave or to profane food.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, moreover he should not raise animals of the flock<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Sheep or goats. The prohibition applies only to goats whose grazing is destructive to young trees and should be permitted in semi-desert conditions only, never near cultivated land.</i>, should not overstep bounds by vows<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">The scrupulous should not make vows for fear of not fulfilling them to the letter. Laughter might be considered a sacrilege; since nobody would laugh in the presence of a king, how could one laugh in the always present fear of the Almighty King of Kings.</i> and laughter, should not defile himself with the dead<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Since priests may not defile themselves unless it be for a close relative (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:1" href="/Leviticus.21.1"><i>Lev.</i> 21:1</a>). However, for the majority view, burying the dead is a most sacred duty because its reward is entirely in the hands of Heaven since the object of the duty can no longer give thanks.</i>, and be a regular in the house of study. They said to him, these are not included.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He will not buy wet from him,” hence dry is permitted since the <i>amē haäreẓ</i> are trustworthy as to preparation<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Fluids always become impure by contact. Produce in the ground cannot become impure. Produce harvested dry can become impure if first it was “prepared” by contact with water (or any of the fluids mentioned above), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:38" href="/Leviticus.11.38"><i>Lev.</i> 11:38</a>. Since the <i>pu‘al</i> verb יתן is written defective, orthographically identical to the active <i>pi‘el</i> form, it is concluded that even passive wetting must have an active ingredient, <i>viz.</i>, that the wetting should be agreeable to the owner. Since the status of the produce depends on the owner and the rules were known to the <i>am haäreẓ</i>, his word in this matter can be trusted.</i>. And it was stated: “The <i>am haäreẓ</i> is trustworthy to say, these fruits were never prepared. But he is not trustworthy to say that they were prepared but did not accept impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">If the produce is prepared, the <i>am haäreẓ</i> by his person will make it most likely that it is impure.</i>.”
By the first statement he invalidated the second<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Jehudah, who in Mishnah 2 permitted to be a guest at the <i>am haäreẓ</i>’s, accepts all restrictions given in Mishnah 3. Why can he not accept that the <i>ḥaver</i> will eat only dry produce not prepared for impurity?</i>. Does this not disagree with Rebbi Jonah, since Rebbi Jonah said, <i>ḥaverim</i> are not suspect to eat or to serve to others? He should not go, defile his body and come to defile pure food<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">If the problem were only food, R. Jehudah could disagree. But since the chairs and couches in the house of the <i>am haäreẓ</i> must be presumed to be originally impure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:6" href="/Leviticus.15.6"><i>Lev</i>. 15:6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:20" href="/Leviticus.15.20">20</a>), the <i>ḥaver</i> will not be able to remain undefiled in that house; returning, he might defile all pure food in his own house.</i>. It does not disagree even with Rebbi Yose, since Rebbi Yose said, <i>ḥaverim</i> are suspect to eat but not to serve to others. There one speaks about purity but here about tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">It follows that the “<i>ḥaver</i>” of R. Yose is not really a <i>ḥaver</i> but a person trustworthy for tithes.</i>. “He who is trustworthy for purities is trustworthy for tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">See the previous Halakhah.</i>.”
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 2:13" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).2.13">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 2:13</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 30b" href="/Bekhorot.30b">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i> 30b</a>.</i>: “Everybody who comes has to accept<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Tosephta of the next paragraph, he has publicly to accept the restrictions imposed on a <i>ḥaver</i>.</i>, even a learned <i>ḥaver</i><sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">One is forced to explain this “<i>ḥaver</i>” by “trustworthy person.”</i>; but a rabbi<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">The חכם everywhere is the acting rabbi.</i> who has been sitting in rabbinical court does not have to accept because he did it at the moment he joined the court.” Rebbi La said, only if he really accepted it from the first hour on<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi La, student of R. Simeon ben Laqish, represents the later generations when abstinence from ritually unclean food was no longer a prerequisite for a rabbinical position.</i>. Rebbi Yose asked, if it was from the first hour, what is the difference between a <i>ḥaver</i> and an <i>am</i> <i>ḥaäreẓ</i>? The position of Rebbi La parallels that of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, for Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish came to those of the house of Rebbi Yannai<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">The yeshivah of R. Yannai, after the latter’s death.</i>; the women saw him and removed themselves from before him<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">So that he should not come in contact with any of their clothes and become impure.</i>. He said to them, I shall come to your house; I am an <i>am haäreẓ</i> with respect to purities<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">He was not a <i>ḥaver</i> in the sense of the Mishnah even though he was number two in the rabbinical establishment of his time.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">A different text with the same meaning in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 2:14" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).2.14">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 2:14</a>, a substantially different text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 30b" href="/Bekhorot.30b">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i> 30b</a>.</i>: “He answers<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">The acceptance of the rules of fellowship has to be in public in front of the resident <i>ḥaverim</i>; his wife, servants, and minor children will accept before him.</i> to the fellowship, his sons and household answer to him.” Some Tannaïm state: “He, his sons, and his household answer to the fellowship.” They do not disagree: the first, if they are dependent on their father<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">The acceptance of the rules of fellowship has to be in public in front of the resident <i>ḥaverim</i>; his wife, servants, and minor children will accept before him.</i>, the second, if they do not depend on their father. Rebbi Ḥalaphta ben Saul stated, adults answer to the fellowship, minors answer to him<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ḥalaphta ben Saul disagrees with the explanation just given; according to him, only minors do not have to appear personally before the fellowship. In the Babli, Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel is reported to require appearance of the (adult) sons before the fellowship because that is the more impressive ceremony.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 2:11" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).2.11">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 2:11</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 30b" href="/Bekhorot.30b">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i> 30b</a>: “One accepts for ‘wings’ and after that one accepts for purities.”</i>: “One brings close<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">People who are interested in following the strict rules are first trained to follow the strict discipline of washing their hands before they are taught the intricacies of the rules of pure food.</i> for ‘wings’<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">“Wings” are the hands the person who is a candidate for fellowship has to promise to wash before eating (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Bekhorot 30b" href="/Rashi_on_Bekhorot.30b">Rashi in <i>Bekhorot</i> 30b</a>). The institution of washing one’s hands for profane food follows the discipline of the heave (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 2:1:2-15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.2.1.2-15"><i>Bikkurim</i> 2:1</a>, fol. 64d). The washing required here is for any food since even dry food may become wet by saliva. The Babylonian version of the same discipline is to wash only for meals taken with bread (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 106a" href="/Chullin.106a"><i>Ḥulin</i> 106a</a>), but there fellowship was unknown.</i> and after that one teaches about purity<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Pure food. Taking טהרות as a plural of abstraction.</i>.” Rebbi Isaac ben Eleazar said, wings, smells<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">From נדף “to give smell” (Maimonides to <i>Zavim</i> 4:6). The Biblical original impurities produced by a person with gonorrhea refer not only to things he touches but also those on which he sits or which he carries or moves otherwise. A lesser impurity is imparted to things above him but which he does not move. These are called מדף, “a smell of impurity.” The list given is a syllabus of the different kinds of impurity the novice has to learn about.</i>, pushings<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">Vessels and foodstuffs that the <i>zav</i>, the person with gonorrhea, pushes or moves. These become impure even if never touched by the <i>zav</i>.</i>, purity<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">All other impurities treated in Mishnah tractate <i>Ṭahorot</i>.</i>, tithes.
“In earlier times, they said: One removes from fellowship a <i>ḥaver</i> who became tax collector. They changed to say, one removes him from fellowship for the entire time he acts as tax collector. Once he left his collectorship, he is again treated as a <i>ḥaver.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 3:4" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).3.4">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 3:4</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 31a" href="/Bekhorot.31a">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i> 31a</a>. Since taxes in antiquity were usually farmed out, the tax collector had to pay the anticipated taxes up front to the government and then try to recover them from the population. Since he incurred expenses for the collection, he was forced to collect more than what he had paid to the government. Any amount taken above his expenses is possibly theft, and certainly any amount taken above the official rate is robbery. The tax collector would not be in business if he did not make money. Hence, he probably is a thief and a robber and disqualified as member of a religious fellowship. However, in the absence of bidders the Roman government would force people to become tax collectors; the prior rule was therefore no longer enforceable or applicable.</i>”
Ḥiyya ben Rebbi Abun<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">He probably is R. Ḥiyya ben Abba.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: One does not remove from fellowship a <i>ḥaver</i> who went outside the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">The land outside the Land of Israel is impure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Amos 7:17" href="/Amos.7.17"><i>Amos</i> 7:17</a>); the person who leaves the Land automatically becomes impure. But this impurity is not from the Torah and is easily removed upon return; hence, leaving the Land on trips was not frowned upon (except for priests who had no compelling reason to go abroad.) Because of this impurity, the Israeli fellowship was impossible in Babylonia at all times.</i>. Hence<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">The place of this sentence is not here but three paragraphs earlier (but it possibly is a statement by R. Joḥanan.) Since the minor takes the obligation of fellowship through his father, his father, not the fellowship, is required to teach him.</i>, a minor does not need to be brought close.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The sages did not obligate bakers to separate more than the heave of the tithe and <i>ḥallah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">They do not have to take the Second Tithe, not even in order to redeem it, in order to keep prices down. The Halakhah will address the questions raised by such a policy.</i>. Grocers are not permitted to sell <i>demay</i>. All who sell wholesale in large quantities may sell <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">Since the buyer knows that he buys <i>demay</i>.</i>.
