The following sell wholesale in large quantities, for example grain buyers<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>sitona</i>,<i>ae</i>,<i>m</i>., “purchaser of grain, commissioner, purveyor”, from Greek σιτώνης “public buyer of grain,” wholesale grain merchant who worked with or for the government (E. G.).</i> and sellers.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> There<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 5:1 requires that he who buys from a baker has to give <i>ḥallah</i>, heave of the tithe, and redeem the Second Tithe. The Mishnah here demands that the baker himself give <i>ḥallah</i> and heave of the tithe.</i>, we have stated: “If one buys from the baker<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">The baker is the craftsman who does the actual baking in his <i>tannur</i>, a stove in the form of a truncated cone, the fire burning on the bottom. The bread dough is brought in from the top and slapped onto the walls to be baked into pittah; this motion of “slapping down” is expressed by the root נחת. The seller of baked goods, either the baker in a separate store adjacent to his baking establishment, or an independent dealer, is the פְּלָטֵר, from Greek πρατήρ. The baker will also bake other people’s dough for a fee; that case is not considered here.</i>, how does he tithe?” There you say, the buyer separates, and here you say, the baker separates. Rebbi Jonah said, Rebbi Eleazar and Rebbi Joḥanan disagree. One says, here if he works in purity<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">The baker is not a <i>ḥaver</i>, but he has hired a <i>ḥaver</i> to knead the dough so that it should not become impure. If the baker handles dry flour or dry bread, nothing happens since profane dry food can be made impure only by original impurity. Therefore, the <i>ḥaver</i> may take the heave of the tithe and <i>ḥallah</i>.</i>, there if he works in impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">One does not trust him to handle impure heave and <i>ḥallah</i> following the rules.</i>. The other says, here in retail, there in wholesale. We did not know who said what. Since Rebbi Joḥanan said<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">In a related discussion, Halakhah 5:1. (The reading מה is from the Rome ms., Venice print: מאן.)</i>, because of purity, it follows that Rebbi Eleazar is the one who said, here in retail, there in wholesale. It is difficult for Rebbi Joḥanan, if he works in purity, let him separate everything<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">Why should he be exempt from Second Tithe?</i>. It would be logical that he should not give anything because one does not deliver certain grain<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">Grain from which it is known with certainty that the Heave of the Tithe was not taken. The baker here must be an <i>am haäreẓ</i> since we already have stated that the <i>ḥaver</i> may not sell <i>demay</i> at all. Hence, in the majority of cases, the Second Tithe, together with the heave of the tithe, are already taken and there would remain only <i>ḥallah</i> which is a duty upon bread dough only.</i> to the <i>am haäreẓ</i>. It is difficult for Rebbi Eleazar, if it is in retail, let him separate everything. Grocers are not permitted to sell <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">There is no reason why the bread retailer should be treated in any way different from other retailers.</i>!
The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: It<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of Mishnah 2:4.</i> is Rebbi Meïr’s, since Rebbi Meïr said, they permitted to sell <i>demay</i> only to the commissioned grain merchant<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">But not any other wholesaler of foodstuffs. The full statement is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 56a" href="/Bava_Metzia.56a">Babli <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 56a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose objected, does not the Mishnah disagree? “The following sell wholesale in large quantities, <i>for example</i> commissioners and grain merchants.” Hence, there must be others! Rebbi Yose said, this is not what Rebbi Eleazar referred to, but the following, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 3:10" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).3.10">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 3:10</a>, on the statement of the Mishnah, “The Sages did not obligate bakers to separate more than the heave of the tithe and <i>ḥallah</i>,” in slightly different wording.</i>: “When is this statement operative, if he sells in his bakeshop or at the door of his bakeshop. But if he was selling in the bakery or in his bakeshop adjacent to the bakery, he must give all tithes on it.” The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: It is Rebbi Meïr’s, since Rebbi Meïr said, they only permitted to the commissioned grain merchant to sell <i>demay</i>. Which (statement of) Rebbi Meïr? The one we have stated next<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">The next Mishnah, in abbreviated form.</i>: “Rebbi Meïr says, something which usually he measured wholesale and he measured retail, the retail is adjunct to the wholesale.” Rebbi Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">The Great Rebbi Ḥiyya, collector of Tosephtot. His formulation, which in contrast to the Mishnah refers not to the standing of the seller but to the goods sold, is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 3:10" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).3.10">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 3:10</a>.</i> stated: “Something which usually is measured wholesale and he measured it retail, the retail is adjunct to the wholesale<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">Somebody who buys a retail quantity from a wholesaler must expect to receive <i>demay</i>.</i>.” And so the bakery is retail but the bakeshop is wholesale, there<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">If the bakery, or the baker’s workshop physically connected to the retail operation, would sell wholesale, it nevertheless would be a retail operation.</i> the wholesale is adjunct to the retail.
The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the Mishnah’s statement that the retailer but not the wholesaler must tithe <i>demay</i>. The colleagues say that it is a religious law to protect the innocent. Rebbi La says, it is a commercial law that the one who takes the larger margin is better able to absorb the costs.</i>: Because of the children lest they should eat <i>ṭevel</i>. Rebbi La in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, in retail, because the seller takes the large margin, the seller separates<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">He separates heave of tithes and Second Tithe and redeems the Second Tithe.</i>. In wholesale, because the buyer will take the large margin, the buyer separates. One statement supports one and another statement supports the other. A statement supports the colleagues: “Rebbi Neḥemiah said, that which is connected to a retail outlet is like retail, connected to a wholesale outlet it is wholesale.<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">Anything which the retailer sells must be tithed because it is accessible to the general public, but anything the wholesaler sells, even an occasional retail quantity to the limited circle of his customers, may be <i>demay</i>. The opposite statement is ascribed to R. Neḥemiah in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 3:11" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).3.11">Tosephta, <i>Demay</i> 3:11</a>: “R. Neḥemiah says, anything sold in retail quantity is retail, in wholesale quantity it is wholesale,” supporting R. La.</i>” A statement supports Rebbi La. It was stated: “Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna of the fourth generation, associate of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel.</i> said, if he measured with a retail measure, he is obligated. Even if he did measure only one seah and a quarter <i>qab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">¼<i>qab</i> = 1/24<i>seah</i>.</i>, he must tithe the quarter <i>qab.</i>” Rebbi Zeïra said, computation of gain is between them<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">The argument between R. Neḥemiah here and R. Ismael ben R. Joḥanan ben Beroqa is whether price plays any role. R. Ismael negates the role of price; R. Neḥemiah’s argument is detailed by R. Zeïra.</i>. If he measured for him the <i>seah</i> at the rate of quarter <i>qabs</i>, since the seller takes the large margin, the seller separates. If he measures the quarter <i>qab</i> at the rate of <i>seahs</i>, since the buyer will take the large margin, the buyer separates.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Meïr says, something which usually is sold retail and he sold wholesale, the wholesale is adjunct to the retail<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">And must be tithed; in the following case, the retail quantity may be sold as <i>demay.</i></i>; something which usually is sold wholesale and he sold retail, the retail is adjunct to the wholesale. What is wholesale? For dry produce, three <i>qab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">About 6.4 liter or 1.7 US gals.</i>, for liquids, the value of one <i>denar</i><sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">According to Maimonides, one gold denar which equals 24 or 25 silver denar.</i>. Rebbi Yose says, baskets of figs, baskets of grapes, and boxes of vegetables, all these if sold <i>en bloc</i><sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Arukh</i> gives two interpretations. 1) “Anything that is neither weighed nor measured is called אכסרה.” 2) “ ‘At a loss’, for in Arabic loss (or damage) is called כֹסארה خسارة”. The first interpretation seems to be appropriate here; according to the second one, all produce should be exempted if sold at a loss. Since the Halakhah does not discuss R. Yose’s statement, his opinion is not followed in practice.</i> are free.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> In the opinion of this Tanna, they gave a measure for dry produce but not for fluids, they gave a value for fluids but not for dry produce. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 3:12" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).3.12">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 3:12</a>.</i>: “A <i>hin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">A fluid measure, half a <i>seah</i>, equal to the three <i>qab</i> mentioned in the Mishnah as dry measure.</i> is wholesale, less than a <i>hin</i> is retail. A <i>nummus</i><sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">In R. Ḥiyya’s time of honest coinage and stable prices. Latin <i>nummus</i>, also called <i>nummus sestertius</i>, “a small coin.”</i> is retail, more than a <i>nummus</i> is wholesale.” In the opinion of that Tanna, they gave a measure for fluids but not for dry produce, they gave a value for dry produce but not for fluids.
Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Yoẓadaq: It is a condition imposed by the Court that the heave of the tithe is on the seller and Second Tithe on the buyer. Where do we stand? If in retail, because the seller takes the large margin, the seller separates. If in wholesale, because the buyer will take the large margin, the buyer separates<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">In both cases, one party pays for both heave and Second Tithe.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, explain it if he says it is of his own harvest<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">Not classifiable as either wholesale or retail.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, even if you say it is not his own harvest, if he says in my opinion it was brought in order<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">If the seller says that the produce is in order it only means that the heave of the tithe was taken (reading and interpretation of R. Abraham ben David.) Maimonides does not read נתקון here but נְתַקֵּן, “let us put in order;” if both parties agree to put the produce in order at the point of sale, the heave is on the seller and the Second Tithe on the buyer, irrespective of a wholesale or retail transaction. (Maimonides <i>Maäser</i> 10:5).</i>, even so they tell him that it is a condition imposed by the Court that the heave of the tithe be on the seller and Second Tithe on the buyer.
Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Hoshaiah: It is a condition imposed by the Court that the fat<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">That fat of a four-legged animal, which in a sacrifice would go on the altar, is forbidden for profane use (Lev. 3:17). It has to be cut out before the piece of meat is sold.</i> be on the butcher and the sciatic tendon<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">It may not be eaten (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 32:33" href="/Genesis.32.33"><i>Gen</i>. 32:33</a>). It must be cut out together with all the fat in which it is embedded. The butcher would naturally cut out only the minimum of fat in order to maximize his profit.</i> on the buyer. Rebbi Abbahu made it common usage in Caesarea that both should be on the buyer, so that they would amply take out.

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One lets the poor and the stranger<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ξένος, meaning either a non-resident or a passer-by (<i>Berakhot</i> p. 619).</i> eat <i>demay.</i> Rabban Gamliel let his laborers<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">If these were not poor, they would not be working as day laborers. However, since it was usual that the employer feed his journeymen, Rabban Gamliel saved by giving them <i>demay</i>, and this is not permitted.</i> eat <i>demay.</i> The House of Shammai say, the administrators of charity give the tithed food to those who do not tithe, and the untithed food to those who tithe, so that it turns out that everybody eats produce that is in order<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">It is stated explicitly in a <i>baraita</i>, quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 17b" href="/Eruvin.17b">Babli <i>Eruvin</i> 17b</a>, that the House of Shammai disagree with the first sentence of the Mishnah and forbid to give <i>demay</i> to the poor.</i>. But the Sages say, one collects<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tamḥui</i> and chest, as described in <i>Peah</i> 8:7, is collected and distributed without inquiring into the degree of religious observance of either donor or recipient.</i> without asking and one distributes without asking, and he who wants to put in order should put in order.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Jonah said, our Mishnah speaks about poor <i>ḥaverim</i>, and about guests following Rebbi Joshua. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Ma‘serot</i> 2:1: “Donkey drivers and private persons on the road from place to place may eat and they are free (from the obligation to tithe) until they reach their destination. Therefore, if their host gave them a separate room and they stayed there overnight, they have to tithe, otherwise they are exempt. It happened that Rebbi Joshua went to Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai at Beror Ḥayil, and some inhabitants of these localities brought them figs. They asked him, do we have to tithe? He said to them, if we stay overnight, we are obligated to tithe; otherwise, we are not obligated to tithe.” Because R. Joshua was such an important personality he and his students certainly were placed in a separate room; therefore, staying overnight alone creates the obligation of tithes. The obligation for heave and tithes only starts with the storage of the harvest. Since R. Joshua would not travel without a large group of students, keeping him overnight creates the duty to give heave and tithes. This is the explicit interpretation given in Yerushalmi <i>Ma‘serot</i> 2:3.</i>: “It happened that Rebbi Joshua went to Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai in Beror Ḥayil<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Neither name nor location of this place are certain but it is known that it was the home town of Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai.</i>, and some local people brought them fruits. Rebbi Joshua said to them, if they<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">His students.</i> stay overnight, we are obliged to tithe; otherwise, we are not obliged to tithe.” Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah speaks about <i>am haäreẓ</i> poor. If you restrict to poor <i>ḥaverim</i>, you lock the door before <i>am haäreẓ</i> poor<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">If it becomes expensive to give to the <i>am haäreẓ</i> poor, nobody will give to them.</i>. How does Rebbi Yose hold about “the stranger?<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Jonah, there is no problem; since the traveller is not really poor it is necessary to say that the Mishnah applies only to <i>ḥaverim</i> who will put their food in order. But why does R. Yose need to mention the traveller?</i>” Following what was stated: “(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:45" href="/Leviticus.25.45"><i>Lev.</i> 25:45</a>) ‘Those who dwell among you,’ to include the stranger; Rebbi Eliezer said, that means the Gentile stranger.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">It is permitted to buy oneself goodwill with <i>demay</i> even though one may not satisfy a formal obligation with <i>demay.</i></i>”
It was stated: “One has to declare.” In the opinion of Rebbi Jonah, it is understood<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">One has to inform him that he receives <i>demay</i> so that he may bring it in order.</i> that the Mishnah speaks about poor <i>ḥaverim.</i> In the opinion of Rebbi Yose, the Mishnah speaks about <i>am haäreẓ</i> poor; even if he informs him, what does he gain<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>am</i> <i>haäreẓ</i> does not care, what has one gained by informing the recipient? The answer is that only a minority of the <i>amē haäreẓ</i> do not care, so why should he hurt the decent majority because of the actions of a few nonobservant persons?</i>? Because of one who does not put in order?
Rebbi Mana said, this means that he who gives in his home<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">One has to inform him that he receives <i>demay</i> so that he may bring it in order.</i> may not give from Sabbatical value because he would pay his debt with Sabbatical value. As it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>am</i> <i>haäreẓ</i> does not care, what has one gained by informing the recipient? The answer is that only a minority of the <i>amē haäreẓ</i> do not care, so why should he hurt the decent majority because of the actions of a few nonobservant persons?</i>: “Neither Sabbatical produce nor Second Tithe may be used to satisfy a loan or a debt. One does not use them for best men’s meals<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since these meals are due in reciprocity, they constitute a debt that can not be satisfied either with Sabbatical fruits, which are ownerless even if grown on the farmer’s own field, or Second Tithe, which is Heaven’s money.</i>. One does not pay with them obligatory payments. One does not use them to pledge for charity in the synagogue. But one may use them for goodwill; then one has to notify.”
There<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Demay</i> 7:3: “A worker who does not trust his employer should take one fig and say: This fig and the nine following shall be tithe on the 90 remaining that I will eat; this one will be heave of the tithe, the others will be redeemed by coins. (Mishnah 4) Then he saves one fig (assuming the employer gave him 100 for lunch.) Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, he should not save (but buy one with his own money and give it to the Cohen) because he reduces the work of the employer (by eating less than the allotted ration, he has less strength to work.) Rebbi Yose said, he should not save because this is a condition of the Court (that the employer must pay for the heave of the tithe.)”<br>The argument here refers to what Rabban Gamliel did. Since he is mentioned by name, what he did is not accepted. The Sages conclude that the employer must give his farmhands fully tithed food.</i> we have stated: “The worker who does not trust the employer.” There you say, the worker separates. But here you say that the employer separates<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Since the action of Rabban Gamliel is not quoted as opinion of the Sages.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, there if he serves him counted portions, here if he lets him eat from the bowl<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Cooked food, or figs and dates, in a big bowl from which each farmhand scoops up what he can. Then it is impossible for the worker to determine what the heave of tithe should be.</i>.
Rebbi Zeriqan, Simeon bar Abba, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: A medical doctor who is a <i>ḥaver</i>, if he feeds a sick person who is an <i>am haäreẓ</i>, shall put the food into his patient’s hand<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>am haäreẓ</i> will eat <i>demay</i> but the <i>ḥaver</i> is forbidden to serve others anything not fully tithed.</i> but not into his mouth. That is, if it is <i>demay</i>, but if it is certain<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">That no heave of the tithe has been given. In that case, the doctor would commit a sin and lose his fellowship standing in handing such food to the <i>am haäreẓ</i> since it would clearly be forbidden (assuming that the sickness is not life-threatening and some other remedy is available.)</i>, it is forbidden even to put it in his hand. This means, if it is the patient’s property, but the doctor’s is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>ḥaver</i>, or even simply the person trustworthy for tithes, is not permitted to serve others anything that is not in order.<br>“Descendant of Noah” means a Gentile, since all mankind is descended from Noah. The expression is used here to indicate that all of mankind are obligated by the Seven Commandments given to Noah (prohibition of murder, idolatry, incest and adultery, eating limbs taken from a living animal, blasphemy, and anarchism.)</i> for a Jew; for a descendant of Noah<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Since parts cut from live animals are forbidden not only to Jews but to all mankind (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 57b" href="/Sanhedrin.57b">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i>57b</a>, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 9:4" href="/Genesis.9.4">Rashi on <i>Gen.</i> 9:4</a>).</i> it is permitted even if it is certain and the patient’s property, but the doctor’s is forbidden. But if it was a limb taken from a living animal it is forbidden even if it is the patient’s property, that he should not be caused to stumble<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the rules for the adminstrators of charity.</i>.
It is difficult for the House of Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the rules for the adminstrators of charity.</i>, should he lose because he is worthy<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Assuming that everybody gets the same, the observant end up with less to eat than the nonobservant. Hence, the observant should get slightly more than the nonobservant.</i>? He adds what he has to put in order. What is the reason of the House of Shammai? Even if you regulate him now, can you regulate him in the future<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">If he takes it home untithed, the <i>am haäreẓ</i> will eat it untithed, and it will turn out that the administrator has led a fellow Jew into sin by giving him untithed food.</i>? You cannot regulate him. What is the reason of the rabbis? If you investigate him<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">If you ask people whether they follow the rules of fellowship or not, the nonobservant will not give and you have committed two evils: you have reduced the amount of food available for the poor and you have prevented fellow Jews from fulfilling the commandment of charitable giving.</i> he will reduce his charity giving.
“<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephata <i>Demay</i> 3:16,17.</i> The administrators of charity should not publicly announce on a holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">On weedays, they announce in the synagogue how many poor they have to feed and how much they need from every householder. On holidays, when money transactions are forbidden, even charity cannot be collected publicly.</i> in the way they announce on weekdays, but they collect in private, carry it in their bosom, and distribute for each quarter separately. The administrators of the charity chest<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Peah</i> 8:7. In the Tosephta, this is the majority opinion, against R. Meïr who holds that one does not take from questionable sources during the Sabbatical year.</i> should not be inquisitive in the courtyards of those who eat from the yield<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody may eat from the yield of the Sabbatical year the spontaneous growth of fallow fields. Here, the reference is to produce grown for cash in the Sabbatical.</i> of the Sabbatical year. If one gave them bread it is permitted, because Jews are not suspected of giving anything other than money or eggs<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Money received for a crop in the Sabbatical year or eggs, not subject to Sabbatical rules, bought with such money. If somebody gives bread, one may assume that it was not from grain grown for cash in the Sabbatical year.</i>.” Rebbi Ḥananiah in the name of Rebbi Phineas: That means, at a place where people sow<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For cash, to be able to pay their taxes.</i> but do not eat. Since they are not suspected to eat, <i>a fortiori</i> not to feed.
“Cohanim who prepare dough in purity<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>ḥallah</i> has to be taken from bread dough under the rules of the heave and be eaten in purity, and the <i>am haäreẓ</i> by his touch would give primary impurity to the wet dough, as explained in Halakhah 2:3, it was customary to hire a Cohen to knead the dough in purity, take <i>ḥallah</i> to eat it, and then hand the dough over to the <i>am haäreẓ</i> to bake it.</i> should not be inquisitive in the courtyards of those who eat from the yield of the Sabbatical year.” Some Tannaïm<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 3:17" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).3.17">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 3:17</a>.</i> stated: “They should be inquisitive.” Rebbi Phineas said, he who says that they should be inquisitive, because of their <i>ḥallah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Produce of the Sabbatical year, being legally abandoned property, is exempt from heave and tithes. But dough prepared from grain grown in the Sabbatical year, even if the grain was legally harvested, is always subject to <i>ḥallah.</i> So the Tosephta requires the Cohen not only to take <i>ḥallah</i>, but also to check whether the grain might be <i>demay.</i></i>; he who says that they should not be inquisitive, for he would save him from the simple sin and lead him to a deadly one. <i>Ṭevel</i> is a deadly sin, the Sabbatical year is a simple prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">If the Cohen would refuse to knead the dough of the nonobservant and to take <i>ḥallah</i>, the nonobservant would knead the dough himself and not give <i>ḥallah.</i> However, dough from which <i>ḥallah</i> was not taken is <i>ṭevel</i>, and its consumption is as bad a sin as consuming grain from which heave was not taken. Since the Cohen by his action leads the nonobservant into sin, the sin is the Cohen’s.</i>.
What does he do<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The Cohen who has taken <i>ḥallah</i> from dough he suspects to be from grain raised during the Sabbatical year, from which one cannot partake.</i>? Rebbi Huna said, he brings a suspect Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">He is suspected to eat from produce commercially grown in the Sabbatical year. Still, the observant Cohen must make sure that he eats <i>ḥallah</i> in purity; hence, he has to send him to the <i>miqweh</i> (and, as R. Mana points out, watch him in purity until sundown.) See <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.1">Mishnah <i>Berakhot</i> 1:1</a>.</i>, lets him immerse himself, and serves it to him. But then does he not deliver pure foods to an <i>am haäreẓ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Who can be assumed to make everything impure (except in Jerusalem, during the time of the Temple, on holidays when “all of Israel are <i>ḥaverim</i>.”) In this case, the observant Cohen would lead the nonobservant into sin.</i>? Rebbi Mana said, he brings a suspect Cohen, lets him immerse himself, watches him until sundown and then serves it to him. But then does he not support the sinners? Rebbi Simeon from Carsan in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: He keeps it until the day before Passover and then burns it<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Pure <i>terumah</i> and <i>ḥallah</i> must be eaten; they may be burned only if they are either impure or on Passover eve if they contain leavened material. Since it is forbidden to make holy things impure intentionally, the Cohen has to keep <i>ḥallah</i> in purity until he is forced to burn it on Passover eve.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who wants to cut off greens of vegetables<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">For example turnip greens which usually are not used but may be used as vegetables by the poor. Hence, since it is likely that the leaves will be collected as food, the preparation of the vegetables for transportation and sale signifies the end of the harvest and requires tithing from then on.</i> in order to lighten his load should not throw them away before he tithed. He who buys vegetables on the market<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">These vegetables have a fixed price per unit. The moment the buyer takes up a bunch, he is obliged to pay a fixed amount. Hence, the act of lifting up bunch transfers property rights to the buyer in exchange for a debt incurred. Since now the bunch belongs to the buyer, he cannot return it untithed without losing his standing as being trustworthy for tithes.</i> and then decides to return them should not return them before he tithed, because nothing is missing<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah manuscripts of the Yerushalmi tradition and the Munich ms. of the Babli have מעושר. The manuscripts of the Babylonian tradition and the Rome ms. have מחוסר, which explains the word used here. In Galilee, ח was pronounced like ה and ע.</i> but counting<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The number of bunches the buyer took up.</i>. If he stopped to buy but saw another, better, load, he may change his mind because<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Or better: As long as he did not lift up any of the bunches. Then they never were his property and he is not responsible.</i> he did not take it up.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Eleazar said<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The discussion refers to the first statement of the Mishnah that he who separates greens from bunches of vegetables must tithe first. Since the leaves are thrown away, they are legally abandoned, and anybody who collects abandoned property is free from all obligations of tithing. This being the case, how could the poor who collect the leaves possibly incur any guilt in not tithing and why should the transporter be liable for tithes?<br>Rebbi Meïr’s statement was discussed in Halakhah 2:4.</i>, it is Rebbi Meïr’s, since Rebbi Meïr said, they permitted to sell <i>demay</i> only to the commissioned grain wholesaler. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is according to everybody; there is a difference because the one who takes it up takes it up in retail quantity and the one who abandons does not abandon wholesale<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, while he might get away without tithing from the point of view of Biblical law, he cannot get away if he wants to retain his standing as being trustworthy for tithes.</i>. But does this not disagree with Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">This disagreement between R. Meïr and R. Yose is discussed in <i>Peah</i>6:1 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 43a-44a" href="/Nedarim.43a-44a">Babli <i>Nedarim</i> 43a–44a</a>).</i>, since Rebbi Meïr is the one who says that as soon as a person abandons anything of his property, it is legally abandoned as soon as it leaves his power. There is a difference because the one who takes it up takes it up in retail quantity and the one who abandons does not abandon wholesale. It does not even disagree with Rebbi Yose, for Rebbi Yose says that nothing may leave the power of its owners except if it is taken up; otherwise it was not legally abandoned. There is a difference here, because the one who abandons abandons wholesale, and the one who takes it up, takes up only retail quantities<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Yose, if a large quantity was abandoned, it does not become abandoned property by being collected in small quantities by different persons.</i>.
Ḥizqiah said<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The reference is to the middle part of the Mishnah, that if somebody took from the farmer’s stall (who is neither wholesaler nor retail grocer) a bunch with a clearly marked price, he cannot return it without tithing. Ḥizqiah notes that מעוּשר should be מחוּסר as noted before. In normative Hebrew, זכייה מניין would be a construct state זכית מנין.</i>, nothing is missing for the owners but the credit by counting.
How does he do it<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">How does one give tithes from the vegetables one returns to the market stall.</i>? He takes from the uppermost layer and puts it in order. Is that not forbidden because of robbery<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">If he declares one of the vegetables as tithes, he takes away from the seller. Even if we say that the seller as retailer should be required to tithe himself, if enough customers act like this one, there will be only tithe and heave of the tithe left, and nothing to sell, without the seller having made a single sale. (There are other complications which will be discussed in the next paragraph.)</i>? Like that of Rebbi Simeon bar Cahana<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna of the third generation, student of R. Eliezer.</i> who was supporting Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Eliezer was leaning on his arm while walking.</i>. They passed by a vineyard. He said to him, bring me a sliver<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Taking the sliver from the wooden fence, since taking property worth less than a <i>peruṭa</i> (one sixth or one eighth of a copper <i>as</i>) is not considered robbing.</i> as a toothpick. He changed his mind and told him, do not bring me anything; if everybody would do that, the fence of this man would be gone. Rebbi Ḥaggai was supporting Rebbi Zeïra. A person passed by who was carrying a load of chips. He said to him, bring me a chip as a toothpick. He changed his mind and told him, do not bring me anything; if everybody would do that, the load of this man would be gone. Is not Rebbi Zeïra particularly pious? No, he told us that we should observe the words of our Creator. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: When he took one stalk, nothing was missing but counting them<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Joḥanan repeats the opinion of his teacher Ḥizqiah.</i>. How does he do it? He pays for one of them<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">If the seller is paid, no robbery has occured. The one stalk now is taken as heave of the tithe. The rest of the bunch is permitted to a <i>ḥaver</i> without tithing and the seller can sell it as such.</i> and puts it in order.
But does it not turn out that he creates an obstacle for those who come after him<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">If the produce is <i>demay</i> or <i>ṭevel.</i> One may not give heave from produce already tithed and if the next buyer would use from the already tithed produce for heave of the tithe, that act would be void and he would in fact be eating <i>ṭevel.</i></i>? Let him make a separate heap. But why can he not fix them in his own produce<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Why should the buyer give the seller money to be able to put that bunch in order; let him return it as is with a remark that the prospective buyer will give the heave of the tithe (and redemption of the Second Tithe) from his own produce at home.</i>? Did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">There is a similar text in Tosephta <i>Maäser Šeni</i> 4:8: “One may give the heave of the tithe for produce of an <i>am haäreẓ</i>, even if this creates an obstacle for those who will come later,” meaning later buyers from the <i>am haäreẓ</i> who is the seller; the buyers might treat the produce that was put in order as <i>demay</i> and use it for illegitimate tithing of other produce.</i>: “A person may fix his tithes for someone else, even if he creates an obstacle for those who come after him.” Rebbi Ḥinena said, they made that ordinance for the buyer<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">This particular exception was meant only for the <i>ḥaver</i> who has no choice but to buy from an <i>am haäreẓ</i> seller, in the absence of a <i>ḥaver</i> on the market. Since the <i>ḥaver</i> is prohibited from buying produce that is not in order, he is forced to stipulate that he will put the seller’s produce in order before concluding the deal. He naturally will tell the seller that his produce will be put in order, but he has absolutely no guarantee that the seller will transmit this information to the other buyers. However, since everything will be fine if the seller really warns the next buyers not to use his produce for tithing, no blame can be attached to the <i>ḥaver</i> who uses his own to put the <i>am haäreẓ’</i> s produce in order. But this is permitted only to a bona fide buyer, not to one who changes his mind alter already acquiring the produce.</i> but not for him who returns [produce].
So says the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last part of the Mishnah: “If he stopped <i>to buy</i> but saw another, better batch, he may change his mind because he did not take it up.” Since “to buy” has a connotation of “taking it,” the word is changed to “selecting.”</i>: “If he stood selecting.” So far, of the same kind. Also, from a different kind.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who finds produce on the road and takes it to eat it<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">To eat it on the spot. One may assume that the produce was lost before it was brought to the farmer’s house to be stored. Hence, no heave or tithes are due.</i>, but then changed his mind to store it, should not store it until he tithed it<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Since the first act of storing subjects the produce to heave and tithes.</i>. If from the start he took it up only so that it should not be spoiled<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">One may not let food lie on the road to be spoiled by people trampling upon it. If he simply takes that produce and puts it near the road where it will not go to waste, he did not acquire anything and has no obligation for heave and tithes.</i>, he is free. Anything a person is not permitted to sell as <i>demay</i>, he may not send to a friend as <i>demay.</i> Rebbi Yose permits certain produce<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Produce from which at least the heave of tithe certainly was not taken, may be sent as is to another person on condition that the latter would be informed, since he will not have certain produce lying around his house without putting it in order immediately.</i> on condition that he inform him.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: “One does not pass by foodstuffs.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">But one has to take them up from the road and put them in a safe place. The statement of R. Abbahu is credited in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 64b" href="/Eruvin.64b">Babli (<i>Erubin</i> 64b</a>) to R. Joḥanan in the name of R. Simeon bar Ioḥai.</i>” Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: That means, in earlier times. But now it is permitted because of sorcery<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">As one might come under a spell and get hurt by taking it up.</i>.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.3.3.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Qama</i> 3:3</a>.</i>, we have stated: “He who turns over cow dung in the public domain and a third party was hurt, is liable for damages.” It was also stated<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 2:8" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).2.8">Tosephta <i>Baba Qama</i> 2:8</a>: “He who turns over cow dung in the public domain in order to acquire it, and another person was hurt by it, he is liable, and it is forbidden because of robbery. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, it is not robbery to impair the public domain.” The person who acquired the dung is liable but, since he acquired it, one is forbidden to take it from him, but he may take it from the owner of the cattle since it was in the public domain and an obstacle to travelers.</i>: “It is forbidden because of robbery.” Cahana<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">It is impossible to decide whether Cahana here is Rav Cahana I, a companion of Rav in Babylonia, or Rav Cahana II; see note in <i>Berakhot</i> p. 247.</i> said, only if he turned it over in order to acquire it. But if he did not turn it over in order to acquire it, about that one does not speak. But as regards damages, there is no difference whether he turned it over in order to acquire it or he did not turn it over in order to acquire it; if a third party was hurt, he is liable for damages<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">This contradicts statements of Ḥizqiah in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 3:3:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.3.3.2-5">Yerushalmi <i>Baba Qama</i> 3:3,</a> fol. 3c, and of R. Eleazar in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 29b" href="/Bava_Kamma.29b">Babli <i>Baba Qama</i> 29b</a>.</i>. And here, you say so? Rebbi Abun said, there it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:34" href="/Exodus.21.34"><i>Ex.</i> 21:34</a>): “The one responsible for the pit<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">An unauthorized open pit in the public domain.</i> shall pay,” the one responsible for the damage shall pay. But here (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:22" href="/Deuteronomy.14.22"><i>Deut.</i> 14:22</a>): “You shall give tithes,” you give tithes from your property but not from other people’s property.
“Anything a person is not permitted to sell as <i>demay</i>, he may not send to a friend as <i>demay</i>” Retail quantities which one is not permitted to sell as <i>demay</i>, one may not send to a friend as <i>demay.</i> Wholesale quantities which one is permitted to sell as <i>demay</i>, one may send to a friend as <i>demay.</i> “Rebbi Yose permits certain produce,” be it retail or wholesale quantities, but he forbids retail quantities that are <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the recipient is informed, since it falls under the blanket prohibition of the Mishnah. The exception he makes is for certain produce only.</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: They permitted <i>demay</i> in wholesale quantity because of its being fenced in<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Because everybody knows that wholesale quantities are <i>demay</i>, no <i>ḥaver</i> will refrain from giving the appropriate tithes.</i>.
This is a <i>baraita</i><sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">While the first sentence is Mishnah 2:5, Maimonides (<i>Maäser</i> 11:4) takes everything together as one statement. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 2:12" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).2.12">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 2:12</a> quotes Rebbi Yose that the kinds mentioned in the Mishnah fall under the monetary limitations spelled out in Halakhah 2:5. Maimonides seems to think that the final statement in this paragraph (that R. Yose himself did not act according to his own opinion but deferred to that of his colleagues), applies to the entire Tannaitic statement here. In the opinion of R. Abraham ben David, the first sentence is a quote from the Mishnah, but the following text is from some Tosephta collection. Hence, he thinks that practice should follow R. Yose in 2:5, but not in 3:3.</i>: “Rebbi Yose says, baskets of figs, baskets of grapes, and boxes of vegetables, as long as they are sold <i>en bloc</i>, are free. <sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 2:14" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).2.14">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 2:14</a>: “Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, it happened that the great Rebbi Yose sent me an <i>etrog</i>(<i>citrus medico</i>) from Sepphoris and said to me: this came into my hand from Caesarea. And I understood from this three things: That it was certain, could become impure, and he did not have another one, because if he had, he would have tithed from one on the other.” A discussion of this text appears in Tosaphot <i>‘Avodah zarah</i>58b–59a.</i> Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, the great Rebbi Yose sent me an <i>etrog</i> and said to me: this came into my hand from Caesarea<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Since this was sent from Sepphoris in Galilee, it is likely to come from Caesarea Philippi on the slopes of the Golan heights; cf. Halakhah 2:1. However, the next paragraph might mean that the <i>etrog</i> came from Caesarea maritima.</i>. I understood from this three things: That it was certain, could become impure, and he did not have another one. That it was certain, because the fruits of Caesarea are certain. That it could become impure, because they sprinkle water on it<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. commentary to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1">Halakhah 2:3, Note 137</a>.</i>. That he did not have another one, because if he had, he would have tithed from one on the other.” Why could he not have tithed from itself on itself<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Taken out a small piece as heave of the tithe.</i>? Because he was mindful of the saying of Bar Kappara, for Bar Kappara said that people do not send things that are defective to their friends. But is that not our Mishnah? “Rebbi Yose permits certain produce on condition that he inform him.” This tells you that, even though he disagreed with the rabbis, he did not act according to his own opinion.
Rebbi Zeïra asked before Rebbi Yasa: It this not from the fruits that are exempt at Caesarea<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Etrog</i> is not on the list given in Halakhah 2:1 of <i>demay</i> fruits at Caesarea. Hence, it is not excluded that the <i>etrog</i> came from the Land of Israel near Caesarea Philippi (Banias),</i>? He said to him, did not Rebbi permit fruits at Caesarea? But Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel preceded Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Since he was Rebbi’s father.</i>!

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Wheat brought to be milled<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of the Venice print and the Leyden manuscript. Most Mishnah manuscripts haveהַמּוֹלִיךְ חִיטָּיו לְטָחוּן כּוּתִי “he who brings his wheat to a Samaritan miller.” This text is also confirmed by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Gittin 61b" href="/Rashi_on_Gittin.61b">Rashi, <i>Giṭṭin</i> 61b</a>.</i> by a Samaritan or by an <i>am haäreẓ</i> remains in its prior state for tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">This must refer to the Second Tithe, since grain from which tithe was not taken is referred to as <i>ṭevel</i> or <i>demay.</i></i> and Sabbatical year<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">They are not suspected of switching grain they know belongs to a <i>ḥaver.</i></i>. By a Gentile, it is <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Since he does not care about these things, he will just deliver the correct quantity, irrespective of its source; it may be from the grain delivered by a nonobservant Jew.</i>. Produce deposited with a Samaritan or with an <i>am haäreẓ</i> remains in its prior state for tithes and Sabbatical year; with a Gentile, it is like his produce<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Produce harvested by a Gentile is not subject to tithes and Sabbatical restrictions. However, produce bought by a Jew before it was warehoused is considered to come under the obligation of tithes when it is stored by the Jew. Hence, it depends on when the Jew acquires the Gentile’s produce and one cannot simply say “obligated” or “not obligated.”</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, it is <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">If the Gentile accepts produce for storage, he also accepts from nonobservant Jews and may store everything together. R. Simeon holds that the produce of the Gentile is exempt from tithes and heave; hence, his problem is only with Jewish produce in storage. [This is the interpretation of the Yerushalmi; according to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 67b" href="/Menachot.67b">Babli (<i>Menaḥot</i> 67b</a>) R. Simeon holds that the rabbis decreed <i>demay</i> for Gentile produce so that Jewish landowners should not have an incentive to hand their land over to Gentile sharecroppers.]</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The text is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 1:10:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.1.10.2-6"><i>Ketubot</i> 1:10,</a> fol. 25d.</i>: If his box was exchanged at the miller’s, if it was established that an <i>am haäreẓ</i> gave to be milled there that day, he has to worry<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">He has to worry that the grain is the <i>am haäreẓ</i>’s and therefore <i>demay</i>; then he has to tithe a second time.</i>; otherwise, he does not have to worry. Should he not worry? What is the difference between this and the Saracens? Was it not stated: “If a group of Saracens one day provisioned themselves from forbidden supplies<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">From Jewish suppliers who do not tithe.</i>, then that day becomes a proof for all days<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">All the produce they sell is <i>demay</i> even though the greater part of it may be Gentile and exempt. Hence, if we see that an <i>am haäreẓ</i> uses that mill one day, why should we not assume that an <i>am haäreẓ</i> uses it every day?</i>.” It is impossible for Saracens not to supply themselves. But here it was not established that the <i>am haäreẓ</i> was milling there that day<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The Saracens showed that their persons are not to be trusted. In our case, we know that the <i>am haäreẓ</i> cannot be trusted, which is nothing new and does not need proof. But the mill does not become an <i>amē haäreẓ</i>’ mill by that.</i>.
Rebbi Taypha the Red<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">An Amora of the fourth generation, student of R. Abbahu and sometimes mentioned in connection with the Amora R. Yose.</i> in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: Samaritans can be trusted with deposits. Is that not the Mishnah: “With a Samaritan”? Our Mishnah before they became suspect, he comes to tell us even after they became suspect<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">The Yerushalmi always refers the question whether Samaritans are counted as Jews or as Gentiles to the disagreement between Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel and his son Rebbi, as reported below. However, it is stated in ‘<i>Avodah zarah</i> 5:3, fol. 44d (quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 6a" href="/Chullin.6a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 6a</a>) that in the times of Rebbis Ammi, Assi, and Abbahu, Samaritans were found to use Gentile wine (or to adopt Gentile behavior), and from that moment on Samaritans were considered as Gentiles. However it seems from the Yerushalmi that up to the end of the Talmudic period in Galilee, the separation of Jews and Samaritans was not universally accepted. (One also may assume that parts of Jewish Sadducee communities changed to a Samaritan affiliation after the destruction of the Temple.)</i>. How is it, should he be trusted to say, I took it and replaced it by other deposits that were put in order? If you believe him in that he took it, you should believe him in what he gave. If you do not believe that he took, do not believe that he gave<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 4:22" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).4.22">Tosephta (<i>Demay</i> 4:22</a>), this argument is given in reference to deposits with an <i>am haäreẓ.</i></i>. You believe a Samaritan that he gave<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Grain from another source, but you do not believe him that he exchanged. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 4:24" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).4.24">Tosephta (<i>Demay</i>4:24</a>) one reads: “If he says, I took it and replaced it with some that was put in order, one does not worry either about tithes or about the Sabbatical.”</i> and you do not believe that he took. Rebbi Jonah asked: What are we talking about? If he says, they are my own, even an <i>am haäreẓ</i> should not be trusted. If he says, X<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">A person recognized as a <i>ḥaver.</i></i> took tithes for me, even the Samaritan should be believed. Rebbi Abba said, explain it according to him who says that a Samaritan is like a Gentile. As they disagreed: A Samaritan is like a Gentile, the words of Rebbi. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, a Samaritan is like a Jew in every respect.
Here you say, “by a Gentile miller, they are <i>demay</i>,” there you say, “with a Gentile, they are like his produce.” In the first case, a box among boxes<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">The miller may mistakenly have given his customers a wrong box. In the second sentence of the Mishnah, the produce is stored not in separate boxes but with the Gentile’s produce.</i>, there, produce among his produce.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked before Rebbi Zeïra<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The better reading is: R. Ḥanania before R. Mana; see the preceding note.</i>: Does it mean, really like his produce? Freeing what would be <i>ṭevel</i> at another place<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">It would be a cheap way of tithing to deposit one’s produce with a Gentile and then reclaiming it without taking heave and tithes!</i>? He said to him, for all that is in it<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">It is only equal to the Gentile’s produce if it is <i>in it</i>, i. e., if the produce was in order it does not become even <i>demay</i> at the Gentile’s, but it cannot lose its status of either <i>demay</i> or <i>ṭevel.</i></i>. Rebbi Ḥanina was uselessly opposing Rebbi Aḥa<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">As explained above, this might be “R. Mana.”</i>.
Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abin in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: Rebbi Simeon said, only for one box. If he handed over two boxes, one of them is <i>demay</i> and one is certain<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">We have to assume that here, “certain” does not mean that certainly the heave of the tithe was not taken, but to the contrary, that all heave and tithes were taken and the produce is ready to eat.</i> if they are still in existence<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Then one remains <i>demay</i> and one is certain.</i>; but if the first one was consumed, the second one is <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">We do not say that this may be the one which was put in order.</i>. If a hundred people took from him 100 <i>seah</i> at one time, each one puts his produce in order as <i>demay.</i> If they gave it all to one person, it should be considered as certain<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Simeon suspects the Gentile only to substitute at most one box; if there are 100, of which 99 are in order, there is no reason to treat this as <i>demay.</i></i>. If one person took many <i>seot</i> together, the first one is <i>demay</i>, the rest certain. If he then gave the rest to a hundred people, it should be considered as certain. Did we not state in the name of Rebbi Simeon in the name of Rebbi Ṭarphon: “They made the produce of this Jew like the produce of that Samaritan<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 4:25" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).4.25">Tosephta (<i>Demay</i> 4:25</a>): “Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel and Rebbi Simeon: The Jew’s produce made this Gentile’s produce <i>demay.</i>” The word “Samaritan” in the Yerushalmi seems to be a scribal error. The question is about the interpretation of the Tosephta (or an equivalent <i>baraita</i>), and the answer is that the Tosephta is exactly parallel to the Mishnah and gives the reason for the Mishnah, but it does not extend R. Simeon’s disagreement with the Tanna of the Mishnah.</i>, <i>demay.</i>” That again is the same; Rebbi Simeon said, only for one box.
“<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Toesephta <i>Demay</i> 1:12–14 {commentary in braces}: “12. A storage facility into which Jews and Gentiles contribute; if the majority are Gentile it is certain, if the majority are Jewish, it is <i>demay</i>; these are the words of R. Meïr {who holds that produce of the Holy Land is subject to heave and tithes even if grown by a Gentile.} But the Sages say, it is <i>demay</i> even if all the contributors are Gentile except one Jew {since produce of the Holy Land is not subject to heave and tithes if grown by a Gentile.} 13. Rebbi Yose said, when are these words said, for a private storage facility, but at a government storage facility {for storing taxes paid in kind} one follows the majority. They said to him, you taught us about the storage facility of Jabneh, which is inside the walls, that it is <i>demay</i>, and most of the contributors are Samaritans {who are subject to tithes but certainly will not tithe what they personally do not use}; but a storage facility outside the Land, e. g., that of Ragab {a place in Jordan} is obligated only according to percentages {of the supply coming from the Land.} 13. Rebbi Joshua ben Qabusai said, all my life I read this verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:19" href="/Numbers.19.19"><i>Num.</i> 19:19</a>): ‘<i>The</i> pure shall sprinkle on <i>the</i> impure,’ and I did not understand it until the storage at Jabneh; I learned from the storage facility at Jabneh that one pure person sprinkles on many impure persons.”</i> Rebbi Joshua ben Qabusai<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna of the Fourth generation, son-in-law of R. Aqiba. His father-in-law considers Samaritans as true converts (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 75b" href="/Kiddushin.75b">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 75b</a>) but thinks one cannot intermarry with them because of their deviating interpretation of some incest prohibitions.</i> said, all my life I read this verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:19" href="/Numbers.19.19"><i>Num.</i>19:19</a>): ‘<i>The</i> pure shall sprinkle on <i>the</i> impure,’ that one pure person sprinkles on one impure person, until I learned it from the storage facility at Jabneh. This means that one pure person sprinkles on many impure persons.<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Since one rabbinic Jew makes <i>demay</i> in the presence of many Samaritans.<br>{In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 61b" href="/Nazir.61b">Babli, <i>Nazir</i> 61b</a>, the verse is taken to mean that only a person who may become impure by Biblical standards, i. e., a Jew, can sprinkle the water with the ashes of the red heifer to cleanse another Jew from the impurity of the dead. In <i>Sifry</i>,<i>Num.</i>129, R. Aqiba infers that the water was purifying only if a pure person sprinkles on an impure one but sprinkling on a pure person makes him impure. This is accepted by the Yerushalmi, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 1:2:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.1.2.2-8"><i>Yoma</i> 1:2</a> (fol. 38d); a Babli source (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 14a" href="/Yoma.14a"><i>Yoma</i> 14a</a>) quotes and follows a dissenting opinion of the Sages. <i>Sifry zuta</i>,<i>Ḥuqqat</i> 19, notes that only a pure person becomes impure; a person impure but not through a corpse is not influenced by being sprinkled. Since <i>Sifry zuta</i> in general follows R. Ismael, this supports the position of the Yerushalmi that R. Aqiba’s statement is accepted by everybody.}</i>”
“But the Sages say, it is <i>demay</i> even if all the contributors are Gentile except one Jew<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 1:12" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).1.12">Tosephta 1:12</a>, given above.</i>”. But we have stated: “With a Gentile, they are like his produce<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Why is it not <i>demay</i> as in a storage facility?</i>.” Rebbi Eleazar said, the Sages who follow the line of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Joḥanan said, these are really the Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">They disagree with R. Meïr on the status of Gentile-grown produce and hold that it is exempt from all tithes.</i>. They asked him, what is the reason? He told them, if you get whitehaired, I might tell you<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">But by that time, R. Johanan would have died long ago.</i>. What would he have told them? That they accepted tithes voluntarily<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The returnees from Babylonia accepted the obligation of tithes voluntarily, since these obligations were originally bound to the land distributed by Joshua, and that distribution was obliterated by the exile. See the author’s <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i>, Northvale NJ 1995, p. 280–281. Since the obligation is similar in nature to a rabbinic ordinance, it follows the rules of leniency valid for such ordinances.</i>. Samuel bar Abba<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian Sage Samuel.</i> asked: It is understandable that he did not tell them about pure food, because of a fence for pure food<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">He did not want to imply that heave does not have to be watched according to the standards of Biblical purity because of its rabbinic status, because then they would have disregarded the rules of purity altogether. The same applies to the tithes.</i>. He did not tell them about tithes, as a fence for tithes. He did not tell them about sacrifices; if he had told them, what would he have told them<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">They seemed to have asked about some leniencies in the treatment of Gentiles’ sacrifices; the question is not preserved. Samuel asked, why did he not answer that question since the answer could not have any practical consequence.</i>? For tithes, following him<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">“Practice” in the language of the Yerushalmi is similar to the notion of “practice going back to Moses on Mount Sinai” in the Babli; it means a rule established by the returnees from Babylonia, the “Men of the Great Assembly,” as part of the establishment of rabbinic Judaism. It is part of the original decree of <i>demay.</i></i> who says that they accepted tithes voluntarily.
Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Rebbi Simeon agrees that one must separate tithes as a matter of practice<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">“Practice” in the language of the Yerushalmi is similar to the notion of “practice going back to Moses on Mount Sinai” in the Babli; it means a rule established by the returnees from Babylonia, the “Men of the Great Assembly,” as part of the establishment of rabbinic Judaism. It is part of the original decree of <i>demay.</i></i>. Hence, Rebbi Simeon says that one separates, and the rabbis say that one saparates<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Assuming that the statement of R. Joḥanan refers to the Mishnah, viz., that the Sages say produce deposited with a Gentile is like his own produce, the assertion that R. Simeon requires tithing is trivial since <i>demay</i> certainly must be tithed. The only inference to be drawn is from “agrees”, which would imply that the Sages also require tithing and that the declaration of “his own produce” can only be used in a negative, not in a positive sense. Hence, the Sages also require tithing.</i>
.What is the difference between them? Rebbi Simeon says, he separates and takes money from the tribe<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">He separates heave of the tithe and sells it (cheaply, because there are few qualified buyers) to the Cohen. The Sages would require the heave to be given as a gift.</i>. The rabbis say that he separates and does not take money from the tribe. But is it not even like heave from outside the Land, does he not take money from the tribe<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">In those parts of the Land conquered and sanctified by Moses and Joshua but not settled by the returnees from Babylonia, heave and tithes were taken as a remembrance and sold to the Cohanim. Outside the Biblical boundaries, no heave was ever taken (and could not be taken because that land is impure.)</i>? Maybe it was said on the principle of <i>ṭevel</i> for produce of the Gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of R. Joḥanan never was meant as commentary on our Mishnah but as a remark on the status of produce grown by a Gentile in the Land of Israel. Hence, the inference about the stand taken by the Sages in our Mishnah is invalid.</i>? Since we have stated: Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Simeon say, a Gentile may acquire real estate in the Land of Israel to free it from the tithes imposed on it<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">They disagree with R. Meïr who holds that all land in the Land of Israel is subject to heave and tithes, irrespective of the owner; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 4:6:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.4.6.9"><i>Peah</i> Chapter 4, Notes 129–131</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Ḥiyya. in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Rebbi Simeon agrees that nevertheless he<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">The Jew who buys produce grown in the Land by a Gentile. This obligation is one of tradition.</i> must separate tithes as a matter of practice.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who gives to the innkeeper’s wife, tithes what he gives her and what he takes from her because she is suspected to switch<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">The motives imputed to the innkeeper’s wife (or the female innkeeper) are not clear. She is supposed to cook for her guests the food they provide. The anonymous Tanna holds that a <i>ḥaver</i> never hands anything <i>demay</i> over to a fellow Jew. This Mishnah (and the next) are quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 6" href="/Chullin.6">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 6a–b</a>; there, the interpretation is that the innkeeper will try to please her guests and maybe substitute fresher produce or spices for him, whereas R. Yose takes a dim view of innkeepers and thinks that if she takes anything it is in order to steal.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, we are not responsible for the dishonest<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">If she steals and eats <i>ṭevel</i>, it is her own fault if she commits a deadly sin.</i>; he tithes only what he receives from her.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Both Rebbi Yose and Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Maӓser Šeni</i> 5:1 states that a vineyard in the fourth year (whose fruits may not be eaten without redemption) is signalized by lumps of earth placed around it, and any orchard in the first three years after planting (whose fruits are forbidden for any use) by lumps of clay. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel notes that this has to be done only in the Sabbatical year, when all growth is ownerless, but that in all other years, when taking from enclosed orchards and vineyards is theft, no signalizing is required since we are not responsible for the salvation of thieves.</i> said the same thing. Just as Rebbi Yose said, we are not responsible for the dishonest, so Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, we are not responsible for the dishonest. It is reasonable that Rebbi Yose will agree with Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, but Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel will not agree with Rebbi Yose. Rebbi Yose will agree with Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel that we are not responsible for the dishonest. But Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel will not agree with Rebbi Yose, because it is not fitting for a <i>ḥaver</i> that anything not in order should leave his house<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">This is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 32a" href="/Eruvin.32a">Babli (<i>Erubin</i> 32a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 9a" href="/Pesachim.9a"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 9a</a>) in the name of R. Ḥanina (there called R. Ḥanina from Khusistan.)</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>ḥaver</i> who married the daughter of an <i>am haäreẓ.</i></i> who gives to his mother-in-law tithes what he gives to her and what he receives from her because she is suspect to switch spoiled food<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">If something that the daughter cooks in her mother’s kitchen is spoiled, the mother will take from her own stores to produce a perfect meal for her son-in-law. R. Jehudah does not disagree with the first sentence; he just gives the reasoning behind the rule.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, she wants to put her daughter in a good light and is ashamed before her son-in-law. Rebbi Jehudah agrees that if he gives to his mother-in-law in the Sabbatical year she is not suspect of switching to feed her daughter from Sabbatical produce.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Joḥanan said, the first sentence also gives the opinion of Rebbi Jehudah. But the rabbis say, one who gives to his mother-in-law is like one who gives to his neighbor. As we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 4:31" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).4.31">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 4:31</a>, speaking of a <i>ḥaver</i> who gives something to bake or cook to his <i>am haäreẓ</i> neighbor. The Tosephta is a little more explicit, reading in the last sentence: “he worries about the sour dough and spices for the Sabbatical year and tithes,” not the foods he gave her.</i>: “He who gives bread to his neighbor to bake for him, or a dish to cook for him, has to worry neither about the Sabbatical year nor about tithes. That is, if he also gave her sour dough or spices. But if he did not give sour dough and spices, he worries about the Sabbatical year and about tithes.”
Does it mean his mother-in-law after the first<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 6:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.6.2.6"><i>Peah</i> Chapter 6, Note 46</a>.</i> or the second marriage ceremony? Let us hear from the following: “Rebbi Jehudah agrees that if he gives to his mother-in-law in the Sabbatical year she is not suspect of switching to feed her daughter from Sabbatical produce<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">In case the mother herself will not eat from Sabbatical produce used as merchandise.</i>.” Is there a difference between first and second marriage ceremony? Here also, there is no difference between first and second marriage ceremony.

Chapter 4



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who buys produce from a person who is not trustworthy for tithes and forgot to tithe them<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Friday afternoon, and now, on the Sabbath, he has no other produce to eat.</i> may ask the seller on the Sabbath and eats on the seller’s information<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Even though on weekdays, when tithing is possible, the seller’s information would not be trusted.</i>. At nightfall, when Sabbath ends, he may not eat until he tithed.<br>If he did not find the seller, but another person who is not trustworthy for tithes told him that it is tithed, he may eat on that information<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">On the Sabbath only.</i>. At nightfall when Sabbath ends, he may not eat until he tithed.<br>Heave of the tithe that returned to its origin<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">If heave falls into profane food of the same kind, the entire lot cannot be eaten except by Cohanim. If heave falls into <i>ṭevel</i>, in general the produce cannot be fixed since heave cannot be used to take heave for other produce. (If the heave which falls into profane food is less than .99%, something can be done.) All rules are given in <i>Terumot</i> 5. If heave of the tithe falls into the produce from which it was taken, there results a major complication and a potential great monetary loss since Cohanim will pay little for produce containing heave. If the heave should be impure, the entire batch would be a total loss since, in the case which is described in the Mishnah, the heave (1%) falls into the remainder (99%) and, therefore, is turning everything into <i>dema</i>‘. In that case, and since most <i>amē haäreẓ</i> are supposed to tithe, if the untrustworthy seller asserts that heave was taken, no heave fell into the produce and all is permitted for profane use.</i>. Rebbi Simeon from Shezur<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna, student of Rebbis Joshua, Eliezer, and Tarphon from lower Galilee, today’s village of Sejur.</i> said, even on weekdays he asks the seller and eats on his information.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: They permitted it because of the glory of the Sabbath. If it is because of the glory of the Sabbath, why does he have to ask him<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Since the seller is not trustworthy, his testimony is irrelevant. The answer is that some reason must be given to lift the strict rules of <i>demay</i> if those rules should be followed at all.</i>? By way of finding a cause. Rebbi Bibi in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: The fear of the Sabbath is on him<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">On the <i>am haäreẓ</i>, who will not desecrate the Sabbath.</i> and he will speak the truth. If the fear of the Sabbath is on him, why did we state that at nightfall, when the Sabbath ends, he may not eat until he tithed? Because of one who does not fear the Sabbath. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:1" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.1">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 5:1</a>.</i>: “If he asks him on weekdays, he may not eat on the Sabbath.” According to him who says that the fear of the Sabbath is on him, this is fine. According to him who says that it is because of the glory of the Sabbath; when he asks him on weekdays, should he not eat on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan’s argument seems weak. But in the next Halakhah, the tables are turned.</i>?
It was stated only for someone acting in error, not intentionally, as we have stated: If he forgot to tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The leniencies are only admitted if somebody actually forgot to prepare, not if it was an intentional effort to seek leniencies.</i>, if he had made no previous stipulation<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This is explained in Mishnah 7:1: If somebody is invited to eat at another person’s house and he does not trust his host with tithes, he can stipulate that after he returns home he will give tithes on what he ate there. While such a condition is forbidden for <i>ṭevel</i>, where heave can be given only from what clearly is in the tither’s possession, this kind of stipulation is permitted for <i>demay.</i> It is even permitted for one person to make the stipulation for a whole group of people eating together.</i>. But with a stipulation, one eats even on the stipulation by a friend.
May one eat on the question of another person? How is that understood?
