If two persons bought at the same time from him<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In the first two sentences, “he” and “him” always refer to the seller; in the last three to the buyer.</i>, if one of them asked him, the other may not eat until he tithed or also asked; I say he tithed one batch, but not the other. If one person bought from him two fig cakes and asked him about one of them, he may not eat from the second cake until he tithed or asked him; I say that he tithed one but not the other. At nightfall when Sabbath ends, he tithes from one on the other. According to him who says that it is because of the glory of the Sabbath, this is fine<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">We might assume that the leniencies expressed in the first two Mishnaiot apply only where the Sabbath enjoyment would be severely curtailed because nothing of the same kind is available in the buyer’s house. However, even if other produce of the same kind is available the taste of that particular bunch might be preferred.</i>. According to him who says that the fear of the Sabbath is on him, why should he be able to tithe from one on the other?
So far, if he has nothing of the same kind<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">We might assume that the leniencies expressed in the first two Mishnaiot apply only where the Sabbath enjoyment would be severely curtailed because nothing of the same kind is available in the buyer’s house. However, even if other produce of the same kind is available the taste of that particular bunch might be preferred.</i>. Even if he has of the same kind, that is a matter of preference.
A holiday next to the Sabbath, be it before the Sabbath or after the Sabbath, and likewise the two holidays of the diaspora, according to him who says they are one sanctity, he may eat; according to him who says that they are two sanctities, he may not eat<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody agrees that the sanctity of Sabbath is greater than the sanctity of holidays and that the sanctity of the Biblical first day of holidays is greater than that of the rabbinic second day of the diaspora. Here the question is whether these different degrees of sanctity fit together smoothly or whether there is a clean break between them even if that break has no temporal extension. The problem is discussed in <i>Erubin</i> 3, fol. 21b, and <i>Yom</i> <i>Tov</i> 1:1, fol. 60a, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 38a-39a" href="/Eruvin.38a-39a">Babli <i>Erubin</i> 38a–39a</a>, in connection with <i>eruv teḥumin</i> as explained in <i>Peah</i>, Chapter Eight, Note 56. The question is now whether one may make one <i>eruv</i> for the Sabbath and another one for another direction for the Sunday following which is a holiday. If the sanctities fit together smoothly, this is not possible. If there is a clean break, it is possible.</i>. Even according to him who says that they are two sanctities, he may eat as long as there was no possibility to tithe between them<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Even if it is possible to have two <i>eruvin</i>, it certainly is not possible to tithe. Hence, there is no legal “nightfall after the Sabbath” as envisaged by the Mishnah.</i>. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:1" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.1">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 5:1</a>.</i>: “If he asks him on the holiday, he eats on the Sabbath; if he asks him on the Sabbath, he eats on the holiday.” Rebbi Jonah in the name of Rebbi Zeïra, explain it regarding produce he had intended to use on Sabbath eve. But produce he had not intended on Sabbath eve (to use during the Sabbath) is not included<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">One cannot extend the validity of the query of an untrustworthy person beyond the original intent.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, the words of the rabbis support Rebbi Jonah, my father, since we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 4:4: If an <i>am haäreẓ</i> declares that under the penalties of breaking a vow he will have no relations with the other person, a <i>ḥaver</i>, unless that other person comes to his house to eat with him on the Sabbath but the <i>ḥaver</i> does not trust the <i>am</i> <i>haäreẓ</i> with the tithes, the <i>ḥaver</i> may eat at <i>am</i> <i>haäreẓ</i>’s the place the first Sabbath even though he is not trustworthy for tithes, on condition that he tell him that all is tithed. On the second Sabbath, even though he makes a vow to abstain from all benefit from him, he shall not eat until he tithed. {Since tithing must be done before the Sabbath, it seems that the question also must be asked before the Sabbath.} This somewhat contrasts with Mishnah 7:1: “One who is invited by a person whom he does not trust with tithes says on Friday afternoon: Anything that I shall in the future separate for what I am going to eat shall be First and Second Tithes. The First Tithe shall become the heave of the tithe and the Second Tithe shall be redeemed with coins.” Hence, it is possible to tithe mentally under the right circumstances. In the case of a holiday following a Sabbath, if one is mentally prepared, one may transfer the permission of Sabbath to the holiday.</i>: “He who takes a vow regarding his friend that he will eat at his place but he does not trust him with the tithes.” Rebbi Yannai ben Rebbi Ismaël in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: On the Sabbath of the preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">πρωτογαμία, ἡ, Greek word in a Latin inscription from Carthage, “the Jewish celebration of the preliminary marriage” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 6:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.6.2.6"><i>Peah</i>, Chapter 6, Note 46</a>). A big meal was given for the community on the Sabbath preceding קידושין and not to appear would have been an insult to the families of the young couple. The invited guest could retroactively tithe what he ate, according to Mishnah 7:1. If he forgot, he could rely on the assertion of the untrustworthy.<br>In medieval Germany, the festivity was called <i>spinholz</i> (from Italian <i>sposalizio</i>).</i> they permitted because of hatred. Is the Sabbath of the preliminary marriage not comparable to produce that on Sabbath eve he intended to use (later on the Sabbath)<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Since he already knew on Friday that he had to attend.</i>? Nevertheless, he<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan said, if it were not necessary for peace in the community, the rabbis would not have permitted to accept the word of an untrustworthy person. Similarly, the permission to use on holidays the assertion of tithes by an untrustworthy person given on the Sabbath must be restricted to food also intended for the Sabbath.</i> only said “because of hatred.”
If he invited him the first Sabbath but he did not come, the second time he should not eat unless he tithes or asks him<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 4:4 states that on the first Sabbath he actually eats there he may rely on his host’s word. But the second time around, the untrustworthy person’s word is not good enough. Why is it accepted here since actually it is now the second time that the guest asks about tithing? The answer is that the first question did not concern him personally since he was somehow prevented from attending the meal.</i>. Did he not already ask him? Rebbi Jonah said, there he asked for himself. But here a third party asked about it.
Rebbi Jonah asked: May he ask him in a roundabout way? Rebbi Jonah asks in this way. Rebbi Jonah bought wheat from Bar Ḥaqulah. He went to him and said, not that I suspect you, but since I bought wheat from you and I saw a multitude<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ὄχλος “multitude, troups” (<i>Berakhot</i>, p. 612).</i> with you, I said maybe you forgot to put them in order. Were they put in order? He complained strongly about him and said: Why do you suspect me?
It was stated: “Heave of the tithe from <i>demay</i> that returned to its origin makes <i>dema</i>‘<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Dema</i>‘ (a word of unclear etymology, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:28" href="/Exodus.22.28"><i>Ex.</i>22:28</a>) describes profane produce mixed with heave, which is unfit for human consumption if heave and food are impure, and is food only for Cohanim and their families if both the heave and the food are pure. It can be put in order only if the amount of heave in the total is less than 1 in 101. Since heave from <i>demay</i> is genuinely holy only in a minority of cases, that heave does not necessarily create <i>dema</i>‘. (Maimonides <i>Maäser</i> 12:4 and R. Abraham ben David ad <i>loc</i>.)</i>, not to its origin does not make <i>dema</i>‘. Rebbi Simeon from Shezur said, both at its origin and not at its origin it does not make <i>dema</i>‘.” Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked before Rebbi Zeïra: For one who says, it makes <i>dema</i>‘, it makes <i>dema</i>‘; for one who says, it does not make <i>dema</i>‘, it does not make <i>dema</i>‘; this is reasonable<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">That means, I can understand R. Simeon from Shezur, that we always follow the majority of cases and, therefore, heave from <i>demay</i> cannot create <i>dema</i>‘. I also could understand the position that, once the name of heave was attached to any produce, it must be treated like heave in all respects.</i>. But if one says, in its origin it makes <i>dema</i>‘, not in its origin it does not make <i>dema</i>‘; what is the difference between in its origin and not in its origin? Rebbi Ḥaggai came up. They said, this one will say, “by Moses, I shall explain the reason<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ḥaggai’s standard answer to questions of this type.</i>.” He said, by Moses, I shall explain the reason: He who says, it makes <i>dema</i>‘, because it permits the rest to be eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Since without tithing, <i>demay</i> may not be eaten by rabbinic ordinance, it should in this respect be treated like heave taken according to Biblical precept.</i>. He who says, it does not make <i>dema</i>‘, because it does not permit the rest to be eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For other produce, it simply is food of questionable status.</i>. Rebbi La taught according to that of Rebbi Ḥaggai<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">He declares practice to follow the anonymous Tanna in the <i>baraita</i>.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra said: A case came before Rebbi Ḥanina and he taught according to Rebbi Simeon from Shezur. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: Practice does not follow Rebbi Simeon from Shezur<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 75b" href="/Chullin.75b">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 75b</a>, both R. Ḥanina and R. Jonathan endorse the ruling of R. Simeon from Shezur as valid practice.</i>.
“In addition, Rebbi Simeon from Shezur said: If he separated the heave of the tithe and it was burned, he asks the seller and eats on his information<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:2" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.2">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 5:2</a>: “Heave of the tithe which returned to its origin. Rebbi Simeon from Shezur said, even on weekdays he asks the seller and eats on his information. In addition, Rebbi Simeon from Shezur said: If he separated heave and tithes and the produce was stolen, he asks the seller and eats what he separated on his information, because just as the fear of the Sabbath is on the <i>am haäreẓ</i>, so the fear of <i>dema</i>‘ is on him.” The different <i>baraita</i> here has to be interpreted in the same way, viz., that the main body of produce was burnt and he was left only with heave and tithes. Because of the monetary loss, he was permitted to treat heave and tithes as profane food on the untrustworthy seller’s word.</i>.” Did Rebbi Zeïra not say that a similar case came before Rebbi Ḥanina and he taught according to Rebbi Simeon from Shezur? Rebbi Avin said, this question does not compare to the first question. There it was, not that I suspect you, but since I bought wheat from you yesterday and I saw a multitude with you, I said maybe you forgot to put them in order. Were they put in order? But here, because I suspected you, I put them in order but they went up in fire. Were they put in order<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">How can he expect to get an honest answer if the question is an insult to the seller?</i>? Rebbi Samuel, the son of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">An Amora of the fifth generation, student of his father and of R. Nasa, also mentioned together with Rebbi Mattaniah.</i> said that Bar Kappara stated: The fear of <i>dema</i>‘ is on him and he will tell the truth<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Since everybody knows how bad the consequences of <i>dema</i>‘ are, even if he himself is only aware that he must separate the first heave, he will not be a party to such a grave sin. It follows that the Tosephta before us is in the tradition of Bar Kappara, and that it is part of the original statement of R. Simeon from Shezur.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">See Note 18. Even though Mishnah 2:2 stated that the trustworthy person may not eat at an <i>am haäreẓ</i>’s place, in our case when the human relationship is endangered, when the <i>am haäreẓ</i> vowed to cease all relations with the trustworthy person unless he accepts his invitation, and it is on a Sabbath, when the fear of the Sabbath is on the <i>am haäreẓ</i>, an exception was made. But the following Sabbaths, he may eat only if either the <i>am haäreẓ</i> tithes before him or he tithes the <i>am haäreẓ’</i> s produce.</i> who takes a vow concerning his friend that he should eat at his place but he does not trust him with the tithes, he may eat at his place the first Sabbath even though he is not trustworthy for tithes, on condition that he tell him that all is tithed. On the second Sabbath, even though he makes a vow to refuse any benefit from him, he shall not eat until he tithed.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Yannai ben Rebbi Ismaël in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: On the Sabbath of a <i>protogamia</i><sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">See Note 19.</i> they permitted because of hatred. Rebbi Avin said, here they permitted <i>ṭevel</i> because of human relations. Rebbi Ḥanina said, Rebbi Jeremiah asked: If it is because of human relations, why is it only on condition that the host tell him that it was tithed<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since probably the question about tithes will offend the host. No answer is given.</i>? If he invited him on the first Sabbath but he did not come, can the second Sabbath become the first<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">This refers back to the Mishnah, not to the <i>protogamia</i>. The question was answered in Halakhah 2.</i>? Rav Ḥisda said, here we have learned that a <i>ḥaver</i> may not eat at a meal that has no name<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>ḥaver</i> may not eat at a meal offered by an <i>am haäreẓ</i> except at a particular religious festivity, such as the <i>protogamia</i>. The statement refers to the pronouncement of R. Yannai, not the Mishnah. A similar statement, in the name of the Tanna R. Simeon, is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 49a" href="/Pesachim.49a">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i>49a</a>, with a different thrust since the Babli, in contrast to Mishnah and Yerushalmi, frowns upon marriages between <i>ḥaverim</i> and <i>amē haäreẓ</i>. The restrictive statement here also is from a Babylonian author.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Eliezer says, nobody has to give a name to the tithe of the poor from <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The tithe of the poor is due in years 3 and 6 of the Sabbatical cycle. Since<i>demay</i> is only <i>ṭevel</i> by doubt, no poor man can go to court and sue to collect the tithe. The tithe of the poor is only a civil obligation and no sanctity is attached to it. R. Eliezer says that in years 3 and 6 the tithe is nonexistent for <i>demay</i> but the Sages, according to Maimonides in his Mishnah Commentary, hold that in order to protect the separation and redemption of Second Tithe in other years, one has at least to indicate that the tithe of the poor, if given, would have to be at a fixed part of the stored produce (e. g., at the Northern or Southern end.)</i>. But the Sages say, he has to give a name but does not have to separate. He who gave a name<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">If he said, for example, that the heave of the tithe should be the Northernmost 1% of the produce, or the heave of the poor the 10% next to a given corner. If this is not done, nothing at all from that produce can be eaten on the Sabbath.</i> to his heave of the tithe of <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The heave of the tithe for certain tevel must be given by the Levite receiving the First Tithe, not the farmer. In contrast, the heave of the poor is given only from produce that is certain.</i>, or to the certain tithe of the poor, should not separate them<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simson of Sens proves from Mishnah 7:1, and it is also clear from the next paragraph, that once a name is given to heave and tithes, the actual handling of it on the Sabbath is permitted. What is questionable is taking it and handing it over to the recipients since transfer of property on the Sabbath is not permitted.</i> on the Sabbath. But if a Cohen or a poor person were used to eat from his food at his table, they may come and eat it<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">He and they sit at the same table and eat from the same produce on condition that Cohen or poor eat from the corner that was declared to be their part. In that way, the produce is no longer <i>demay</i> or <i>tevel</i> and the owner may eat simultaneously with his guests as long as the guests know that they are eating heave or tithe.</i> on condition that he inform them.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Abba bar Huna<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, he is called Rabba bar Rav Huna, son of the Amora Rav Huna, and fourth head of the Yeshivah at Sura founded by Rav. In the Midrash (<i>Ekhah rabbati</i> on <i>Thr.</i> 1:3), this tradition is ascribed to Rav Bibi and Rav Huna. The title is incorrect; it should be רב but because of the elided א in the name Abba the text cannot be emended.</i> in the name of Rav: He who eats his produce without having separated the tithe of the poor commits a deadly sin<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 16b" href="/Makkot.16b">Babli, <i>Makkot</i> 16b</a>, Rav is quoted as saying that such a person is whipped, implying that it is not a deadly sin.</i>. What is the reason of Rebbi Eliezer? Because he<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The farmer knows that not separating the tithe of the poor makes his food a deadly affair; he will separate it. (Since most farmers consider themselves poor, he probably will eat the tithe himself. If he is not actually poor he has overstepped a positive commandment, not a prohibition.) As a consequence, <i>demay</i> in the years of the tithe of the poor will not contain that tithe. Such an argument were impossible if we would read “Second Tithe” in the statement attributed to Rav.</i> knows that it would be a deadly sin, he will separate it. What is the reason of the rabbis? He only has to give a name but does not have to separate it<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, he is called Rabba bar Rav Huna, son of the Amora Rav Huna, and fourth head of the Yeshivah at Sura founded by Rav. In the Midrash (<i>Ekhah rabbati</i> on <i>Thr.</i> 1:3), this tradition is ascribed to Rav Bibi and Rav Huna. The title is incorrect; it should be רב but because of the elided א in the name Abba the text cannot be emended.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Maäser Šeni</i> 3:6: “If somebody was seen separating the Second Tithe, he is trustworthy for the First Tithe, the words of R. Eliezer. But the Sages say, if he is trustworthy for the First, he is trustworthy for the Second; he who is trustworthy for the Second is not trustworthy for the First. If he separated heave, First and Second Tithes, and ate from it, he is trustworthy for that kind of food but not for any other kind, the words of Rebbi Eliezer. But the Sages say, even for that kind he is not trustworthy {unless he passes the formal tests of trustworthiness}; you cannot trust him for he might put in order for himself but leads others astray {by eating kosher produce but selling <i>ṭevel.</i>}”<br>The text here makes little sense. It seems that the first two words of the next clause are dittography. The implication is that the formal induction into <i>ḥaverut</i>, described in Chapter 2, does solve all problems in theory but not in practice since many people will be reluctant to wear a badge that proclaims their superiority over fellow Jews. Hence, we need some guidelines to recognize people who follow the rules without ostentation.</i>: He is trustworthy if they saw him separate the Second Tithe. It was stated: For the Second, he is trustworthy for the First, the words of Rebbi Eliezer. But the Sages say, if he trustworthy for the First, he is trustworthy for the Second. He who is trustworthy for the Second is not trustworthy for the First. What is the reason of Rebbi Eliezer?<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Yerushalmi discusses R. Eliezer but not the Sages, it endorses R. Eliezer’s stand in principle.</i> Is it because he is not suspected of advancing the tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 1:4:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.1.4.6">Chapter 1, Note 208</a>.</i>, or because if he separated the Second it is a standing presumption that he separated the First<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Since not separating the heave of the tithe is much more severe a transgression than not separating the Second Tithe.</i>? But did we not state: “Rebbi Eliezer says, nobody has to give a name to the tithe of the poor from <i>demay.</i>” Hence, for the Second it is needed<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eliezer certainly agrees that separating the Second Tithe is part of the procedure needed to fix <i>demay.</i></i>. Since he has to separate the Second, on which the owner earns no goodwill<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Each time he redeems Second Tithe, he loses a peruta's worth of value in the coin set aside for these redemptions. If all perutot in the coin are used up, he has to destroy it.</i> he certainly will separate the First, on which he earns goodwill<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">While he has to give the First Tithe to a Levite or a Cohen (at least as long as they had documentary proof that their male ancesters were admitted to service in the Temple), the choice of the person who receives his tithes is his and it is perfectly legitimate to use the First Tithe and the {pure} heave of the tithe to cement friendships and commercial ties.</i>!
“If somebody brought out Second Tithe from his house to somebody and said: ‘It is redeemed,’ he is trustworthy; ‘redeem for me and yourself,’<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">R. Abraham ben David (Maimonides, <i>Maäser</i> 12:5) reads פדוי לי ופדוי לכם “it is redeemed for me and is redeemed for you,” interpreted to mean “it is redeemed following my standards and I hope that is good enough for you.” He declares himself not to follow generally accepted standards and makes himself untrustworthy. The classical commentators follow this reading.</i> he is not trustworthy<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In the second case, when he says, redeem it for me, it is clear that Second Tithe was not given. Hence, if it is spelled out that he is not trustworthy, it implies that he is not trustworthy for First Tithe and the heave of the tithe. It follows that the first “trustworthy” has exactly the same meaning and in the first case he is declared to be trustworthy for First because he is believed to be trustworthy for Second.</i>.” The <i>baraita</i> is Rebbi Eliezer’s, since Rebbi Eliezer said, he who is trustworthy for the Second is trustworthy for the First. Rebbi Yose said, everybody agrees, they made it like additions to First Fruits<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">First Fruits are presented to the Temple and eaten there by the Cohen under the laws of purity of sacrifices. First Fruits are chosen when the farmer sees a first fruit forming in his orchard; then he binds a string of bast around that twig to recognize the fruit later. If he thinks that it is shabby to bring just one fruit to the Temple, he will add other fruits of the same kind to his basket. The First Fruit itself, as a sacrifice, is exempt from all heaves and tithes; the additions are not. But if the Cohen in the Temple asks the <i>am haäreẓ</i> farmer whether the additional fruits are tithed, he is by law required to believe what the farmer tells him (Maimonides <i>Maäser</i> 13:14).</i>. Just as additions to First Fruits are eaten in purity and are free from <i>demay</i>, so this is eaten as Second Tithe and is free from the First. Rebbi Ḥananiah confirmed in the name of Rebbi Issi<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">R. Assi.</i>: it is Rebbi Eliezer’s<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">There is no support in any Tannaitic material for R. Yose’s creation of a new category of fruits exempt from <i>demay.</i> Hence, the <i>baraita</i> is R. Eliezer’s and the Sages make trustworthiness dependent exclusively on the rules given in Chapter 2.</i>.
This is the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">A clarification of what the Mishnah means; cf. J. N. Epstein, מבוא לנוסח המשנה, 2nd ed., p. 445.</i>: He should not hand them over on the Sabbath, since we have stated there: “The House of Shammai say that one does not bring <i>ḥallah</i> and <i>gifts</i><sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The parts of a profane, slaughtered animal that should be given to the Cohen (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 18:3" href="/Deuteronomy.18.3"><i>Deut.</i> 18:3</a>).</i> to the Cohen on a holiday, whether they were separated the day before or on the day itself, but the House of Hillel do permit this.” The first statement implies the second, the second implies the first. The first statement implies the second; there is no difference between holiday and Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">There is an obvious difference between holiday and Sabbath in that on holidays one is permitted to bake, hence to create <i>ḥallah</i>, and to slaughter, hence to create new gifts for the Cohen. But property may not be transferred at all according to the House of Shammai, and according to the House of Hillel only in a roundabout way, when the Cohen eats his part at the table of the giver.</i>. The second implies the first; that is, if they<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Cohen and poor person eating at the householder’s Sabbath table. They may eat unseparated tithe or heave only if they are regular guests and the householder knows that they will come.</i> are used to. But if they are not used to, no person comes<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">The text has two synonyms, סלק “comes up,” על “ascends.”</i> to eat his piece of bread at another’s house.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 7:8" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).7.8">Tosephta <i>Bava Qama</i> 7:8</a>:“There are seven kinds of thieves and the worst of them is the thief of opinons: He who importunes another person to be his guest while he knows that the other will not accept, he who offers many gifts while he knows that the other will not accept, he who opens a new barrel for a guest when from its contents he will sell to a grocer, etc.” In these cases, the person invited, offered gifts, or wine from a brand new barrel, thinks that the other party is ready to go to real expense on his behalf when in reality the pretending giver knows that it will cost him nothing. This paragraph is added because in the Mishnah, Cohen and poor have to be informed that they are eating heave and tithe which is forbidden to their host.</i>: “One should not importune anybody to be his guest if he knows that the other person will not accept. He should not offer many gifts of closeness if he knows that the other person will not accept.” What are gifts of closeness? He knows that the other person expects that he would exert himself for him. In Jerusalem one turned his clasp<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel text, <i>Avodah zarah</i> 1:3, fol. 39a, an unexplained word עיכלה is used, in <i>Ekhah rabbati</i>4(2) יד אונקלי שלו. The commentary מתנות כהונה to <i>Ekhah rabbati</i> indicates that in his Yerushalmi manuscript the word is פיבליה, identified by A. Kohut as Latin <i>fibula</i>, “clasp” to hold the toga together. The Yerushalmi spelling corresponds to the late Greek form φίβλα. Kohut conjectures that on the way to a dinner one wore the clasp inverted as a sign that no other invitation could be accepted. [Babylonian sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Berakhot (Lieberman) 4:9" href="/Tosefta_Berakhot_(Lieberman).4.9">Tosephta <i>Berakhot</i> 4:9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 93b" href="/Bava_Batra.93b"><i>Baba batra</i> 93b</a>) deal with preparations of the host, not the guest, for the dinner. The word appearing here, פילכיה “his distaff, spindle” does not fit.]</i> from left to right.
It was stated: “One does not carry to the mourner’s house in colored glass containers because he carries a false load<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">After a burial, the mourners may not prepare a meal for themselves but must receive their first meal from the neighbors. The Babli (<i>Moëd qaṭan</i>27a) prescribes that all food be brought in simple baskets woven from leaves of the date palm (so that the poor also could take part in this good deed); the <i>baraita</i> there must be of Babylonian origin. Colored glass is frowned upon because is looks expensive but may contain very little food since it is opaque.</i>.”
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 3:13-14" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).3.13-14">Tosephta <i>Giṭṭin</i> 3:13–14</a>, partially quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 61a" href="/Gittin.61a">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 61a</a>. Since a Jewish community is required to have organized charity, if the Gentiles do not have similar services, the Jewish social services must be offered also to the Gentiles to advance intercommunal relations. The paragraph is inserted here probably from the parallel text in <i>Avodah zarah</i>1:3, since the next paragraph is dependent on this one and belongs to <i>Avodah zarah.</i></i>: “In a town where Gentiles and Jews live together, the overseers of charity collect from Jews and Gentiles and provide for Jewish and Gentile poor, visit Jewish and Gentile sick, bury Jewish and Gentile dead, and console Gentile and Jewish mourners. Also, one takes in vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">If one finds a usable vessel that has some distinguishing mark, if it was lost by a Jew it is the duty of a fellow Jew who finds it to take it with him, advertise for the loser, and return it (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:1-3" href="/Deuteronomy.22.1-3"><i>Deut.</i> 22:1–3</a>). This is extended to vessels lost by Gentiles for the sake of intercommunal peace.</i> from Gentiles and from Jews for the sake of peaceful coexistence.”
The weavers asked Rebbi Ammi, what about a Gentile wedding feast<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Whether one may be a guest at such a celebration.</i>? He wanted to permit it to them from this: “Because of peaceful coexistence.” Rebbi Abba told them, did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 1:2" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.1.2">Tosephta <i>Avodah zarah</i> 1:4</a> it is noted that one may not trade with a Gentile on the day of his wedding or the day he was appointed to a post in local government (since on these occasions religious ceremonies are unavoidable.)</i> that the holiday of a Gentile wedding feast is forbidden. Rebbi Ammi said, if it had not been for Rebbi Ba, we would have come to permit their strange worship<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., idolatry.</i>; praised be He Who distanced us from them.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who tells somebody who is not trustworthy, buy for me from somebody who is trustworthy, or from somebody who tithes, is<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The untrustworthy person who was sent to buy produce. In such a case, the store from which he has to buy must be specified, otherwise all he bought is <i>demay.</i> If the untrustworthy emissary did not execute his job to the letter, the produce he bought is still <i>demay.</i></i> not trustworthy; from X, he is trustworthy. If he went to buy from him and said, I did not find him, but I bought for you from somebody who is trustworthy, he is not trustworthy.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:3" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.3">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 5:3</a>; the discussion here in the Halakhah is part of the statement: “He who says to somebody who is not trustworthy, buy for me from somebody who is trustworthy, or from somebody who tithes, is not trustworthy; from X, he is trustworthy. Rebbi Yose {the Tanna, ben Ḥalaphta} says, he is not trustworthy; maybe he found a source that is closer. Rebbi Yose agrees that if he said, go, eat the loaf, and I shall pay for it, go, drink a glass, and I shall pay for it, he is trustworthy.”</i>: “Rebbi Yose said, even if he specified, from X, he is not trustworthy, unless he said, you buy and I will pay.” What is the reason of Rebbi Yose? I say that he found someone close by and bought from him, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.4.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 6:6</a>. A bill of divorce is not valid until it is delivered into the hand of the wife (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:3" href="/Deuteronomy.24.3">3</a>). However, the wife can appoint an agent and the hand of a duly appointed agent is like the hand of the principal. If she is the wife of a Cohen, she may eat heave as long as she is his wife but not when she is a divorcee. Hence, the exact moment when she is divorced is identical with the exact moment in which the wife of a Cohen may no longer eat heave.</i>: “Receive my bill of divorce at such and such a place; she may eat heave until the bill of divorce reaches that place. Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eleazar ben Shamua‘, one of the last students of R. Aqiba, discussed in the introduction to <i>Berakhot</i>. He is the authority for bills of divorce.</i> prohibits her immediately.” What is the reason of Rebbi Eleazar? I say that he found it behind the door. It turns out that Rebbi Eleazar is like Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Yose is like Rebbi Eleazar. Rebbi Yose says more than Rebbi Eleazar; does not Rebbi Eleazar agree that if she says to him, do not accept it except at such and such a place<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">If the authorization contains such a restriction, the commission of the agent is not valid outside that place, and if the agent receives the bill of divorce at any other place she is not divorced.</i>, that she continues eating until the bill of divorce reaches that place? But here, even if he says, only from X, he is not trustworthy, except if he said, you buy and I will pay.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If someone enters a town, does not know anybody there, and asks: Who is trustworthy here, who tithes? If somebody told him, I am not trustworthy, but Mr. X is trustworthy, he may be believed<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the speaker himself is not trustworthy, in this case he may be believed, as explained in the Halakhah.</i>. If he went and bought there and asked, who is selling old produce here<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of “old produce” is difficult to establish. The traditional explanation, represented by Maimonides (<i>Maäser</i> 12:7). R. Abraham ben David, and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Ketubot 56b" href="/Rashi_on_Ketubot.56b">Rashi (<i>Ketubot</i> 56b</a>) is that the buyer is looking for old (better quality) wine and is ready to buy <i>demay</i> and tithe it if only he gets the quality desired. The interpretation of the Mishnah would be that the first informant, who directed the buyer to the second one’s store, was not an uninterested bystander, but in effect was indirectly paid for steering the stranger to the second one’s store. The plural “they may be believed” in the last sentence then would include the seller who asserted to the stranger that he was trustworthy by the rules. His trustworthiness is not invalidated by the kickback.<br>R. Simson of Sens refers “old produce” to that grown before the Sabbatical year if the question was asked in the Sabbatical. But the question here is about tithes, not the Sabbatical. Usually, “old” refers to grain that started to grow before the last Passover, since “new” grain, summer wheat planted after Passover, may not be eaten until after the next Passover (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:14" href="/Leviticus.23.14"><i>Lev.</i> 23:14</a>). But Rashi (<i>loc. cit</i>.) shows that this notion cannot apply here and summer wheat cannot be grown in the Land of Israel.</i>? If the seller said, the one who sent you to me, they may be believed even though they act as if they would do reciprocal favors to each other.<br>Donkey drivers who entered a town and one of them said, my produce is new, my colleague’s is old<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">All commentators agree that the meaning of “old” and “new” here is identical with the meaning in Mishnah 7.</i>, mine is not in order, my colleague’s is in order, are not to be believed<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Since they come in a group, they had ample time to devise a strategy by which each one of them gets the business of a specified place.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, they are to be believed.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> But is a single witness trustworthy<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Since the informant himself is not trustworthy, should not his information be treated as testimony which is invalid unless corroborated by two witnesses. The explanation of R. Joḥanan is ascribed to Rava (Rav Abba bar Rav Joseph bar Ḥama of the fourth generation) in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 56b" href="/Ketubot.56b">Babli (<i>Ketubot</i> 56b</a>).</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan said, they were very lenient for a guest because of the necessities of life.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:4" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.4">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 5:4</a>: “He who enters a town with heave in his hand, does not know anybody there, [or stands by a threshing floor with heave in his hand] {and wants to give the heave to a trustworthy Cohen,} and does not know anybody there, may ask either <i>ḥaverim</i> or <i>amē haäreẓ</i>, the words of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel. Rebbi says, about heave one may only ask a <i>ḥaver</i>.” One has to interpret the <i>baraita</i> in the Yerushalmi similarly, that the question is about heave. But the interpretation of the Yerushalmi, given by R. Jonah, is the opposite, that the stranger wants to know whether he may take without worry, not to whom to give.<br>The phrase in brackets is not in the Vienna ms. of the Tosephta and R. S. Liebermann explains this as scribal omission. However, while the thrust of the Tosephta is not that of the Yerushalmi, a Yerushalmi version would not have the phrase, or does not have the phrase, as asserted by R. Jonah at the end of this paragraph. The Vienna scribe probably did not make an error here.</i>: “He who enters a town and does not know anybody there, or stands by a threshing floor and does not know anybody there, may ask either a <i>ḥaver</i> or an <i>am haäreẓ</i>, the words of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel. Rebbi says, about heave one only may ask a <i>ḥaver.</i>” It turns out that Rebbi follows Rebbi Meïr and Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel follows his own reasoning. As we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bekhorot 5:4" href="/Mishnah_Bekhorot.5.4">Mishnah <i>Bekhorot</i> 5:4</a>. The firstborn of a cow, sheep, or goat must be given to a Cohen. If it is without blemish, it is dedicated as sacrifice from birth. If it develops a defect, the Cohen may slaughter and eat it at home (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:19-23" href="/Deuteronomy.15.19-23"><i>Deut.</i> 15:19–23</a>). Hence, the Cohen has a monetary interest in finding a defect in such a firstborn.</i>: “About any bodily defects that may be caused by humans, laymen shepherds are trustworthy, Cohen shepherds are not. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, he is trustworthy about another’s flock but not his own. Rebbi Meïr said, whoever is suspect<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">And this includes absolutely all Cohanim in matters regarding firstborn animals.</i> in a matter may neither judge nor testify about it.” Rebbi Jonah said, they disagree only if it is a matter of taking, but in distributing even Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel agrees. What is the difference between taking and distributing? A distribution is public knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Since a distribution of tithes is publicly announced, even the stranger would find enough information about the person who is making the distribution.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:5" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.5">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 5:5</a> again has a thrust opposed to the <i>baraita</i> of the Yerushalmi: “If he was standing in a group of people and asked, who here is trustworthy, who here tithes? If one person told him that he himself was trustworthy, he is not trustworthy, about another one that he tithes, he is trustworthy. If they told him that this one tithes, in his presence he is not trustworthy; but in his absence, he is trustworthy; in a court of law, he is trustworthy.” The question asked against the thesis of R. Jonah clearly refers to the last clause of the Tosephta, not the <i>baraita</i> quoted in the text.</i>: “If somebody entered a town, found there groups of people, and asked, who is trustworthy here, who tithes here? If one person told him that he himself was trustworthy, and the others told him that he was trustworthy, that one is trustworthy. If they told him in the person’s presence that he was not trustworthy, he is not trustworthy; but in his absence, he is trustworthy.” Rebbi Jonah said, if that somebody was from that town, the group must be of <i>ḥaverim</i>, if he is a stranger, the group may be composed of <i>amē haäreẓ</i>. It is difficult for Rebbi Yose, you say that <i>ḥaverim</i> are not trustworthy in his presence<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">As noted above, this refers to the Tosephta or a similar text, when somebody is declared trustworthy only if others declare him so in his absence. Does one suspect <i>ḥaverim</i> to lie in this matter to please somebody? The question is not answered.</i>?
What is the reason of Rebbi Jehudah? Because of the lives of the townspeople<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Donkey drivers should be encouraged to visit the place to increase the supply of foodstuffs While we have translated עיר as “town,” it is often used in the sense of “hamlet.”</i>. What is the reason of the rabbis? They can provision themselves in another town.

Chapter 5



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who buys from the baker<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Who is not trusted for tithes and whose bread is <i>demay</i>. The seeming contradiction of this Mishnah and Mishnah 2:4 is discussed in Halakhah 2:4 and referred to again in the Halakhah here.</i>, how does he tithe? He takes the heave of the tithe and <i>ḥallah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Heave of the tithe is 1%; <i>ḥallah</i>, if it can be eaten in ritual purity, has a rabbinic ratio of 2.0833% = 1/48. Today <i>ḥallah</i> is a symbolic little piece. All must be designated (but should not actually be separated) before the declaration.</i> and says: One percent here on that side is tithe, the rest of the tithe is adjacent to it. That which I turned into tithe is made heave of the tithe for it and the rest is <i>ḥallah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The rest of first tithe does not have to be given or separated.</i>. The Second Tithe is North (or South) of it and is redeemed by coins<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 4, Note 56.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Also <i>ḥallah</i>? Is <i>ḥallah</i> due for <i>demay</i>? Did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 1:3. The opinions of Rebbis Joḥanan and Eleazar are detailed in Halakhah 1:3.</i>: “<i>Ḥallah</i> of the unobservant and food containing heave are free from <i>demay</i>.” Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya, in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: This is from the House of Shammai. We have stated accordingly<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Not in other Tannaitic sources. But in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 1:28" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).1.28">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 1:28</a> we read: “Replacements for heave and its fifth {from a layman who ate heave in error, who has to replace it together with a 25% fine, measured from below, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:16" href="/Leviticus.5.16"><i>Lev.</i>5:16</a>}, the fifths of the fifth {if the layman then took the fifth by error, he has to restitute it with an additional fifth, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:26" href="/Leviticus.5.26"><i>Lev.</i> 5:26</a>}, the leftover of the ‘<i>omer</i> sacrifice, the two breads {leavened breads for the priests, offered on Pentecost}, the show breads, the leftovers of flour sacrifices, and additions to First Fruits; Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah says in the name of Rebbi Simeon that the House of Shammai obligate but the House of Hillel exempt.” It is difficult to make coherent sense of this Tosephta (cf. תוספתא כפשוטה p. 206–207), but the general tenor is that the House of Shammai treat as <i>demay</i> anything that may be food for human consumption.</i>: “Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah says in the name of Rebbi Simeon that the House of Shammai obligate for <i>ḥallah</i> and the House of Hillel exempt it.” Did the House of Shammai include seed goods? But he who buys for sowing or animal feed, flour for tanning or oil for lighting, is he not free from <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 1:3, accepted also by the House of Shammai.</i>? But it must be for somebody who kneads his dough with fruit juice<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Pure fruit juice, other than grape juice and olive oil, without a drop of water added, is not one of the fluids that make food ready to become ritually impure. Also, dough made with fruit juice is really cake dough, not bread dough, and therefore should not be subject to <i>ḥallah.</i> However, the Mishnah, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 2:1:17-2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.2.1.17-2.3"><i>Ḥallah</i> 2:4</a>, declares that dough made with fruit juice is subject to <i>ḥallah</i> even though it cannot become impure. [Maimonides, in his Code (<i>Bikkurim</i> 6:12), not in his Commentary to the Mishnah, restricts the obligation of <i>ḥallah</i> to dough made with grape juice and olive oil; he follows the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 2:1:7-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.2.1.7-10">Yerushalmi <i>Ḥallah</i> 2:2</a> which seems to indicate that practice does not follow the Mishnah.] If we follow the Mishnah in <i>Ḥallah</i>, we should require the person who buys such bread from the baker to separate <i>ḥallah</i> in order to make public the obligation to give <i>ḥallah</i> from such “bread.”</i>. Did not Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina say that this follows Rebbi Eleazar ben Jehudah from Bartota<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ṭebul Yom</i> 3:4: “Dough prepared with some fluid {i. e., that came into contact with some of the fluids that prepare food to become impure, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1">Chapter 2, Notes 136–137</a>} and was kneaded with fruit juice {other than grape juice or olive oil} and was touched by a person who went to the ritual bath that day {but will be pure for heave and sacrifices only at sundown (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:7" href="/Leviticus.22.7"><i>Lev.</i> 22:7</a>)}, R. Eleazar ben Jehudah from Bartota said in the name of R. Joshua that he made all of it unfit; R. Aqiba said in his {R. Joshua’s} name that he made {heave} unfit {for consumption) only at the place he touched.” Since practice follows R. Aqiba in his dispute with a single opponent, practice must be that fruit juice does not transmit impurity and, hence, does not impart impurity to <i>ḥallah</i>.<br>R. Eleazar ben Jehudah from Bartota, Tanna of the third generation, was a student of R. Joshua and teacher of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel. The name Bartota has been tentatively identified in Upper Galilee.. R. Eleazar was known to give to charity more than he could afford, so that the administrators of charity tried to avoid being seen by him.</i>, hence it does not follow the rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since our Mishnah is anonymous, it is difficult to accept that it represents an opinion that clearly is not the practice.</i>. But it agrees with Rebbi Joḥanan, since Rebbi Joḥanan said<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In Halakhah 1:3, R. Joḥanan says that, if the baker hires a <i>ḥaver</i> to knead his dough in purity, he will also be careful with heave and tithes, and the baker should tithe. If he prepares his dough in impurity, the buyer should tithe, and this is the case here. R. Eleazar holds that the Mishnah means a wholesaler, who does not have to tithe, but Mishnah 1:3 deals either with a retail baker or a private person baking for his family and guests.</i>, one case for him who works in purity and one case for him who works in impurity. But what following Rebbi Eleazar, since Rebbi Eleazar said, in both cases if he works in purity? It must be that he is a guest. Was it not stated<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in Halakhah 2:2 together with the following sentences, <i>qq. v</i>.</i>: “He who is trustworthy for purities is trustworthy for tithes?” Rebbi Yannai ben Rebbi Ismaël stated and said: That is, if one is his guest, but for the public he is not trustworthy unless he accepts it publicly. And here we deal with the public<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah states explicitly that one buys from the baker who offers baked goods to the public.</i>.
Rebbi Abin, Rebbi Shammai, in the name of Rebbi Aḥa, understood this from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 5:2 states that a person who wants to separate heave and heave of the tithe together must separate 1% for heave of the tithe. Heave of the tithe has to be given as 10% of the tithe which in itself is 10% of the produce left after heave was taken. Hence, the 1% mentioned in the next Mishnah is 1% of what is left after heave was taken. Similarly, the Mishnah does not imply that <i>ḥallah</i> is subject to the laws of <i>demay</i> but that the 1% for heave of the tithe is to be measured only after regular <i>ḥallah</i> was taken.</i>. “He who wants to separate heave and the heave of the tithe together.” As you will say there, except for that which may become sanctified as heave, here also except for that which may become sanctified as <i>ḥallah</i>.
We have stated <i>ḥallah</i> between First and Second Tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah prescribes taking heave of the tithe and <i>ḥallah</i> together, and only then to give a name to Second Tithe and to redeem it. Both tithes should have been given when the grain was first stored at the farm barn. The obligation of <i>ḥallah</i> comes much later, when grain is first made into flour and then the flour into bread dough.</i>. Rebbi Jonah in the name of Rebbi Zeïra: This means that <i>ḥallah</i> is not subject to “do not give too late<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">“From your storage and your <i>dema‘</i> you should not give too late,” <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:28" href="/Exodus.22.28"><i>Ex</i>. 22:28</a>. From this one concludes that a person who gave heave of the tithe before the first heave cannot declare that he followed all the rules of tithing. No similar rule seems to exist for <i>ḥallah</i>.</i>.” Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Zeïra: The Mishnah is not formulated in sequence, do we not deal with <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Since heave and tithes should have been given at the threshing floor, any fixing of <i>demay</i> is in itself in the wrong order.</i>? Did not Rebbi Abba, son of Rebbi Ḥiyya, say in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan that the Mishnah deals with <i>demay</i>; hence, not with certain produce<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to Mishnah 1:4 which states that the order of separation for <i>demay</i> is irrelevant, that one may separate the heave of the tithe (which is part of the First Tithe) after the Second Tithe. R. Joḥanan notes that this is one of the special leniencies for <i>demay</i>.</i>? And here<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnah 5:1.</i> we deal with <i>demay</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:6" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.6">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 5:6</a>: “He who wants to separate heave, heave of the tithe, and <i>ḥallah</i> together …” There the order of the declaration is heave, heave of the tithe, <i>ḥallah</i>, Second Tithe. Since the first heave is mentioned, one speaks about produce from which nothing at all was yet taken. Nevertheless, <i>ḥallah</i> appears between First and Second tithes.</i>: Also for certain. On that, Rebbi Jonah in the name of Rebbi Zeïra said, this means that <i>ḥallah</i> is not subject to “do not give too late.” Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Zeïra: The Mishnah is not formulated in sequence, should not <i>ḥallah</i> precede the First Tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>ḥallah</i> has the status of heave, once the obligation has started it should take the place of heave that <i>must</i> be given before any tithe.</i>? Then <i>ḥallah</i> should be subject to tithes but not tithes to <i>ḥallah</i>, because everything that precedes another, the other is obligated for it<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Just as if one would transgress giving First Tithe before the heave, the heave given later must be not only from the remaining produce but also from the tithe, since anything that comes later needs to be put in order by fulfillment of the prior obligation. Then the requirement that <i>ḥallah</i> should be taken from dough made from grain from which heave and tithes were taken would be put on its head.</i>. And we have stated in this respect<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 4:10" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).4.10">Tosephta <i>Terumot</i> 4:10</a>. It follows that heave and <i>ḥallah</i> are coordinate, not subordinate one to the other. Hence, each of them needs to be put in order with respect to the other. But definitely this should give <i>ḥallah</i> a status prior to tithes.</i>: “He who makes dough from <i>ṭevel</i>, whether he takes <i>ḥallah</i> before heave or heave before <i>ḥallah</i>, what he did is done. <i>Ḥallah</i> should not be eaten until he take heave for it, heave should not be eaten until he separate <i>ḥallah</i> for it.” But why does First Tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., the heave of the tithe that must be given from the First Tithe.</i> have precedence? Because it started at the threshing floor. But does the Second Tithe not start at the threshing floor? Rebbi Mattaniah said, it would have been logical that <i>ḥallah</i> should precede everything else<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Not quite everything else, but everything else which has to be given for <i>demay</i>, i. e., comes after heave.</i>. Why does First Heave have precedence? Because it started at the threshing floor and it is called “beginning<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:12" href="/Numbers.18.12"><i>Num.</i> 18:12</a>. <i>Ḥallah</i> also is called “beginning” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:20" href="/Numbers.15.20"><i>Num.</i> 15:20</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:21" href="/Numbers.15.21">21</a>) but its obligation does not start at the threshing floor.</i>.” Second Tithe, even though it started at the threshing floor, is not called “beginning.”

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who wants to separate heave and heave of the tithe together takes one in thirty-three and one third<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Heave is 2% given by estimate, heave of the tithe is 1% exactly of the remainder, or .98% of the original amount. So together they would be 2.98% or 1 in 33.557, not 1 in 33.3333. This Mishnah follows the ruling of Abba Eleazar ben Gimel (<i>Terumot</i> 1:7, fol. 40d) that all heave may be given by estimate and that heave of the tithe may also be given by the owner himself, not only by the Levite who is the recipient of the First Tithe. Since the heaves are given by estimate, one has to increase the amount somewhat, to 3% or 1 in 331/3.</i> and says: One percent of what is here on this side is profane<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Since the first heave must be given before the heave of the tithe, the amount designated for heave of the tithe must be declared to be profane until after heave has been designated.</i> and the remainder is heave for everything. The profane here on that side is tithe; the rest of the tithe is adjacent to it. That which I have declared as tithe is made heave of the tithe for it, (the rest is <i>ḥallah</i>,) the Second Tithe is either North or South of it and is redeemed by coins.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:6" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.6">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 5:6</a>.</i> He who wants to separate heave, heave of the tithe, and <i>ḥallah</i> together takes for heave, heave of the tithe, and <i>ḥallah</i>, that is one in 20<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The computation is not quite exact. If full amounts are taken, i. e., if it is possible to eat heave in ritual purity, heave is supposed to be 2%. Then heave of the tithe is 1% of the remaining 98%. The grain put in order therefore is 97.02% of the original amount. <i>Ḥallah</i> is 1/48 of the dough (in our case, of the grain), or 2.0833% of the remainder, 2.02125% of the original amount. Hence, the total taken should be 5.00125% of the original amount, not 5% as stated in the Tosephta. However, 1/800 of one percent is not a quantity that is verifiable by eyesight; as the Halakhah will state later, it is disregarded. Also, it is required that both heave and <i>ḥallah</i> be given by estimate, <i>not</i> by measurement; hence, the natural error in such an estimate is much larger than the small overshoot we should require for the heave of the tithe, as indicated in the Mishnah.<br>Today, when no heave can be eaten in the absence of the ashes of the red heifer, both heave and <i>ḥallah</i> are given in minute amounts to avoid destroying food. The modified declaration for putting in order <i>demay</i>, produce bought on a market in Israel, can be found in most of current prayer books printed in Israel. Produce sold by the farmers’ cooperative <i>Tenuvah</i> in Israel (but not produce for export) is all tithed.</i>, and says: One percent of what is here on this side<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Of the 5% set aside, 1% is for heave of the tithe (to be generous, since it should be only .98%); 1 in 48 is <i>ḥallah</i> which also is a heave.</i> is profane <i>ṭevel</i> and another one in 48 next to it; the remainder is heave for everything. The profane which is in it and that which I made profane <i>ṭevel</i> is made tithe; the rest of the tithe is adjacent to it. That which I have declared as tithe is made heave of the tithe for it<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Both here and in the Tosephta, the declaration that the rest is <i>ḥallah</i> is missing. It should be deleted from the Mishnah and inserted here.</i>, the Second Tithe is North or South of it and is redeemed by coins.”
Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: He has to recite five benedictions<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">One for heave, one for the First Tithe, one for the heave of the tithe, one for Second Tithe, and one for <i>ḥallah.</i> Rebbi Ḥiyya prescribes a collective benediction: Praised are You, Eternal, our God, King of the Universe, Who sanctified us by His commandments and commanded us to separate heaves and tithes. (Maimonides <i>Maäser</i> 1:17).</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: He subsumes them all in one benediction.
So far for a Cohen or a Levi<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Normally the Israel farmer gives heave to the Cohen and tithe to the Levite (or Cohen, who also is a Levite) and then the Levite gives heave of the tithe to the Cohen. The Cohen himself, if he is a farmer, has to separate heave, tithe, and heave of the tithe, even though he keeps everything for himself, as is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:26" href="/Numbers.18.26"><i>Num.</i> 18:26</a>): “You also shall separate the heave of the Eternal,” and explained in <i>Sifry Num</i>. #120. He and the Levite are owners both of the grain and the tithe, and can separate both heave and heave of the tithe. However, an Israel should not be owner of the tithe; how can he ever give heave of the tithe?</i>, but an Israel? If he will declare heave of the tithe, does he not separate for something that is not his<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The question is not answered. If one follows Abba Eleazar ben Gimel, quoted in the Note to the Mishnah, there is no question. If one does not follow him, in fact an Israel cannot separate heave and heave of the tithe together.</i>?
If one gives tithe and after that heave, one has preceded<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to tithe before giving heave; this explains the language of the Mishnah.</i>. First heave and after that tithe, might he not give too little tithe or add to his tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Since tithe is supposed to be exactly 10%, and the heave of the tithe exactly 10% of the tithe, do Mishnah and Tosephta, which permit heave and heave of the tithe to be separated together by estimate, not lead into sin since it is almost impossible to guarantee that the heave of the tithe should be exactly 1% and, hence, the implied tithe should be exactly 10%?</i>? As we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 8:13" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).8.13">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 8:13</a>; there the text is similar but the statements are switched in error.</i>, he who gives too little tithe, his tithes are in order but his produce is damaged<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since the produce still contains some unseparated tithe and with it tithe of the heave; its consumption by a non-Cohen would be a deadly sin.</i>, if he gives too much tithe, his tithes are damaged<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Since the superfluous part in it contains 10% Second Tithe or tithe of the poor.</i> but his produce is in order. Rebbi Yose said, he relies on a stipulation by the Court. What is the stipulation by the Court? As far as one’s mind might err<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Even in measurements, a perfect result is not possible. Hence, any estimate that is not clearly in error is accepted as exact and the pedantic argument from before is not accepted. This argument is not directly applicable to heave of the tithe; since the Torah prescribes exactly 1%, it is not clear from where the Court should have the authority to stipulate that we accept deviations from the Biblical norm. Hence, the stipulation must refer to tithe. While tithe has to be 10% by Biblical decree, and using the food before tithe was given is a deadly sin, tithe in the hand of the Levite or Cohen is profane food after heave of the tithe was given; in contrast to Second Tithe, which retains its status of holiness until consumed in Jerusalem or redeemed, First Tithe is the absolute possession of Cohen or Levite. Hence, the amount transferred to the Levite is a transfer of profane goods and subject to the eminent power of the Court which may declare property forfeit. Since the Court has the power to set the standards for tithe, the amount of heave of the tithe is a derivative of the amount set aside for tithe.</i>.
What is a stipulation by the Court? That one should fix the First Tithe to the North, Second Tithe to the South, and the heave of the tithe at the extreme North<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">That one always should proceed in the same way any time one has produce to put in order as a matter of discipline lest one forget a step. For anybody following these rules, the first stipulation applies and good faith estimates are as valid as exact measurements. Consequently, if one does not follow this particular way of doing things, even the first stipulation is not valid.</i>. What is a stipulation by the Court? It is that if the Second Tithe is redeemed, that which is in excess is also redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The possibility of estimating is given not only for First, but also for Second Tithe.</i>. All this refers to dry produce; for fluids<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The method of Mishnah and Tosephta is not applicable to those fluids, wine and olive oil, which have to be tithed as Biblical commandment since these fluids will not remain static. The question is not answered; it seems that wine and olive oil must be tithed separately, the slow way.</i>?
If somebody had 100 figs, 50 large ones and 50 small ones. If he takes from the large ones, should he take nine? If he takes from the small ones, should he take eleven? If he takes ten large ones, there might be one profane among them and we would worry lest perhaps he use that one to make it heave of the tithe for another place, but it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:26" href="/Numbers.18.26"><i>Num.</i> 18:26</a>): “The heave of the Eternal, tithe from tithe,” not profane from tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Probably a scribal error; it would be more reasonable to say “not tithe from profane,” since once produce is put in order, nothing of it can ever become heave or tithe again. But there is no manuscript evidence for this and the problem is not taken up in <i>Sifry.</i></i>. Rebbi Abun bar Cahana<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Galilean Amora of the third generation, a student of R. Joḥanan who lived until the time of R. Mana.</i> said, he stipulates and says, the tithe due from this produce should be fixed from the tip of each stem und continue until it reaches<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">If the total volume of all figs is V and that of the ten equal selected large figs is ε + V/10, then in each fig there is a volume of ε/10 profane and the rest is tithe. Hence, there is no fig selected that cannot be used for heave of the tithe.</i>; in each of the ten there is heave of the tithe and a tiny bit of profane.
That takes care of large quantities. If there was little, if he had ten figs, five large ones and five small ones? If he takes from the large ones, he has to take one minus<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Latin <i>parum</i>, adv., “too little, not enough.”</i> a little. From the little ones, he has to take one plus a little. If he took one of the large ones, it contains a little bit of profane and one has to worry that maybe he turns that profane part into heave of the tithe for another place, and it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:26" href="/Numbers.18.26"><i>Num.</i> 18:26</a>): “The heave of the Eternal, tithe from tithe,” not profane from tithe. Rebbi Abba from Carthage said, he stipulates and says, the tithe due from this produce should be fixed from the tip of each stem and he marks it and says to the Cohen, as far as where I marked it<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The heave extends only from the stem to the mark on the fig.</i>.
“What is here on this side is profane <i>ṭevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the Tosephta at the start of the Halakhah; it is possible that it also refers to the Mishnah, where the word <i>ṭevel</i> is understood to qualify “profane.” The question is, why is the language of the declaration so involved, and could it not be simplified by omitting this step?</i>.” Why does he not say, what is here on this side is tithe? He would precede<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">He would give a name to the tithe before the heave, which is forbidden.</i>. Why does he not say, that on this side is heave<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Not on the 1% mentioned in Mishnah and Tosephta, but the 2% left out of the designation.</i>? It would turn out that either he diminishes or increases the heave<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">This “heave” cannot refer to the first heave which has no measure from the Torah and must be given by an estimate; the 2% are only a guideline for the average person. It must refer to the heave of the tithe which should be exactly 1%. If everything is declared <i>ṭevel</i>, 10% can be declared to be tithe following the stipulation of the Court mentioned earlier and the stipulation will cover any deviation from the exact 1% for the grain actually separated from the harvest.</i>. Since he says, what is here on this side is profane <i>ṭevel</i>, whether he diminishes or increases the heave does not matter.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who buys from the baker may tithe from the warm on the cold and from the cold on the warm<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">That means, from old bread for fresh, even though it may be not only from two different batches but also from the lesser quality on the better, which is a questionable but legal practice, as will be explained in tractate <i>Terumot</i>.</i>, even from many types<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Greek τύπος. It is not clear whether “type” or “shape” is the form of a baking pan or the form of bread baked on sheets. The commentators take “shape” to be the baking mould even though it forces Maimonides to note that the technique of baking bread had changed from Mishnaic times to his time. It seems more likely that “type” refers to the kind of the bread since different kinds of bread require different kinds of flour. This is the position of R. Jehudah.</i>, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jehudah forbids it; I say that the wheat of yesterday was from one source and today’s is from another<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">One could be from a source that was tithed and another from a source that was not, since all the tithing here is for <i>demay</i>.</i>. Rebbi Simeon forbids for heave of the tithe and permits for <i>ḥallah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ḥallah</i> becomes obligated at the baker’s; hence, everybody will agree that all bread from one baker can be fixed with one <i>ḥallah</i>. It follows that R. Jehudah, who does not mention <i>ḥallah</i>, will agree, and if R. Jehudah and R. Simeon disagree, R. Simeon must be the more restrictive since he forbids all tithing from one batch to another, even of the same day.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> How long? A student of Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, up to 30 days<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Not including the thirtieth day.</i>. How is that? If he bought from him at the beginning of 30 days, in the middle, and at the end. The first and the second are one kind, the first and the third are two kinds.
“Rebbi Simeon forbids for heave of the tithe and permits for <i>ḥallah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the Mishnah.</i>.” Then Rebbi Jehudah forbids for <i>ḥallah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Simeon is quoted as disagreeing with R. Jehudah (otherwise the Mishnah would say, R. Jehudah and R. Simeon say …); it seems that R. Jehudah forbids to give one <i>ḥallah</i> for different batches from the same bakery.</i>. Does it not become <i>ṭevel</i> at his place<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">It is irrelevant from where the baker bought his flour; the obligation of <i>ḥallah</i> starts when he makes his dough and only depends on the baker’s actions.</i>? But perhaps he borrows impure sour dough to make his bread-dough<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">“Impure” does not mean literally impure, but that he bought from an <i>am haäreẓ</i> who does not care whether his food is ritually pure or not as long as it is not dedicated to the Temple. But the <i>am haäreẓ</i> does not take the heave of the tithe seriously, so we are back to the situation described by R. Jehudah that one batch of flour is from a source which was tithed, the other from a source not tithed.</i>. Even so, does it not become <i>ṭevel</i> at his place<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The distinction made in the preceding sentence is relevant for heave of the tithe, irrelevant for <i>ḥallah</i>.</i>? But perhaps he borrows an impure loaf<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">A loaf from another <i>am haäreẓ</i> baker.</i> to fill up his oven. If he borrows an impure loaf to fill up his oven, must he then not tithe every single loaf<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The other source could have given <i>ḥallah</i> on its dough and after baking the loaves are indistinguishable; one may not separate heave (<i>ḥallah</i>) from a loaf already put in order, and there would be no way out but to take <i>demay</i> <i>ḥallah</i> from every single loaf and not from one for all.</i>? That is for Rebbi Joḥanan, since Rebbi Joḥanan said, there<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 2:4, see Halakhah 2:4, 3:1.</i> if he works in purity, here if he works in impurity. But for Rebbi Eleazar, who says that in both cases if he works in purity<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">How could R. Jehudah forbid taking one <i>ḥallah</i> for all?</i>? Rebbi Yudan, the father of Rebbi Mattaniah<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">A Galilean Amora of the third generation, father of the better known R. Mattaniah of the fourth generation. His teachers are unknown.</i> said, we refer to one who works in impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">We also refer to one who works in impurity; he who cares for purity also cares for tithes.</i> and it becomes <i>ṭevel</i> at his place, and Rebbi Jehudah also agrees. We have stated accordingly: “Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Simeon forbid for heave of the tithe and permit for <i>ḥallah</i>.” Hence, Rebbi Jehudah forbids for heave of the tithe and permits for <i>ḥallah</i>; Rebbi Simeon forbids for heave of the tithe and permits for <i>ḥallah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">What is the difference between them?</i>. What is the difference between them?
