In the opinion of Rebbi Jehudah, one may not separate from today’s for yesterday’s or from yesterday’s for today’s, but one may separate from one oven batch on another oven batch<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">He states clearly that one may not tithe from one day to another; batches of the same day may be taken together.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Simeon, even from one oven batch on another one may not separate.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who buys from a merchant<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πρατήρ “seller”; he might buy from different bakeries, and everybody agrees that bread from different sources incurs different obligations of <i>ḥallah</i> and requires separate tithing.</i> tithes from each type<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Greek τύπος. It is not clear whether “type” or “shape” is the form of a baking pan or the form of bread baked on sheets. The commentators take “shape” to be the baking mould even though it forces Maimonides to note that the technique of baking bread had changed from Mishnaic times to his time. It seems more likely that “type” refers to the kind of the bread since different kinds of bread require different kinds of flour. This is the position of R. Jehudah.</i> separately, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jehudah says, from one for everything. Rebbi Jehudah agrees about him who buys from a store with a monopoly<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Greek μονοπώλιον “right of monopoly,” certain to have more than one source, as explained in the Halakhah.</i> that he tithes from each single loaf.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Meïr says, a baker makes one type and a merchant uses several bakers<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in Babli Baba <i>Meẓia‘</i> 56a.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, a baker makes several types and a merchant uses only one baker.
What is a monopoly? At the House of Rebbi Yannai they say, nine sellers and ten bakers. Eight buy from eight and one from two<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">A store has monopoly character if it outsells its closest rival by a ratio of two to one, or if it sells products from twice as many manufacturers.</i>.
Rebbi Jonah asked: If he had before him two types, it would be evident that they are two. If all bakers agreed to make one and the same type, does he have to tithe each loaf separately<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, are all stores together one monopolist chain?</i>? Rebbi Jonah asked: As you said, nine stores and ten bakers, eight buy from eight and one from two. Similarly, nine bakers and ten stores; eight buy from eight and one from two, or nine from the nine and one buys from all of them? He must tithe each loaf separately.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The definition of the monopoly is only a paradigm; the rule is applicable to every similar case.</i>

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who buys from a poor person, and similarly, the poor to whom bread slices or pieces of fig cake were given, tithe from each piece separately. But he mixes together dates and dried figs and then gives. Rebbi Jehudah said, when is that<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Usually, statements of R. Jehudah introduced by “when is that?” give the reason for the preceding statement and do not express disagreement.</i>? If the gift is large. But if the gift is small, he tithes from each single one.<br>He who buys from the grain wholesaler and later buys from him a second time should not tithe from one batch for any other, not even if they are from the same crate, not even if they are the same kind<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Since the wholesale commissioner surely buys from several farmers, two batches of the same kind of grain will probably come from two different sources and maybe one of them was already tithed but the other was not; then it is impossible to tithe from one for the other. Since the wholesaler has no monetary interest in telling whether the two batches of grain came from the same source or not, and as a wholesaler he sells <i>demay</i> with rabbinic permission, his statement may be believed.</i>. But the grain wholesaler is trustworthy to say that they are from the same source.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Pedaiah, Rebbi Jonah in the name of Ḥizqiah, there is no mixing except for wine and oil<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Statement of Samuel in Babli <i>Roš Hašanah</i> 13b. It probably means that only fluids can be tithed when mixed because fluids from different sources quickly diffuse and material from each source is contained in every volume element in the fluid.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan says, they can be mixed up to the size of olives. Our Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “But he mixes together dates and dried figs and then gives<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan notes that dry matter may also be mixed if the parts are small enough. Dates and figs, fresh and dried, are much larger than olives. The answer is that figs and dates, in order to be tithed together, must be in pieces, each of them smaller than the volume of an olive. Then these pieces must be mixed. {Maimonides does not mention any cutting into pieces either in his Commentary to the Mishnah or in his Code (<i>Maäserˊ</i> 4:8). R. Simson in his commentary requires that figs and dates be mashed together into cakes. This contradicts both the Yerushalmi and the Mishnah, because mashed figs are not גרוגרת but דבילה.}</i>.” He explains it up to olive-sized bits.
Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">He restricts his statement in the first paragraph, that fluids only may be tithed mixed, to cases where a Biblical commandment is involved. In his opinion, rabbinic <i>demay</i> was never under this rule.</i> in the name of Rebbi Zeïra: They permitted for <i>demay</i> following Rebbi Jehudah, since “Rebbi Jehudah says, when is that? If the gift is large<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of this condition is explained in the next paragraph.</i>. But if the gift is small, he tithes from each single one.”
In the House of Rebbi Yannai they said: The Mishnah speaks of threshing time<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">When R. Jehudah makes a difference between large and small gifts, he refers only to times when the harvest is brought to the threshing floor or the barn and all farmers give equally. In that case, we say that probably they also gave tithes and the rules of <i>demay</i> allow for special leniencies. But if not all farmers at the place give in equal amounts, the poor recipient must split his gifts into packets corresponding in size to the smallest packet he received, and if this consists of a single fruit he must tithe all his gifts singly (or, giver by giver.) [The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:9" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.9">Tosephta (<i>Demay</i> 5:9</a>) has another explanation, that “big gifts” are given only in years of a bumper crop; in that case, everybody may be assumed to tithe.]</i>, when everybody was giving from a hundred <i>seah</i>. If all were giving from a hundred <i>seah</i> but one only from fifty, he must tithe from fifty. If all were giving from fifty but one only from forty, he must tithe from forty. If all were giving from forty but one only from thirty, he must tithe from thirty. If all were giving from twenty but one only from ten, he must tithe from ten. If all were giving from ten but one only from one, he must tithe every single one.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Is the grain wholesaler also to be believed if the client expresses satisfaction with the earlier buy? In that case, the wholesaler has a monetary interest in the answer and should not be believed.</i>: Even if he declares the buy to be good? “That wheat I bought from you yesterday was good!” “These are the same!”
It was stated: “He who buys from the retail merchant and returns to buy a second time. If he recognizes that the amphora is the same, he tithes from one on the other<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:11" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.11">Tosephta, <i>Demay</i> 5:11</a>, has an opposite statement: “He who buys from the retail merchant and went back and bought a second time, even if he recognizes the amphora that it is the same, he may not tithe from one on the other.”</i>.” But if he does not recognize it, he does not. That means, in the case of <i>’ydtyqrym’</i><sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">This word has not been identified. The Rome ms. has אורתי קדמיתא, [Cirillo (ed. Luncz) has אולותוקרימא.] Lieberman (תוספתא כפשוטה p. 254–255) is correct in insisting that the word designates a kind of barrel; his emendation οἴνου κεράμια is unsupported by the evidence. D. Sperber (Sinai 78, 40–41) reads *ὄρθοκεράμια, “upright standing jar.” But he takes שְׁפָייָא to mean “flat lying vessels,” not parallel to the usual שפיתא “vessel used to draw wine.” It is difficult to imagine a clay vessel lying with its opening horizontal, even if closed by a clay stopper, without losing all its contents. Liebermann quotes a Syriac source showing that the שפיתא used to draw wine from the storage tank was also used to transport the wine from the producer to retail stores.<br>Perhaps the word is a combination of ὑδρία, ἡ “water pot”, but also used for a vessel or urn of any kind, including “wine pot”, and κέραμος, ὁ “clay”, but also anything made of clay, including “wine jar” (E. G.).</i>, but not in the case of vessels (used to draw wine) which usually are refilled one from the other.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who buys from a farmer and then buys from him a second time tithes from one batch for the other, even if they are from two crates, even if the are from two different hamlets<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">If the farmer tithes, <i>demay</i> is a formality for all his produce. If he does not tithe, all his produce needs tithing and it is possible to tithe from one for the other.</i>. If the farmer sells vegetables on the market, in case that his supply comes from his own vegetable gardens, one tithes from one for all others, from other vegetable gardens, one has to tithe each bunch separately.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Jonah asked<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The entire discussion is about the farmer who sells not only his own produce on the market.</i>: According to Rebbi Meïr, should he not tithe each stem separately? As we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Makhshirin 6:2" href="/Mishnah_Makhshirin.6.2">Mishnah <i>Makhširin</i> 6:2</a>: “All bundled vegetables on the market are impure. R. Jehudah declares the fresh ones pure. R. Meïr said, why did they declare them impure? Only because of spittle!” The first Tanna holds that on the market one sprinkles water on the vegetables to keep them fresh. Hence, they are subject to impurity and, since everybody touches them, we must assume that an unclean person may have touched them and treat the vegetables as impure. R. Jehudah disagrees for those fresh vegetables which do not need refreshing; hence, they are not subject to impurity and it does not matter who touched them. R. Meïr restricts the impurity to bundles; since in binding them the farmer will have taken the string into his mouth the bundle was wetted intentionally, and, unless the farmer is a <i>ḥaver</i>, the bundle will certainly be impure. If the farmer is a <i>ḥaver</i>, the bundle still will probably become impure on the market.</i>: “Rebbi Meïr said, why did it become impure<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Elided א, for טמאו.</i>? Only because of spittle!” As you will say, there it is usual to open bundles and he<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The producer of the bundles.</i> binds using his mouth, but here, is it usual to open bundles to risk<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Does the seller risk to open bundles? Is it not usual to have the good-looking vegetables outside and the lesser quality inside? Who would risk losing his sales by having less fresh vegetables visible from the outside?</i> having the outer ones inside and the inner ones outside?
Rebbi Jonah said, not only from his own vegetable gardens, but even from vegetable gardens of others, if they tithe under his instruction<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Then all vegetables are either tithed or not tithed; in both cases, one can tithe <i>demay</i> without worry.</i>, he tithes from one for the other, but if they do not, he has to tithe each bunch separately.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who buys <i>ṭevel</i> from two sources may tithe from one for the other even though they said that nobody is permitted to sell <i>ṭevel</i> except in case of need<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">If the first heave was not taken, it is clearly possible to take heave from one batch for the other, as long as they are of the same kind. The need to sell <i>ṭevel</i> arises if some grain that was tithed fell into a heap of <i>ṭevel.</i> In that case, heave can no longer be taken from that heap, because what one takes out may be from the profane grain and cannot become heave. Then it is necessary to take the heave for this heap from some other heap. But nobody can take heave for another person’s property. Hence, a transfer of property may be necessary.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Jonah said, this is the Mishnah: Nobody is permitted to sell <i>ṭevel</i> except in case of need, and only to a <i>ḥaver</i><sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>ḥaver</i> not only must be observant but also knowlegeable in all the rules concerning <i>ṭevel</i>. [This כיני מתניתא is not discussed in the systematic study of the term in R. J. N. Epstein’s מבוא לנוסח המשנה.]</i>. In that case, if <i>ṭevel</i> of an <i>am haäreẓ</i> was mixed with profane, can that never be fixed? What shall he do? He goes to a <i>ḥaver</i> who buys <i>ṭevel</i> for him<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jonah implies that <i>ṭevel</i> can be sold to a <i>ḥaver</i> acting not only for himself but also as trustee for others.</i> and tithes for him.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One tithes from a Jew’s produce for that of a Gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">This Mishnah represents only the opinion of R. Meïr, as stated in the Halakhah.</i> and from a Gentile’s for that of a Jew; from that of a Jew for that of a Samaritan, and from that of a Samaritan for that of a Samaritan. Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eleazar bar Shamua, cf. מבוא לנוסח המשנה ע׳ תתשעז. The attribution is reasonable here since the first Tanna is identified as R. Meïr, contemporary of R. Eleazar. The reason of R. Eleazar is that Samaritans always tithe what they eat but not what they sell, but it could happen that a Samaritan farmer tithes for his own use and then for some reason sells that produce which is already in order and cannot be used to tithe any other. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:22" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.22">Tosephta (<i>Demay</i> 5:22</a>), the statement and the reasoning behind it are attributed to R. Eliezer (ben Hyrkanos); this is reasonable there since his opponent is his contemporary, R. Ṭarphon.</i> prohibits from that of a Samaritan for that of a Samaritan.<br>A flower pot with a hole is like earth<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">If a root can grow out through the hole, the soil in the pot is considered part of the earth and in all respects the produce grown in such a pot is produce grown on a field.</i>. If he gave heave from the earth for a pot with a hole, or from a flower pot with a hole for produce of the earth, his heave is heave. From a pot without a hole<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">The produce of a pot without hole is not “produce of the field;” there is no Biblical commandment to give heave and tithes from such produce. The amount separated for heave, therefore, is not heave in Biblical law; only the rabbis ordered that heave and tithes should be given also from produce not grown on the earth (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:6:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.6.2-5"><i>Kilaim</i> 7:6</a>, fol. 31a.) But since it is not heave from the Biblical point of view, heave must be taken a second time from the produce subject to heave.</i> for one with a hole it is heave<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">However, it does not create <i>dema</i>‘ (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:25" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.25">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 5:25</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:6:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.6.2-5">Yerushalmi <i>Kilaim</i> 7:6</a>).</i>, but he must take heave a second time. From a pot with a hole for one without a hole<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Since the pot without a hole cannot cause heave to be given by Biblical law, the “heave” still is <i>ṭevel</i> and heave and tithes must be removed. The rest should be treated as heave by rabbinical decree, as in the previous case.</i>, it is heave that should not be eaten unless heave and tithes were taken for it<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 89b" href="/Yevamot.89b">Babli <i>Yebamot</i> 89b</a>, it says “heave and tithes from another place,” considering it similar to <i>dema</i>‘. This definitely is not the position of the Yerushalmi.</i>. If he took heave from one <i>demay</i> for other <i>demay</i>, or from <i>demay</i> for certain produce, it is heave, but he must take heave a second time<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Since we do not know whether the produce from which heave was taken was already tithed or not.</i>. From certain produce for <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Since in the majority of cases <i>demay</i> was tithed, the heave taken would remain <i>ṭevel.</i> Hence, it must be treated as if it were certain <i>ṭevel.</i></i>, it is heave that should not be eaten unless heave and tithes were taken for it.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Parallels <i>Giṭṭin</i> 4:12, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 47a" href="/Gittin.47a">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i>47a</a>; there the dispute is between two Amoraim, Rabba (in Babylonia, holding that Gentile land is subject to tithes) and R. Eleazar (in Galilee, holding that the Gentile’s land is free from tithes.) The verses quoted also are totally different. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Gittin 47a:8:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Gittin.47a.8.1">Tosaphot <i>(Giṭṭin</i> 47a, s. v. אמר רבה</a>) prove that in the opinion of the Babli, R. Meïr frees Gentile land from heave and tithes except if it is leased to a Jewish sharecropper. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:21" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.21">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 5:21</a>, the position of R. Meïr is the same as here, the opposition consists of R. Jehudah, R. Simeon, R. Yose. The Tosephta is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 66b" href="/Menachot.66b">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i> 66b</a>; there, R. Meïr and R. Jehudah agree with R. Meïr here; R. Simeon and R. Yose oppose. In the interpretation of the Babli <i>Menaḥot</i>, the disagreement is not about the status of the Land; it is universally held that ownership of real estate by a Gentile does not free the Land from its obligations. Rather, it is the ownership of the grain at the moment the obligation of heave and tithes starts, i. e., when the grain is stored. Since a Gentile is not subject to the laws of the Torah, R. Simeon and R. Jehudah hold that his grain cannot be subject to those laws. Cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 4:6:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.4.6.9"><i>Peah</i>, Chapter 4, Notes 129–131</a>.</i> is Rebbi Meïr’s, since Rebbi Meïr says that a Gentile may not acquire real estate in the Land of Israel to remove it from tithe; Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Simeon say, a Gentile may acquire real estate in the Land of Israel to free it from tithe. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: The reason of Rebbi Meïr (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:46" href="/Leviticus.25.46"><i>Lev.</i>25:46</a>): “You shall transmit them<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Non–Jewish slaves.</i> by inheritance to your sons after you, to inherit by the rules of real estate.” This brackets real estate with slaves. Just as you may buy slaves from them but they cannot buy from you<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 46a" href="/Yevamot.46a">Babli <i>Yebamot</i> 46a</a>, this is derived from the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:45" href="/Leviticus.25.45"><i>Lev.</i> 25:45</a>: “ ‘Also from the children of the inhabitants that live among you, from them you may buy;’ you, but not they.” As a matter of practical law, since the Jewish buyer of a non–Jewish slave is required to convert him, and the slave then is subject to the commandments of the Torah; if his owner would sell him to a Gentile, the slave automatically would go free.</i>, so real estate you may buy from them but they cannot buy from you. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose said before Rebbi Yasa, the following supports Rebbi Meïr (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:23" href="/Leviticus.25.23"><i>Lev.</i> 25:23</a>): “The land shall not be sold permanently, because the land belongs to Me,” absolutely<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">This cryptic note refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Behar, Section 4 8" href="/Sifra,_Behar,_Section_4.8"><i>Sifra Behar</i>,<i>Parashah</i> 4(8</a>): לצמיתות לחולטנית, “permanently means absolutely.” See R. Shaul Israeli, ספר ארץ חמדה<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup> Jerusalem 1999, p. 195 ff.</i>. He said to him, that in itself supports Rebbi Simeon. “It shall not be sold”<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">If it could not be sold, there would be no sin in selling it, since the transaction would be void. But since selling agricultural real estate in the Land of Israel to a Gentile is a sin, by necessity the sale would be valid and would imply unrestricted transfer of title.</i>, because if it was sold, it would be sold absolutely.
The Great Rebbi Ḥuna from Sepphoris said, Rebbi Ḥanina instituted in Sepphoris following Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">That produce grown by a Gentile on his own land, or the land of another Gentile, is free from any duty of heave and tithes and cannot be used for any tithing. Since two authorities testify to it, this is the received practice. See in Note 104 that the names of R. Simeon’s partners vary with the sources; this might be the reason that he alone is quoted.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra said, Rebbi Ḥanina instituted in Sepphoris following Rebbi Simeon.
Rebbi Zeïra said before Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Even though Rebbi Meïr says that a Gentile may not acquire real estate in the Land of Israel to free it from tithes, he agrees here that he may acquire property rights. Rebbi Abba said, in order to eat its fruits. Did we not state: “The buyer<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.9.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i>4:9</a>. The Jew who bought agricultural land in Israel from a Gentile can bring its First Fruits to the Temple even though this is not his inheritance from the land distribution by Jouhua. For Rebbi Meïr, who holds that possession by a Gentile cannot change the status of the Land relative to the duties imposed on it by the Torah, it should be clear that just as heave and tithe were not lifted from the Land, so the duty of First Fruits was not lifted. Why then is the bringing of First Fruits classified as a rabbinical ordinance, “for the good of the world,” in order to encourage people to buy land in Israel, and not as original Biblical obligation?</i> brings First Fruits for the good of the world.” Why does he not bring it as a Biblical obligation?
Rebbi Jonah, Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: He who buys loose produce from a Gentile separates heave and heave of the tithe as a matter of practice, gives it to the tribe, and receives money from the tribe<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">See <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 3:4:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.3.4.10">Chapter 3, Notes 131–132</a>. Since heave is taken as a matter of common practice, heave and heave of the tithe remain the property of the owner (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 11b" href="/Bekhorot.11b">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i> 11b</a>). Since heave and heave of the tithe may be eaten only by Cohanim, presumably they will make a very low offer for the food, but they must pay.</i>. But he who buys produce still in the ground from a Gentile separates heave and heave of the tithe as a matter of practice, gives it to the tribe, and does not take money from the tribe<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Since the obligation of heave and tithes comes only when the harvest is stored, the obligation devolves on the Jew and the tithes are the immediate property of the Cohanim; the owner only has the choice of whom to give.</i>. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:26" href="/Numbers.18.26"><i>Num.</i> 18:26</a>) “If you remove the tithe from the Children of Israel.” From the Children of Israel you remove the tithe, but not from the acquaintances of Cohanim or Levites<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the second case, when the owner can decide to whom to give. However, it is possible that the sentence should read (<i>Sifry Zuṭa Qoraḥ</i>#26): “From the Children of Israel you remove the tithe, but not from Cohanim or Levites.” In that case, the quote has no direct connection with the discussion here.</i>. From the Children of Israel you remove the tithe, but not from the hand of a Gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the first case, when the harvest was the Gentile’s and no Biblical obligation of heave and tithes was incurred. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 11b" href="/Bekhorot.11b">Babli (<i>Bekhorot</i> 11b</a>) reports in the name of R. Joshua ben Levi that only heave, not heave of the tithe, must be given as a matter of practice. This is a serious disagreement between the two Talmudim.</i>.
Rebbi Aḥa said, in the days of Rebbi Hoshaiah they wanted to vote on King’s Mountain<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">A conquest of king Alexander Yannai, not part of the settlement of Judea and Galilee by the returnees from Babylonia, but part of the Jewish state in the later Hasmonean times. It seems that in early Amoraic times King’s Mountain was entirely in Gentile hands.</i>, to free it from tithes. They said, let Rebbi Hoshaiah come. Before he could come, the opportunity was lost<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">A war was going on and the assembly was dispersed.</i>.
Rebbi Judah ben Pazi in the name of Rebbi Hoshaiah: Practice follows Rebbi Simeon. Otherwise, we would say, between Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Simeon, practice does not follow Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 46b" href="/Eruvin.46b">Babli, <i>Eruvin</i> 46b</a>, the question about whose opinion prevails remains unanswered. In rabbinic tradition (<i>Maḥzor Vitry</i> p. 490, see the sources quoted there, note חֹ), between R. Meïr and R. Simeon practice follows the one who is more restrictive. However, R. Asher ben Yeḥiel (<i>Roš</i>,<i>Abodah Zarah</i> 2, #34) holds that between R. Meïr and R. Simeon practice follows R. Simeon.</i>. But because it was stated: “Rebbi Simeon from Shezur said, once some produce that was <i>ṭevel</i> was mixed up with my produce. I asked Rebbi Ṭarphon, and he told me: Go and buy from a Gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ṭarphon also holds that any produce grown in the Holy Land is subject to heave and tithes. The Gentile cannot separate heave and tithes; even if he wants to follow the Jewish rules and donate his tithes to Jewish priests, his heave is no heave. Hence, produce grown by a Gentile is certainly untithed and can be used to tithe other produce.</i> and tithe for it.” On that we have stated: “Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Simeon say, a Gentile may acquire real estate in the Land of Israel to free it from tithe.” Lest you think that this case has two<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ṭarphon and R. Meïr, belonging to two separate generations.</i> opposed by two<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon and R. Jehudah in our text, R. Simeon and R. Yose in the Babli. All three belong to the same generation. [One has to wonder why one tries to insist here on the status of R. Simeon since it is agreed that both R. Jehudah and R. Yose have precedence over R. Meïr (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 46b" href="/Eruvin.46b">Babli <i>Erubin</i> 46b</a>, Yerushalmi <i>Terumot</i> 3:1, fol. 42a, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 4:1:2-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.4.1.2-14"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 4:1,</a> fol. 30b), and even if one agrees to count R. Jehudah with R. Meïr as in the Babli text, R. Yose has precedence over R. Jehudah (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 46b" href="/Eruvin.46b">Babli <i>Erubin</i> 46b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:11:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.11.2-10">Yerushalmi <i>Yebamot</i> 4:11,</a> fol. 6a).]</i>. Therefore, he had to say that practice follows Rebbi Simeon.
In addition, from the following: Rebbi Zeïra said, Abba the Anatolian bought produce from an Aramean<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">In both Talmudim, “Aramean” means Gentile.</i>; he came before Rebbi Judah ben Levi<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">A contemporary of R. Joshua ben Levi. Since the story implies that he was a Levite who could receive tithes, “ben Levi” simply means “Levite.” The same probably applies to R. Joshua ben Levi, and no relationship between R. Jehudah and R. Joshua is implied except for the fact that both were Levites.</i> who sent his son Menaḥem to put it in order; he<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Abba gave to Menaḥem.</i> gave him his tithes. When he returned, Rebbi Joshua ben Levi met him and said, who else but your father would do such a thing<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">To tithe produce grown and harvested by a Gentile. Hence, R. Joshua ben Levi must agree with R. Simeon, and the former is the great authority.</i>? The arguments of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi are inverted. There, he says that he who buys loose produce from a Gentile, separates heave and heave of the tithe as a matter of practice, gives them to the tribe, and receives money from the tribe; and here he says so! Rebbi Abba bar Zamina said before Rebbi Zeïra, Rebbi Simon does not say so, but when he returned, Rebbi Joshua ben Levi met him and said, this does not belong to your father<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The tithes were given by a misunderstanding that they were regular tithes. Since R. Joshua ben Levi requires that the Levite pay for these tithes, they cannot become the property of R. Judah ben Levi unless Menaḥem paid for them. In this version, R. Joshua ben Levi is consistent.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra was offended.
Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Tanḥum bar Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, three<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">The list contains only two entries. But since the application here is to show that R. Zeïra was rightly offended, the case of produce bought from Gentiles has to be added.</i> may tithe without permission: He whose <i>ṭevel</i> was mixed with profane produce<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">As noted before, since every Jewish farmer did give the first heave which may be given in a minimal amount, it is impossible to buy on the market Jewish produce that can be used to fix a mixture of profane and <i>ṭevel</i> produce. Hence, any farmer who uses his own <i>ṭevel</i> produce to take care of his neighbor’s problem, does his neighbor a great favor and we are justified in decreeing that the neighbor’s consent is implied and he does not have to be asked beforehand. “Permission” in the text means consent by the owner of the produce.</i>. And he who buys from the division of the Samaritans<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.3.4.1">Mishnah <i>Taäniot</i> 3:4</a> explains that in Temple times, in Judea everybody has to be believed in matters of ritual purity of wine and olive oil used in the Temple. This implies that in Galilee only <i>ḥaverim</i> are trustworthy. The reason is explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 3:4:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.3.4.2-8">Halakhah <i>Taäniot</i> 3:4</a> (fol. 79c), because “the division of Samaritans” separates Judea from Galilee and it is impossible to bring wine and olive oil in purity from Galilee to Judea (since Samaritans, like Gentiles, do not bury still-births in cemeteries but anywhere at roadsides, and one cannot exclude the possibility that one might step over such a grave which imparts the impurity of the dead to wine and oil.) [In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 25a" href="/Taanit.25a">Babli (<i>Taänit</i> 25a</a>), it is asserted that a strip of Gentile land separates Galilee from Judea.] The division of the Samaritans therefore is the region of Samaria inhabited exclusively by Samaritans and produce from there is certainly <i>ṭevel</i> in all respects. Anybody who removes heave from this produce and turns it into regular <i>demay</i> removes a hidden stumbling block from the unwary buyer.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra said, this implies that he does not take money from the tribe. If you say that he takes money from the tribe, he tithed with permission<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">If money was to be given to the owner of the produce for heave and the heave of the tithe, he would be happy if somebody else did the work for him practically without cost to him. Hence, this would have the status of tithing with the knowledge of the owner; R. Simon cannot be correct in his transmission of the tradition of R. Joshua ben Levi and the latter must hold that practice follows R. Simeon.</i>!
Rebbi Joḥanan said, the reason of Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of the statement of R. Eleazar in the Mishnah.</i>: Just as they declared the produce of the Land of Israel <i>demay</i> notwithstanding the majority<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Since most of the <i>amē haäreẓ</i> farmers did tithe, there would have been no need for <i>demay.</i> Now that there is <i>demay</i>, we have to worry that the bunch before us is not in order but all others, the greater part, are in order. Therefore, <i>demay</i> may not be tithed from one batch on the other.</i> and one may not give heave or tithe from one for the other, so they declared the produce of Samaritans certain<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Since Samaritans are not obligated by their rules to give heave and tithe from produce they sell, we declare all their produce to be certain that no heave or tithes were given, even though there might be a sizeable minority, in particular in regions where Jews and Samaritans farm together, who tithe. Because of that minority, we may not permit tithing from one farmer’s produce for that of another.</i>, notwithstanding the majority one may not give heave or tithe from one for the other.
Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Zeïra: They declared a pot<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">A pot without a hole, since a pot with a hole is not doubtful at all.</i> to be of doubtful status. But did we not state: “A pot with a hole sanctifies in the vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.6.1">Mishnah <i>Kilaim</i> 7:8</a>. The language is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:9" href="/Deuteronomy.22.9"><i>Deut.</i> 22:9</a>: “Do not sow two kinds in your vineyard, lest the fullness of the seeds that you are sowing together with the yield of the vineyard be sanctified.” Sanctified here means that it becomes like dedicated sacrifice, forbidden for all use; קרבן “sacrifice” has the same meaning in non-sacrificial context (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.2.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim 1:2</a></i>,<i>Matthew</i> 15:5).</i>, a pot without a hole does not sanctify in the vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah implies that a pot without hole, put in the earth in a vineyard, certainly is not considered growing in the vineyard.</i>.” Because of doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah expresses the Biblical law, but because of doubt there is still a rabbinic prohibition to grow grain in a pot without holes in a vineyard (Maimonides <i>Kilaim</i> 5:16).</i>.
Just as you say, “from certain produce for <i>demay</i>, it is heave that should not be eaten unless heave and tithes were taken for it,” should one say so also for “from one <i>demay</i> for other <i>demay</i>?<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>demay</i> probably is in order, why should the two cases have different rules?</i>” Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac, this means that a case of doubtful <i>dema‘</i> is like a case of doubt about heave of the tithe from <i>demay</i>; it is free from the rules of certain produce<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">We do not whether the produce is <i>dema‘</i>, containing the forbidden heave of the tithe. Here all the leniencies of <i>demay</i> given in the first two chapters do apply.</i>.

Chapter 6



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who receives a field as sharecropper from a Jew, a Gentile, or a Samaritan, distributes in their presence<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The sharecropper does not have to give heave before taking his own share.</i>. He who leases<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The contract calls for a fixed payment in kind independent of the actual yield. In that case, we do not allow anybody to liquidate his debts from his harvest without separating heave. But in a bid of the rabbis to attract Jewish farmers, the lessee is not required to tithe before he pays his rent. The lessor knows that he has to tithe.</i> a field from a Jew separates heave and delivers to him. Rebbi Jehudah says, when is that? When he gives from that field and from that kind. But if he gives from another field or from another kind<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, the payment is simply a financial transaction and does not enjoy special status.</i>, he must tithe and give him.<br>He who leases a field from a Gentile tithes and delivers to him. Rebbi Jehudah says, also he who is a sharecropper for a Gentile on a field that used to belong to his family tithes and delivers to him<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">As an incentive to make it worthwhile for him to buy back the property from the Gentile.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Joḥanan said, these are the words of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel. But the words of the Sages are: From a Jew, he splits; from a Gentile, he gives heave. We have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 6:1" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).6.1">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 6:1</a>: “He who receives a field as sharecropper from a Gentile tithes and gives to him. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, what if the Gentile does not want to tithe his produce? But he distributes and gives before him.” It is clear that the Yerushalmi does not speak about a lease for a fixed amount, because then the Gentile would not be interested in what the Jewish tenant does, but about a sharecropper who will diminish the landlord’s part if he gives heave or tithes prior to distribution.</i>: “He who leases a field from a Gentile separates heave and then gives to him. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said: If the Gentile does not wish to give heave, I have no right to it<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Gentile is the owner and one may not give heave without authorization by the owner, it is impossible in Jewish law to give heave for the unwilling Gentile. Hence, the sharecropper may not give heave before he divides up the harvest.</i>, but he<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The farmer distributes the harvest untithed in the presence of the landlord.</i> distributes and puts it before him.”
Rebbi Zeïra, Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:28" href="/Numbers.18.28"><i>Num.</i> 18:28</a>): “You, also you,” to include your plenipotentiary<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">“So <i>you</i> shall lift, <i>also you</i>, the heave of the Eternal.” The expression <i>also you</i> is superfluous; since “also” always means an addition, “also you” means a stand-in. Since he is titled “also you”, it follows that he takes the place of the person authorizing him. The same explanation is given in <i>Terumot</i> 1:1 (fol. 40b/c), Babli <i>Qiddušin</i>41b, <i>Baba Meẓia‘</i> 22a.</i>. Just as you are in the covenant, so your plenipotentiary must be in the covenant. You appoint a plenipotentiary, the Gentile may not. Rebbi Yasa wanted to say that the Gentile cannot appoint as plenipotentiary another Gentile, but he can appoint a Jew. Rebbi Zeïra said, from the <i>baraita</i> itself: You appoint a plenipotentiary, does that not mean Jews? Similarly, the Gentile cannot appoint a plenipotentiary, not even a Jew. Rav Hoshaia objects: Does the <i>baraita</i> support Rebbi Joḥanan? “Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said: If the Gentile does not wish to give heave from his produce, he cannot give heave<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In the opinion of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel the Gentile cannot appoint a Jew as plenipotentiary for Jewish ritual.</i>” Rebbi Abba said, if he confirms it after him<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This parallels the position of R. Isaac in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 1:15" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).1.15">Tosephta <i>Terumot</i> 1:15</a>: “If a Gentile separated heave for a Jew, even with his permission, it is not heave. … Rebbi Isaac says, if a Gentile separated heave for a Jew and the owner confirms his action, it is heave.” For a Jew, once permission is given, it does not require an additional action on the part of the owner. But the Gentile’s action is not valid unless it is explicitly confirmed by the Jewish owner afterwards; the Gentile does the mechanical work and the Jew gives the separated amount the status of heave.</i>.
“ ‘He who leases,’ means he pays in produce, ‘he who rents,’ means he pays money, ‘he who receives,’ as sharecropper for a half, a third, or a quarter<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The first two definitions are also found in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 6:2" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).6.2">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 6:2</a>.</i>”.
So far a Gentile; a Samaritan? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 7:25: “He who gives a field to a Gentile sharecropper, a Samaritan, or to somebody who is not trusted with tithes, even though one is not permitted to do so, he has to tithe for him.” Here the obligation to tithe seems to be a kind of fine imposed upon someone who does not let his field to a trusted Jew. If it is imposed on someone who gives his field to a sharecropper, <i>a fortiori</i> on him who leases his field to a Gentile.</i>: “He who gives a field to a Gentile sharecropper, a Samaritan, or somebody who is not trusted with tithes, before they come to the time of tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">When the produce is prepared to be stored, the obligation of tithes begins.</i> he does not have to tithe for him; after they come to the time of tithes he has to tithe for him.” That is, at a place where Jews are present. At a place where no Jews are available<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">There is no reason to impose a fine if no qualified Jewish farmers are available.</i>? Let us hear from the following: Rebbi Simon had fields at King’s Mountain<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">As noted earlier, in Amoraic times no Jews were left on King’s Mountain. This entire piece also appears in <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 1:9, fol. 40b.</i>. He asked Rebbi Joḥanan, who told him: Let them lie fallow, but do not lease them to a Gentile. He asked Rebbi Joshua ben Levi who permitted it to him. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi is of the opinion that a place where no Jews are available has the status of Syria<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 7:5:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.7.5.6"><i>Peah</i> Chapter 7, Note 119</a>. The argument implies that Neḥemiah’s covenant applies only to those regions of the Holy Land outside the immediate neighborhood of Jerusalem actually settled by Jews. The territory subject to tithes and Sabbatical therefore is variable over time [S. Klein, HUCA 5(1928) 197–260.]</i>. For Syria, we hear from the following: Rebbi Ḥaggai descended into Homs. The family Astor asked him: Since no Jews are available here, we lease to Gentiles; do we have to tithe for them? He sent and asked Rebbi Zeïra. Rebbi Zeïra asked Rebbi Immi, who told him that they did not have to tithe. From this you hear that the rules of leasing follow Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 1:9: “One does not lease houses in Israel to Gentiles, not to mention fields. In Syria one leases to them houses but not fields, and outside the Land one sells them houses and leases fields to them. These are the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Yose says, even in the Land of Israel one does lease houses to them and in Syria one sells to them houses and leases fields to them. Outside the Land one sells both to them.” Since one may lease fields to Gentiles, the act of leasing does not remove a field from the duty of tithes. Hence, there is no such duty on the field itself and the Gentile’s part does not have to be tithed by the Jewish landlord. The Yerushalmi’s decision is repeated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 21a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.21a">Babli <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 21a</a>.</i>. Also from the following: Rebbi Ḥanina, the son of Rebbi Abbahu, said my father had a case; he sent and asked Rebbi Ḥiyya, Rebbi Yasa, and Rebbi Immi, and they taught him that the rules of leasing follow Rebbi Yose; hence, as far as tithing for them is concerned, they<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The landlords do not have to tithe for their exempt tenants. (R. Ḥiyya here is R. Ḥiyya bar Abba.)</i> do not tithe for them.
“If<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 1:6" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).1.6">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 1:6</a>. The rule applies both to the sharecropper and to the lessee. Since the preceding Tosephta states the rules for sharecropper and lessee of a Jewish landlord, it follows that this Tosephta (and the one discussed in the following paragraph) deal with a Gentile landlord, which fits in the context of the present Halakhah.</i> he received a field to harvest for wheat<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">That the agricultural worker takes his wages in the form of the finished product: threshed wheat grain, wine, or oil. This is after the obligation of tithes started.</i>, grapes to harvest for wine, olives to harvest for oil, he tithes before he delivers.” Come and see: Before he sows, he separates heave<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">At most, the lessee separates heave; the sharecropper does not give anything before splitting with the owner. This shows that the Tosephta speaks both of sharecropper and lessee.</i>; after it was sown, he tithes? Rebbi Ḥinena said, they made a special decree for the sharecropper, so that the Land of Israel should not lie fallow. But here it is already sown.
“If<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 1:6" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).1.6">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 1:6</a>. In this case, the payment is given before any obligation of heave and tithes was incurred.</i> he received a field to harvest for early ears, grapes to harvest for baskets of grapes, olives to harvest for baskets of olives, he delivers as is.” That means, if (he is paid) in the same kind; but from another field, or from that field but not from the same kind, he must tithe before he delivers. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">This is the opinion of the Sages in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 1:5</a>.</i>: “From the same field, whether it be the same kind or another kind, he separates heave and delivers. From another field, whether it be the same kind or another kind, he tithes and delivers.” The Mishnah follows Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel; what Rebbi Ḥiyya stated follows the Sages, and they disagree. We also may come and state that the Mishnah deals with some place where barley usually does not yield twice as much as wheat<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to another disagreement between the Sages and Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel (<i>Baba Meẓia‘</i> 9:8): “He who receives a field to sow it with barley should not sow it with wheat; wheat, he may sow it with barley. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel forbids it.” In the opinion of the Yerushalmi, this depends on the relative cash value of the crops raised; if barley yields enough to offset its cheaper price relative to wheat, the lessee is permitted to change the crop. “The Mishnah” in our text means the opinion of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, representing actual practice. [In the opinion of the Babli (<i>Baba Meẓia‘</i>106b), wheat needs a more frequent crop rotation and lying fallow than barley and the landlord can always object.] Tosephta <i>Baba Meẓia‘</i> 9:32, dealing with the subject matter of Mishnah 9:8, does not mention the point of view of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel.</i>; what Rebbi Ḥiyya stated refers to a place where barley usually yields twice as much as wheat.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnah 1, dealing with leases of agricultural land from a Jewish landlord.</i> you say, he separates heave and delivers to him. Here you say, he tithes and delivers to him. The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The Sages fined the one who leases from a Gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">To increase the cost of farming the Gentile’s land, so that people would refrain from leasing and, if all landless farmers are Jews, to force the Gentile to sell out to a Jew.</i> but they did not fine the one who leases from a Jew. Rebbi La in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The strict law<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">That a <i>ḥaver</i>, or any observant Jew, should not deliver any produce not completely tithed to a third person.</i> applies to him who leases from a Gentile but not to him who leases from a Jew.
One <i>baraita</i> supports one and one <i>baraita</i> supports the other. One <i>baraita</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:2" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.2">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 5:2</a>: “He who leases a field from a Gentile must tithe before delivering to him. Hence, if the lessee bought from a Gentile, or the Gentile sold his crop to another Jew, it is <i>demay</i>.” The anonymous author of the Tosephta is R. Meïr in our <i>baraita.</i></i> supports the colleagues. “He who leases a field from a Gentile separates heave and delivers to him. Rebbi Meïr says, he tithes and delivers to him.” Could the rabbis say, the Sages fined him who leases from a Gentile but they did not fine him who leases from a Jew? How can a fine apply to leasing from a Jew? But so it is: The rabbis say, the strict law<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">It is the law that only heave but no tithe has to be given as a precaution since the grain delivered by the lessee never was stored by him and, therefore, never was subject to tithes.</i> applies to him who leases from a Jew, but Rebbi Meïr said, the strict law<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">His strict law is that nobody may deliver produce into another’s hand if that produce is not fully put in order.</i> does not apply to him who leases from a Jew. One <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi La: “He<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:2" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.2">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 5:2</a>: “He who splits the yield of a field with a Gentile tithes before delivering the Gentile’s part. Rebbi Simeon says, he separates heave and delivers the Gentile’s part. Hence, if the Gentile converted or sold to another Jew, he has to tithe before delivering.” (R. S. Liebermann explains that Tosephta as dealing with the problem of retroactive clarification, ברירה, but that explanation is impossible since heave and tithes are due only at storage time, see מנחת ביכורים <i>ad loc.</i> In general, the commentaries on the entire paragraph rely too much on unsupported emendations to be taken seriously.)</i> who leases a field from a Gentile tithes and delivers to him. Rebbi Simeon says, he separates heave and delivers to him.” Could the rabbis say, the strict law applies to him who leases from a Gentile, but the strict law does not apply to him who leases from a Jew; Rebbi Simeon said, the strict law does not apply to him who leases from a Gentile? Can anybody make such a statement<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">A law is a law and cannot be abrogated for a special class of people.</i>? But so it is, the Sages fined him who leases from a Gentile but they did not fine him who leases from a Jew. Rebbi Simeon said, the Sages did not fine him who leases from a Gentile; hence, if the Gentile converted or sold to another Jew, it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">It is retroactively permitted to the Jew to have leased the land from a Gentile when the Gentile sold his property to another Jew, since finally it again became Jewish land. Rebbi Simeon prefers to have the Gentiles convert with their land instead of driving them off by artificial obstacles.</i>.
Him<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the rule of R. Jehudah, who makes the situation of the sharecropper on his ancestral land worse than that of any other sharecropper.</i> who says that the law applies, one can understand. He who says, they fined<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Why should he be fined? R. Joḥanan explains that the Gentile got the land not by paying for it but by expropriation of the Jew. In that case, the Jew should be pushed to ask for help from his fellow Jews to buy back his land. The statement of R. Joḥanan is quoted as a <i>baraita</i> in the Babli (<i>Baba Meẓia</i> 101a), with מציק instead of מסיק (identical sounds in Galilean dialect; Babylonian spelling as a rule is more historically correct.)</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan said, one has stated this only in the case of oppressors. Since you say so to him, he exerts himself and buys the land back.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A Cohen or a Levite who received a field (as sharecroppers) share in the tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Cohen and Levite who are farmers are required to separate heave and tithe like everybody else. If they are the owners they may donate the sanctified parts to themselves.</i> in the same proportion as they share in a profane yield. Rebbi Eliezer says, the tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The Cohen gets all the heave from this field and the Levite all the tithe since they expect it for their work.</i> is theirs because they came because of it.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The rabbis object to Rebbi Eliezer: How did they acquire<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">If the landlord must give his heave to the tenant, there must be a legal reason why he has lost his right to give it to any Cohen he prefers (the usual example being that of a landlord whose daughter married a Cohen and who wants to give all his heave to his grandson the Cohen.)</i>? He said to them, do you not agree with me that if they agreed<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In the sharecropping contract. In that case, the act which validates the contract also validates the stipulation. A <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 7:1" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).7.1">Tosephta (<i>Demay</i> 7:1</a>) authorizes that kind of stipulation.</i> among themselves, tithes go to the workers? Even if they made a condition, how did they acquire? If there is nothing said, it is as if somebody said: Harvest from this land so that its tithes should be yours<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">This is the end of R. Eliezer’s argument.</i>. They said to him, there is no stipulation here; if nothing is said there is no stipulation. It is<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">If there is a stipulation, it is a simple contract of payment for work done but not one for heave since the Cohen is not permitted to work for heave. Hence, there must be a contract for this to work.</i> as if he said, harvest this land on condition that you take one tenth for yourselves.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 7:1" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).7.1">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 7:1</a>: “An Israel {a Jew who is neither Cohen nor Levite} who received {as sharecropper} a field from an Israel, a Cohen from a Cohen, a Levite from a Levite, divide among themselves.”</i>: “A Cohen from a Cohen, a Levite from a Levite, an Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 7:1" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).7.1">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 7:1</a>: “An Israel {a Jew who is neither Cohen nor Levite} who received {as sharecropper} a field from an Israel, a Cohen from a Cohen, a Levite from a Levite, divide among themselves.”</i> from an Israel, split the tithes.” Who needs this statement? Rebbi Eliezer; even though Rebbi Eliezer says that the tithe is theirs because they came because of it, here he agrees that they divide up the tithes.
“An<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 7:3-4" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).7.3-4">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 7:3–4</a>, one reads: “An Israel who received a field (as sharecropper) from a Cohen, if he said to him, on condition that the tithes be mine, or yours, or mine and yours; this is forbidden. A Cohen who received a field (as sharecropper) from an Israel, if he said to him, on condition that the tithes be mine, that is permitted. That they be yours, or that they be mine and yours, if he received it under the standard sharecropping contract, it is permitted, otherwise it is forbidden.”</i> Israel who received a field (as sharecropper) from a Cohen, if he<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The landlord who is the active party in all these cases.</i> said to him, on condition that the tithes be mine, or yours, or mine and yours; this is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Since the tithes belong to the landlord, he can give them to whom he wants.</i>. A Cohen who received a field (as sharecropper) from a Cohen, if he said to him, on condition that the tithes be mine, that is permitted. That they be yours, that is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">“Tithes” here naturally must include heave and heave of the tithe. What is forbidden is that the Cohen landlord gives the heaves to the Cohen sharecropper as part of the latter’s wages for his services, since heave may never be used to liquidate an obligation.</i>. That they be mine and yours, that is forbidden. If he received it under the standard sharecropping contract<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Any distribution of heaves that follows the local standard and is not different for a Cohen than for an Israel cannot be considered a special payment made with heave and is permitted.</i>, it is permitted, otherwise it is forbidden.” Even without that, do not the tithes belong to them<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">To the landlord and the sharecropper, as explained in Mishnah 1. What does the <i>baraita</i> tell us as new information?</i>? But so it must read: “My part is mine, your part is mine and yours, if he received it under the standard sharecropping contract, it is permitted, otherwise it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The Cohen landlord may reserve the heaves of his own field for himself only if this is common practice; otherwise, it is exploitation of the defenseless status of the sharecropper.</i>.”
“If an Israel received (as sharecropper) a field from another Israel on condition that the tithes go to a certain person, that clause is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Distribution of tithes cannot become part of a financial package.</i>; on condition that I shall take them and deliver them to a certain person, that clause is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 7:1" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).7.1">Tosephta (<i>Demay</i> 7:1</a>) there is a positive prescription that between sharecropper and landlord of similar status, tithes must be evenly split. There is no prescription on how the tithes should be given to Levite or Cohen.</i>” Is the second version not identical with the first? Rebbi Aḥa said, “if you agree” makes the difference<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Aḥa, the landlord may not prescribe the distribution of the sharecropper’s tithes but he can ask the latter’s consent to distribute them in a certain manner.</i>. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose agrees with the objection that the two clauses are indistinguishable; he must find another reason why one is permitted and the other forbidden.</i> said, you say it is permitted if it follows the standard sharecropper’s contract. You say it is forbidden if it does not follow the standard sharecropper’s contract. It turns out that Rebbi Yose holds with Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Aḥa with Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina. As Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina said, a person gives his tithes for the benefit of goodwill<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">While a person cannot trade his heaves or tithes, he can use them to create goodwill and so reap an indirect monetary benefit, or even a direct benefit if the party involved is not a Cohen. While an Israel may not take money from a Cohen for his heaves, he may take from an Israel for the promise to give all future heaves to the latter’s grandson who is a Cohen. But since it is not possible to trade this kind of goodwill, it is not considered to be money in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 64b-65a" href="/Nedarim.64b-65a">Babli (<i>Nedarim</i> 64b–65a</a>); hence, if the Cohen takes heave without consent of the owner, he cannot be sued for monetary damages (Maimonides <i>Terumot</i> 12:15). This is the only opinion mentioned in the Babli and the only one in <i>Sifry Naśo</i> (6). (Starting here, the next four paragraphs are also found in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:9:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.9.2-5"><i>Qiddušin</i> 2:10</a>.)</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, a person may not give his tithes for the benefit of goodwill<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Joḥanan does not allow any monetary advantage from giving heaves and tithes.</i>. What is the reason of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:10" href="/Numbers.5.10"><i>Num.</i> 5:10</a>) “Everybody shall be the owner of his holy things.” What does Rebbi Joḥanan with this? He may give them to whomever he likes<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">No Cohen or Levy may take heave or tithe by force; only the owner can decide to whom to give [<i>Sifry Naśo</i> (6)].</i>.
A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥaninah: “A vow that no Cohanim or Levites should have any advantage from me, they should take against his will<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.8.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 3:11</a>. This is one of the vows the husband cannot dissolve if made by his wife since dissolution is unnecessary; any recipient of the obligatory gifts to Cohen and Levite may take them by force in this case, and the owner has no monetary gain. It seems to disagree with R. Joḥanan who says that the owner has the choice of recipient. But since the owner does not want to give what he is obligated to, he transgresses commandments of the Torah and the situation can be saved only by others disregarding his rights.</i>.” He explains it about a person who says, I cannot possibly give them <i>any</i> gifts. You should know that this is so, since we have stated: “<i>These</i> Cohanim and Levites, let others take<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Same Mishnah; if the vow excludes a certain group of people, he always can give to others.</i>.” A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “An Israel can say to another Israel, here you have a tetradrachma and give this first born to my daughter’s son, a Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Speaking of the firstborn of a cow or ewe. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 27a" href="/Bekhorot.27a">Babli (<i>Bekhorot</i>27a</a>) a similar statement is made for future heave. Both statements are taken together in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:18" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.18">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 5:18</a>: “An Israel may say to a Cohen {it seems that it should read: another Israel, but there is absolutely no manuscript evidence for that} here you have a tetradrachma and give heave to my daughter’s son, the Cohen, or give a firstborn to my daughter’s son, the Cohen.” The Babli does not want to accept the statement about the firstborn since the Cohen might think the tetradrachma is redemption money and the firstborn does not have to be treated as a sacrifice.</i>.” He explains, if he already wanted to give it to two Cohanim and that daughter’s son was one of them; then one said, here you have a tetradrachma and give all of it to my daughter’s son, a Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Indirect usufruct is permitted by R. Joḥanan.</i>.
They asked before Rebbi Zeïra, in that situation<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">To give money to an Israel so that he should give his heave or the firstborn of his flock to a designated Cohen.</i>, a Cohen to an Israel, what is the rule? At Rebbi Yose’s they said that he did not answer. Rebbi Ḥizqiah in the name of Rebbi Aḥa said, so he said to them: In the opinion of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina, why is a Cohen to an Israel forbidden, not because it looks badly<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">That people could think that he buys the gifts for himself; this would be forbidden. He should not do it lest he get a reputation as a sinner.</i>? Also Rebbi Joḥanan holds that from an Israel to an Israel it is forbidden because it looks badly<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">In the opinion of R. Yose ben R. Ḥanina the rabbis, not the Bible, prohibited the farmer from having any material gain from his heave and tithes.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, there is desecration of sacrifices and you said because it looks badly? Because of the following, as it was stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:20" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.20">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 5:20</a>, a slightly longer version.</i> “Cohanim and Levites who help at the threshing floor have no right either to heave or to tithe, and if the farmer gave, it is desecrated, as it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:15" href="/Leviticus.22.15">Lev. 22:15</a>): ‘They should not desecrate the sanctified things of the Children of Israel,’ but they desecrate them! In addition, they said<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">By a decree of the Court, which has the power of removing property rights, anything that the Cohen receives is <i>tevel</i> and he has to give its heave and tithes to another Cohen. This is made explicit in the Tosephta.</i> that their heave is no heave, their tithes are no tithes, their dedications are no dedications, and about them the verse says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Micah 3:11" href="/Micah.3.11">Micha 3:11</a>): ‘Their heads judge for bribes and their priests come for a price<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">And their prophets perform witchcraft for money. The three punishments for the three sins are: Zion will be ploughed as a field, Jerusalem will be desolate, and the Temple Mount a wooded hill.</i>.’ The Omnipresent brings over them three catastrophies; that is what is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Micah 3:12" href="/Micah.3.12">Micha 3:12</a>): ‘Therefore, because of you Zion will be ploughed over as a field, etc.’”
A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:9:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.9.2-5"><i>Qiddušin</i> 2:10</a>.</i>: “If somebody marries<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The first of the marriage ceremonies, קידוּשין, requires that the groom give the bride something of value and declare that with this gift she becomes his bride. Hence, the materials enumerated must have monetary value. If the groom is an Israel, how can heave have a monetary value if he does not even have the right to goodwill?</i> giving heave, tithes, ‘gifts’<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">These “gifts” are parts of an animal slaughtered for profane food that are given to a Cohen (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 18:3" href="/Deuteronomy.18.3"><i>Deut.</i> 18:3</a>). They are profane food and may be sold by the Cohen to anybody.</i>, water for sprinkling, or ashes of the red heifer<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The water and the ashes are used to cleanse people from the impurity imparted by a corpse. Water and ashes have no monetary value and it is a desecration to take money for sprinkling water with the ashes on impure persons. However, it is permitted to take money for the time spent in transporting both (and keeping all the rules for this transport.) So the groom does not transfer ownership of the ashes since there is no ownership, but he transfers his claim to be paid for his exertions which is a lien on water and ashes (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 58b" href="/Kiddushin.58b">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 58b</a>).</i>, she is married, even if the groom is Israel.” He explains it with heave he inherited from his maternal grandfather, a Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">If the grandfather died before he had time to consume the heave, the grandson may sell the heave to another Cohen; in that case, everybody agrees that the heave has monetary value.</i>.
“<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 7:14" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).7.14">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 7:14</a>; there the condition is that the buyer be trustworthy for tithes. A similar <i>baraita</i>, dealing with a Levite selling to an Israel, is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 63a" href="/Bava_Batra.63a">Babli <i>Baba Batra</i> 63a</a>. Since a Cohen is required to separate heave and tithes but is permitted to take them for himself in order to eat the heaves in purity, his heaves are a kind of lien on the field and he may sell the field without selling the lien.</i> If a Cohen sold a field to an Israel and made the condition that the tithes should be his, the tithes are his.” May he sell them to a Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">May the owner of these heaves sell them to another Cohen?</i>? Let us hear from the following. Rebbi Abbahu said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: He who sells the tithes of his field to another person did not do anything<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Since the tithes are not his; the goodwill represented by them is not money.</i>, the future children of his slave girl to another person, he did not do anything<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following case are based on the principle that nobody can sell anything that does not yet exist.</i>, the fetus of his animal to another person, he did not do anything, the airspace of his dry land to another person, he did not do anything<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Since the airspace is not material, it cannot be bodily acquired. However, if the seller grants the buyer the right to build in the airspace, he sells the right to set the building on walls or pillars and that is a valid transaction (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 64b" href="/Bava_Batra.64b">Babli <i>Baba Batra</i> 64b</a>, <i>Šulḥan Arukh Ḥošen Mišpaṭ</i> 212.)</i>. But he may sell him a field and reserve the tithes for himself, a slave girl and reserve her children for himself, an animal and reserve her fetus for himself. Is it not impossible for a person to sell the airspace of his dry land to another person<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Why does R. Abbahu include in the list something that seems to be obvious?</i>? Explain it if he tells him, tear out some grasses in that dry land<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">This is a positive action which everywhere confirms acquisition of real estate and liens on real estate. R. Abbahu has to inform us that here it does not work because the land is not an object of the sale. Similarly, in the case of the Cohen selling his right, the tithe is not fixed at 10% of the field but is only a lien that cannot thereby be acquired.</i> to acquire its airspace. And here there is real estate before him and he says, pluck something from the ground so that you acquire one tenth of it.
“<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 7:14" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).7.14">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 7:14</a>; there the condition is that the buyer be trustworthy for tithes. There is no mention of heirs other than sons. A similar <i>baraita</i>, dealing with a Levite selling to an Israel, appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 63a" href="/Bava_Batra.63a">Babli <i>Baba Batra</i> 63a</a>. Since the Cohen may stipulate before the sale, he may stipulate anything he wishes.</i> If a Cohen sold a field to an Israel on condition that the tithes should be his, the tithes are his. If he dies, his sons have no right to the tithes. ‘For me and my sons, for me and my heirs,’ his sons have the tithes, his heirs have the tithes.” Rebbi Yudan bar Shalom asked before Rebbi Yose: So far only for heirs whose claim is from the Torah? Even for heirs whose claim is not from the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">For example, the husband inheriting from his wife whose marriage is only rabbinical, e. g. a man marrying an underage girl after her father’s death.</i>!
“<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 7:14" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).7.14">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 7:14</a>, the text is R. Ḥiyya’s. A similar <i>baraita</i>, dealing with a Levite selling to an Israel, appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 63a" href="/Bava_Batra.63a">Babli <i>Baba Batra</i> 63a</a>, also following R. Ḥiyya only.</i> That all tithes should be mine as long as you own it, he gets its tithes as long as the buyer owns it.” When he sold it to a third party, he has no tithes. If the buyer then bought it back from the third party, Rebbi Ḥiyya states that he has no tithes, Rebbi Hoshaia states that the tithes are his.
