This disagreement turns out like the other disagreement, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.10.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 9:13</a>: “A woman who presents two letters of divorce and two <i>ketubot</i> collects two <i>ketubot.</i> Two <i>ketubot</i> and one letter of divorce, or one <i>ketubah</i> and two letters of divorce, or one <i>ketubah</i>, one letter of divorce, and a death, she collects only one <i>ketubah</i> since ‘he who divorces his wife and then remarries her, remarries her on the basis of the first <i>ketubah</i>.’ ” The <i>ketubah</i> is explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 3:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.3.7.1"><i>Peah</i>, Chapter 3, Note 151</a>. The individual stipulations are obligations the groom takes upon himself in addition to the obligatory <i>ketubah.</i></i> “that he who divorces his wife and then remarries her, remarries her on the basis of the first <i>ketubah</i>.” Rebbi Ḥanin<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">An Amora of the third Galilean generation, student of R. Samuel bar Isaac; a frequent author of aggadic statements. The statement here, which appears in identical form in <i>Ketubot</i> 9:13 (fol. 33c), is his only known halakhic statement.</i> stated before Rebbi La: Two Amoraïm, one says for <i>ketubah</i> but not for individual stipulations, and the other says, both for <i>ketubah</i> and individual stipulations. For him who says, for <i>ketubah</i> but not for individual stipulations, he has the tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the Mishnah and the case that the husband dies and the widow presents two <i>ketubot</i> and one bill of divorce. If one assumes that the argument of the Mishnah applies only to the obligatory <i>ketubah</i>, she collects the amount of one obligatory <i>ketubah</i> but both individual stipulations; hence, the remarrying does not eliminate the validity of the first <i>ketubah.</i> In our case, the repurchasing of the field does not eliminate the first contract and the Cohen re-enters into his rights to tithes. (The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 90a" href="/Ketubot.90a">Babli, <i>Ketubot</i> 90a</a>, which does not consider the case of <i>demay</i> presented by R. Hoshaia, holds that if the date of the letter of divorce is between the dates of the two <i>ketubot</i>, she collects both <i>ketubot</i> in full.)</i>. For him who says, both for <i>ketubah</i> and for individual stipulations, he has no tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, the first <i>ketubah</i> is invalidated completely, as is the Cohen’s contract of sale. (This is the only case considered by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 63a" href="/Bava_Batra.63a">Babli, <i>Baba Batra</i> 63a</a>.)</i>. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 7:9" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).7.9">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 7:9</a>; there it is spelled out: בת כהן.</i>: “If somebody received (as sharecropper) from the daughter of a Cohen, she has the tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Since heave is eaten not only by a Cohen but also by his entire family, her claim is as good as her father’s.</i>. If she married an Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">She becomes a member of her husband’s family and loses her priestly status. Hence, all rules for the Israel apply to her.</i>, they split the tithes. If she was widowed or divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">It is implied that she has no surviving descendants from her Israel husband. Otherwise, her status remains that of an Israel (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:13" href="/Leviticus.22.13"><i>Lev.</i> 22:13</a>).</i>, she returns to her prior status.” There when he sold, here when he received<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Since the sharecropper never had title to the land, it is only her status that decides in matters of tithes.</i>. But did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state: “When she sold?<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">This <i>baraita</i> is not in the Tosephta. R. Ḥiyya, who holds that the Cohen loses all his rights when the Israel sells the field to a third party, states that the daughter of a Cohen who sells her field to an Israel while retaining her rights to tithes, and who loses half of the tithes on her marriage to an Israel, can reclaim all her rights as a childless divorcee or widow. The problem is to reconcile the two statements of R. Ḥiyya; the answer is that in the second case the rights of the seller are only suspended, not eliminated.</i>” Rebbi Samuel bar Eudaimon said, there it left the power of both parties; here, it left the power of the seller but not that of the buyer.
“A<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 7:15" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).7.15">Tosephta <i>Demay</i>7:15</a>.</i> Cohen who sold a field to an Israel on condition that the tithes be his for four or five years can oppose the buyer’s planting it as a vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Since the vineyard is <i>orlah</i> for the first three years and its fruits forbidden for all use, the Cohen would lose most of the tithes he had contracted for.</i>, sowing woad<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἰσάτις, Latin <i>isatis tinctoria</i>, a plant used to make dark blue dye. Since woad is not food, it is exempt from tithes and the Cohen would lose all income from the field.</i>, or turning it into a field of reeds<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Reeds were used to make pens and mats and were a commercial crop, exempt from tithes. As was established in the preceding paragraphs the Cohen, while forbidden to pay for tithes, may reserve some income from the field as a lien on the field; R. Aḥa bar Ulla interpreted the contract to mean that the Cohen reserved for himself one tenth of the yield, whether sacred or profane.</i>. Mine forever, he cannot oppose the buyer’s planting it as a vineyard, sowing woad, or turning it into a field of reeds.” There came a case before Rebbi Aḥa bar Ulla<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">An Amora of the third and fourth generations. He was a Babylonian, student of Rav Ḥisda, who immigrated to Galilee and was still alive in the days of R. Jonah.</i> and he obligated him to turn over to him one tenth of the reeds.
“If an<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 7:15" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).7.15">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 7:15</a>, the first two sentences only.</i> Israel sold a field to a Cohen on condition that the tithes be his<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">That the Israel has the right to the tithes and may give them to any Cohen or Levite he pleases.</i> for four or five years, it is permitted. Forever, it is forbidden, since no Cohen can make a Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">No Non-Cohen can have a permanent right to tithes. While the Israel obviously has the right to demand such a clause, since the Cohen as seller has the right to reserve the tithes for himself forever, here it is the buyer who is told that he may not agree to such a contract.</i>. If a Cohen sold a field to an Israel on condition that the tithes be the Israel’s for four or five years, it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Cohen is automatically the owner of his heave and tithes the moment he separates them from his harvest, but since the ownership is a gift from God and not the result of his farming, he may not desecrate heave and tithes by turning them over to an Israel.</i>.” There you say it is permitted, here you say it is forbidden! There, he gave him rights to something he had the right to dispose of, here, he gave him rights to something he had no right to dispose of.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If an Israel received (as sharecropper) from a Cohen or a Levite, the tithes are for the owners<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody, including the Sages who oppose R. Eliezer in the previous Mishnah, agree that this is the standard stipulation and is valid.</i>. Rebbi Ismael says, if a villager received a field from a Jerusalemite, the Second Tithe is the Jerusalemite’s. But the Sages say, the villager can make pilgrimage<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">He is encouraged to make the pilgrimage if there is a Temple. If there is no Temple, the entire rule is void since the Second Tithe <i>must</i> be redeemed.</i> and eat his part in Jerusalem.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> According to the opinion of Rebbi Ismael, if an impure Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">A Cohen who does not follow the rules of ritual purity and, therefore, cannot ever eat heave. Nobody disputes the fact that he will receive his part of the tithes by Biblical decree.</i> and a pure Cohen were partners, would they not split the tithes? There he may sell the tithes to another pure Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Tithes and heave are private property but cannot be given over to a Non-Cohen.</i>, here can the villager make pilgrimage and eat his part in Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Second Tithe cannot be sold; it must be eaten in Jerusalem. R. Ismael thinks that this is a burden on the farmer.</i>?

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Even an Israel who receives the field as sharecropper from a Cohen or a Levite.</i> receives olives to make oil, they split heave just as they split the profane. Rebbi Jehudah says, if an Israel received olives from a Cohen or a Levite for oil or for half the yield as his wages, the tithes are for the landlords.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Jehudah treats olives like fields but the rabbis do not treat olives like fields<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear whether the rabbis exclude only olive groves from the rules of fields or any orchard. From the language of the Mishnah it seems that olives grown for pickling will come under the general rules of Mishnah 3.</i>. Now it seems that Rebbi Judah is thinking like Rebbi Eliezer, as Rebbi Eliezer says<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnah 3.</i>, “the tithe is theirs because they came because of it.” So here, if a Cohen or a Levite received a field (as sharecroppers), the tithes are theirs because they came because of them.
“<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 7:10" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).7.10">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 7:10</a>. As explained later in this paragraph, the technical term “to put in care” is applied only to an animal that is the joint property of the rancher and an investor.</i> A cow of a Cohen that was put in an Israel’s care and gave birth to a firstborn calf, the firstborn calf is the Cohen’s, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. But the Sages say, the firstborn is their common property<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">The Israel who is the rancher may give his part of the firstborn calf to another Cohen.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah said to them, do you not agree with me that the tithes of his field are his? They said to him, because the body of the field is his property, but the body of the cow is joint property. If the body of the cow were sole property of the Cohen, the firstborn would be the Cohen’s.” We have stated accordingly, “<i>put in care</i> means joint responsibility, <i>selling</i> is the sole responsibility of him who gives.”
“If a<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 7:7" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).7.7">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 7:7</a>. In this case, the Cohen is the owner and heave and tithe are his. The tithe, after heave of the tithe is removed, is profane in the hand of the Cohen and he may give them to anybody he wishes, even an Israel.</i> Cohen gave money to an Israel to buy produce for half the profit<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Not necessarily exactly half the profit, but, like a sharecropper, the Israel will be paid in kind rather than in money.</i> and he (the Cohen) said to him: If they go up or down in price, it is on my account, and the tithes are yours, this is permitted. If an<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 7:8" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).7.8">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 7: 8</a>. The Israel is the owner of everything, the Cohen is only his agent. Heave and tithes may not be part of the payment, otherwise the Cohen’s standing would be that of a Cohen helping with the harvest; see Halakhah 3 above. Hence, the Cohen has to be paid in full before heave and tithe are given to him.</i> Israel gave money to a Cohen to buy produce for half the profit and he (the Israel) said to him: If they go up or down in price, it is on my account, and the tithes are yours, if he had paid the wages, it is permitted, otherwise it is forbidden. An<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:18" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.18">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 5:18</a>. R. Saul Lieberman conjectures that “sold” really means “leased his olive trees for one year,” to be paid a fixed amount of olive oil as rent. One may assume that olives growing on trees are meant, to be harvested and pressed by the contractor. In that sense, “sold” is applicable.</i> Israel who sold olives against 60 <i>log</i> of <i>ṭevel</i>. If one<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">A Cohen, who can take heave and tithes for himself. He may bid for the contract and offer better terms than a competing Israel.</i> said to him, sell them to me and I shall give you 60 <i>log</i> that are in order, that is permitted. 60 <i>log</i> <i>ṭevel</i>, and both of us will share in the tithes, that is forbiden<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">This last sentence is not in the Tosephta. Again, the contractor is a Cohen willing to let the Israel have part of heave and tithe.</i>.” If he gives him everything, it is permitted, if he gives him half, it is forbidden? Rebbi Yose said, if he extends credit to him<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">If the contractor is a Cohen and does not have to deliver the 60 <i>log</i> of oil immediately after they are pressed but only later, while another bidder would have to deliver promptly, not only does he give to the Israel landlord part of his tithes as hidden interest for the extension of credit but he uses tithes (of which heave of the tithe has not been removed and which still retains its holy status) to pay a debt and this is contempt of holy gifts. [In the Tosephta dealing with the first case, the Cohen who delivers 60 <i>log</i> of tithed oil, it is stated explicitly that there is no transgression either of the laws of interest or of those of honoring holy things.]</i>. We also have stated in this respect: If he extends credit to him it is forbidden because of interest and because of degradation of holy things.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The House of Shammai say, one should sell one’s olives only to a <i>ḥaver</i><sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>ḥaver</i> will prepare his olive oil in ritual purity. The House of Shammai forbid causing impurity to food grown in the Land of Israel, but the House of Hillel permit this after the destruction of the Temple.</i>. The House of Hillel say, also to one who tithes. But the modest<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">See end of Halakhah.</i> ones of the House of Hillel used to follow the words of the House of Shammai.<br>If two people put the yield of their vineyards into one wine press and one tithes but the other does not, then he who tithes must tithe his own and what could be his at any place<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning is explained in the Halakhah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Joḥanan said, the reason of the House of Hillel is that people eat from their olives while they are being loaded into the oil vat<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Olives when harvested are put into large vats called מעטן, to be transported to the oil press. Dry food cannot become impure. The olives can become impure only if their oil starts to flow; the lowest olives in the vat will start to exude fluid even before being emptied into the oil press. Then they and all olives wetted by them may become impure. But the top olives may be eaten dry; it is possible that the buyer will eat some of them while in a state of purity even if the buyer is impure. Hence, there is no logical necessity that the sale will result in the olives made impure.</i>, as a subterfuge. The House of Hillel follow their own opinion, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ševiït</i> 5:8. The first sentence is labelled as opinion of the House of Shammai in the Mishnah. The House of Shammai argue that nobody would buy cattle trained for ploughing in a Sabbatical year unless he intends to use it then.</i>: “One should not sell him a ploughing cow in the Sabbatical year, but the House of Hillel permit this because he may slaughter it.” Do people really slaughter their cattle<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Cattle trained for ploughing and still vigorous are much too valuable to be slaughtered as food.</i>? As a subterfuge. They agree that one may sell ears of grain<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">In the Rome ms. (and R. S. Cirillo), the reason is given: כדי חייו, necessities of life may be sold to everybody. The problems start only with large quantities, as indicated in the next paragraph.</i> for dough even though one knows that the buyer will not prepare the dough in purity.
<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">In the Venice text, Halakhah 7 starts here. It is clear that this discussion is the continuation of the previous paragraph and has no connection with Mishnah 7. The note in Leyden ms. and Venice print giving a new Halakhah here is clearly in error. For the rest of this chapter, numbers of Halakhot and Mishnaiot in these sources do not agree; the numbering here follows that of the relevant Mishnah.</i> It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Ma‘serot</i> 3:13.</i>: “They agree that one does not sell a wheat stack, a grape vat<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Grapes usually are harvested into baskets made from palm leaves. For transportation to the wine press, the baskets are then emptied into a vat, usually made of clay (or any other material that is impermeable.) If some grape berries are injured in harvesting and start oozing grape juice in the basket, that fluid will not make the grapes subject to ritual impurity since the oozing juice is lost and unwelcome to the vintner. However, in the vat the lowest grapes will be compressed by the weight of the upper layers of grapes and will start losing juice. Since that juice is poured into the winepress, it is welcome because it reduces the workload on the press. Hence, the juice will make all grapes in the vat subject to ritual impurity and selling the vat to a person unwilling to follow the rules of purity would help to make the vat impure.</i>, or an olive vat<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">The argument given for grapes essentially is valid also for olives harvested into boxes which then are emptied into a vat. The difference is that olives are not easily injured when poured into the vat, and when warmed by the weight of the olives on top will start oozing sap that contains little or no oil. Hence, the olives become susceptible to impurity only if we consider the sap to be oil, since only seven kinds of fluid enable foods to be impure (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1">Chapter 2, Notes 136–137</a>). The operative statement is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 9:1" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.9.1">Mishnah <i>Ṭahorot</i> 9:1</a>: “When do olives become susceptible to impurity? When they start oozing sap in the vat but not in boxes, following the House of Shammai. Rebbi Simeon says, the term for oozing is three days. The House of Hillel say, if three olives cling together. Rabban Gamliel says, when the work is complete {i. e., if no more olives are added to the vat}, and the sages accept his position.”</i> to anybody but a <i>ḥaver</i> or to somebody who is known to process in purity.” Is the grape vat not from the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Since grape juice is one of the Seven Fluids, the impurity of grapes in the vat must be considered Biblical.</i>? This does not disagree with Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan had explained in the previous paragraph that even the nonobservant may eat olives in purity from the vat!</i>; just as Rebbi Joḥanan said, the little one is a restriction<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">“Restriction” means a restriction imposed by the rabbis, or the Men of the Great Assembly, not a direct Biblical ordinance. It is difficult to know what the reference means. In the opinion of R. Eliahu Fulda, one refers to a statement of R. Joḥanan reported in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 80a" href="/Kiddushin.80a">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 80a</a> on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 3:8" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.3.8">Mishnah <i>Ṭahorot</i> 3:8</a>: “If a toddler was found with a piece of dough near a dough made in purity, that dough is pure in the opinion of R. Meïr, impure in the opinion of the Sages, since toddlers will grab everything they see.” On this R. Joḥanan notes that the impurity is rabbinic and one may not burn heave if it was made impure in this way. “Little one” then would refer to a toddler. According to R. Moses Margalit, the statement refers to Mishnah <i>Ṭevul Yom</i> 3:1: “All handles of food which would transmit impurity from its original source transmit impurity for <i>Ṭevul Yom</i>. Food that is almost separated, R. Meïr says, if he takes a large piece and a small one is lifted with it, the small piece goes with the large one. R. Jehudah says, if he takes a small piece and a large one is lifted with it, the large goes with the small one. R. Neḥemiah says, that refers to purity, the Sages say, to impurity.” In this case, “small one” refers to a piece of food and R. Joḥanan notes that the restrictive attitude of the Sages represents a rabbinic decree, not an interpretation of Biblical law.<br>{A “handle” of a fruit is an inedible part by which the fruit may be moved, such as the stalk of a pear or an apple. The stalk is wood and not subject to impurity, but as handle it transmits impurity both from an impure person to the fruit and from an impure fruit to any person handling it. The detailed rules are spelled out in Tractate <i>Uqeẓin</i>.<br><i>Ṭevul</i> <i>Yom</i> means a person who had been impure, had immersed himself in water for cleansing, and now is in an intermediate state until sundown when he will be pure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:7" href="/Leviticus.22.7"><i>Lev</i>. 22:7</a>). In the state of <i>Ṭevul Yom</i>, any heave or sacrifices he touches become unusable; the Mishnah discusses transmission of that disability.}</i>, so “vat of olives” is a restriction<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Since olives and grapes cannot be compared for the fluids extracted from them.</i>. Rebbi Ḥizqiah said to Rebbi Jonah in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah: They disagree about connections. Because everywhere “biting”<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">“Biting” means that after touching, two different things cannot be cleanly separated. The standard example are two pieces of dough which after close contact cannot be cleanly separated again. Such a connection changes the two separate pieces into one for all considerations of impurity.</i> is a connection, squeezing is not a connection. But here even squeezing is a connection<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">According to the House of Hillel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 9:1" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.9.1">Mishnah <i>Ṭahorot</i> 9:1</a>, if the olives are squeezed so that three cling together as one clump, their sap prepares for impurity and the olives are enabled to receive impurity in the Biblical sense.</i>; hence from the Torah they are enabled. Rebbi Jonah met Rebbi Jeremiah and asked him, did you say that? He said, that is not from me, even their enablings are restrictions. Did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jonah asked from R. Jeremiah, how does he square his stand with that Tosephta?</i>: “They agree that one does not sell a wheat stack, a grape vat, or an olive vat to anybody but a <i>ḥaver</i> or to somebody who is known to process in purity?” Is the grape vat not from the Torah? Then the olive vat should be from the Torah! What about this? Explain it following Rebbi Meïr, since Rebbi Meïr said, sap is counted as a fluid<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">R. Meïr holds with the House of Shammai that the sap, as a necessary precursor of the oil, has the Biblical status of olive oil. In that case, the mention of the vat of olives in the Tosephta would not be practice to be followed (neither would the prohibition of selling a wholesale quantity of wheat grain, where impurity can be imparted only if water is used in milling or if the flour later comes into contact with water.)</i>.
Rebbi Zeïra said, the reason of the House of Shammai is that a <i>ḥaver</i> should never sell olives to anybody but one who tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">The House of Shammai does not state like the House of Hillel that one may also sell to one who is certain to tithe but who is not formally a trustworthy person and therefore not obliged to tithe <i>demay</i> before selling, since a <i>ḥaver</i> is always bound by the rules of the trustworthy person.</i>.
Who are the modest ones<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Mentioned in the Mishnah. This sentence appears as Halakhah 8 in the Venice print.</i>? Those of noble conduct. Rav Ḥisda said, here we have taught that those of noble conduct are called modest.
<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">In the Venice print Halakhah 9.</i>: Rebbi Eleazar said, this follows Rebbi Meïr, since Rebbi Meïr said, they permitted to sell <i>demay</i> only to the grain wholesaler<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Halakhah 3:2. R. Eleazar considers the distribution of the finished wine to the two partners as a commercial transaction; hence, the one who tithes may not give to his partner anything that is not tithed.</i>. He explains the Mishnah: The one who tithes must tithe his own part as certain<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">That means, he must give heave and tithes from his own grapes before they are pressed. Otherwise, he would not know what is his.</i>, and that part of his which could be spread anywhere as <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">The part of the grape juice he gets after pressing certainly contains juice from his partner’s grapes of which it must be assumed that heave but not tithes were removed. Hence, he must tithe his grape juice as <i>demay.</i></i>. Rebbi Jonah objected: He sells it as certain and tithes only <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">If this is considered a mutual sale, R. Meïr cannot be satisfied with treatment as <i>demay</i>, since it is required that everything be completely in order.</i>? It is only possible in the way Rebbi Joḥanan said, the Mishnah is the word of everybody: The one who tithes must tithe his own part as certain, and his part anywhere as <i>demay</i>; that means one half of his part in his partner’s hand as <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Since we assume that everybody gives heave and we may assume that not more than 50% of his grape juice comes from his partner’s grapes, half of what he gets must be tithed as <i>demay</i>(assuming that both of them put exactly the same amount of grapes into the press; otherwise, he tithes an amount proportional to his partner’s share in the whole.)</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If two people received a field as sharecroppers or inherited it or bought it as partners, one can say to his partner, take your wheat on this side and I shall take on that side, take your wine from this side and I shall take from the other side. But he should not say to him, take the wheat and I shall take the barley, take the wine and I shall take the oil<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">According to Maimonides in his Commentary, the first part does not refer to the laws of <i>demay</i> but to money matters. In his opinion, a splitting of the first kind, designating parts of the same crop for the benefit of either party, can be enforced in court on condition that it not hurt the other party, but a proposal of the second kind, exchanging rights to different kinds of crops, will not be supported by a court. To oppose a settlement that benefits one party without being in any way detrimental to the other is called “sin of Sodom.”<br>According to R. Simson of Sens, all this deals with the laws of <i>demay</i>; in a division of the first kind, this is simply a necessary splitting of produce. The agreement must have been reached before the harvest, when no heaves and tithes were yet due. Hence, “wine” and “oil” must mean “grapes” and “olives;” nevertheless the unequal splitting is forbidden because it too closely resembles a sale that is questionable if one of the partners is a <i>ḥaver</i> and the other an <i>am haäreẓ.</i> The position of Maimonides seems to be more acceptable since the inheritance of <i>ḥaver</i> and <i>am haäreẓ</i> is treated in the following.</i>. If a <i>ḥaver</i> and an <i>am haäreẓ</i> inherited from their <i>am haäreẓ</i> father, the <i>ḥaver</i> may say, take your wheat on this side and I shall take on that side, take wine from this side and I shall take from the other side. But he should not say to him, take wheat and I shall take barley, take the moist produce and I shall take the dry<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">According to Maimonides, this concerns the <i>demay</i> aspects of the situation discussed above. The splitting is permitted since each partner is taking his own. This ruling follows the position taken in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 107a" href="/Bava_Batra.107a">Babli (<i>Baba Batra</i>107a</a>) as practice that brothers who split an inheritance cannot be considered as buying from one another. It is as if each of the brothers received what was bequeathed to them even before their father’s death. However, if they switch barley for wheat or oil for wine, they certainly have to be considered as buyers from one another and a <i>ḥaver</i> is not permitted to sell anything not fully tithed to an <i>am haäreẓ.</i> The <i>ḥaver</i> would have a natural inclination to take dry produce not subject to ritual impurity. [In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 6:8" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).6.8">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> (6:8</a>) even the splitting according to different places is forbidden if one side is pure and the other impure, since for the <i>ḥaver</i> these are two different kinds.]</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">(Halakhah 10 in the Venice print.) <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 2:10" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).2.10">Tosephta <i>Terumot</i> 2:10</a>. The status of Syria is explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 7:5:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.7.5.6"><i>Peah</i>, Chapter 7, Note 119</a>.</i>: “If a Jew and a Gentile bought a field in Syria, it is as if <i>ṭevel</i> and tithed produce were mixed together, the words of Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Gentile is under no obligation for heave, the harvest itself is untitheable; heave and tithes must be given from obligated produce. The Jew’s part of the crop must be tithed from a third, purely Jewish, source.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Gamliel says, the Jew’s part is obligated, the Gentile’s part is free.” Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: When do they disagree? If they split the field while the grain was standing<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Standing grain is not subject to heave and tithes. Hence, if they split when the grain was standing, the partnership was dissolved before the obligation of heave started.</i>. But if they split grain stacks, even Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel will agree with Rebbi that every single stalk belongs to the partnership<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was cut in common, they hold that there is no retroactive determination which part of the harvest really belonged to whom.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: When do they disagree? If they split the field while the grain was standing. But if they split sheaves, even Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel will agree with Rebbi that every single stalk belongs to the partnership. According to Rebbi Jonah, what is the difference between stacks and sheaves<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">Grain is transported to the threshing floor in sheaves. Making sheaves therefore is the start of turning wheat into grain. Grain being subject to heave and tithes, at that time splitting can no longer determine the status of the harvest. For Rebbi, the start of the harvest determines, for Rabban Simeon only the binding of sheaves.</i>? If he harvests a small amount and shares with his partner. Rebbi Hoshaia said, that is, if they bought with the idea that they would not split. But if they bought with the idea that they always would split, Rebbi will agree with Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel that his part already belonged to him from the first moment<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Retroactively the part of each partner was determined even though it is not determined by any place on the common field. (Such retroactivity, in the Babli called ברירה, usually is admitted only for rabbinic obligations. The obligation of a field in Syria is rabbinic.)</i>. Rebbi Yose said, the words of the rabbis disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">They disprove the statement of R. Hoshaia.</i> since Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan said that partners are the subject of the disagreement between Rebbi and Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to Mishnah Š<i>eqalim</i>1:7, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bekhorot 9:3" href="/Mishnah_Bekhorot.9.3"><i>Bekhorot</i> 9:3</a>, about the tithes on newborn calves and lambs and the <i>agio</i> to be paid the agents of the Temple when the yearly Temple tax of one-half <i>sheqel</i> was due (fee for the banker, amounting to 1/24th of the coins exchanged, to exchange half-sheqels into full sheqels.) Partnerships are not subject to tithes on animals or to the <i>agio</i>; partners contribute full sheqels that need no exchanging. The Mishnah states at both places that the rules for <i>agio</i> and tithes are opposite. If heirs never divided up the inheritance, they are subject to tithes because the flock is still their father’s, but are free from <i>agio</i> because they pay as one entity. If they divide the inheritance and afterwards form a new partnership they are free from tithes as partners but subject to <i>agio</i> because each one pays for himself. Rebbi Joḥanan points out that the rule on the undivided inheritance is not accepted by everybody.</i>. Are brothers who split not like these partners<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">If they split the inheritance, they inherited with the intent of splitting. If Rebbi would accept the argument of R. Hoshaia, he could not write in the Mishnah that the undivided inheritance is subject to tithe on animals.</i>?

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a proselyte and a Gentile inherited from their Gentile father, the proselyte may say, take the idols and I shall take money, take the wine for libations and I shall take produce<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">Idols and wine that might be used for libations to the gods are forbidden for any and all usufruct; if they come into the hands of a Jew they must be destroyed. In Jewish view, the proselyte becomes a new person by converting; he has no legal family relationship with his prior kin. The part he inherits under Gentile law is not his until it is delivered into his hands. Therefore, he is permitted to try to get things that are useful to him. But the moment anything idolatrous came into his legal possession, he is bound to destroy it. The Yerushalmi treats this as reasonable legal construction. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 64a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.64a">Babli (<i>Avodah zarah</i>64a</a>) treats it as a special leniency, rather than a general law.</i>. After they came into the proselyte’s possession, this is forbidden.<br>He who sells produce in Syria and says: ‘They are from the Land of Israel,’ must tithe them. ‘They are tithed,’ one believes him because the mouth that forbade is the mouth that permits<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">A a general principle, a person who gives detrimental information about which no other source of information is available has the right to testify to circumstances that would remove the detrimental interpretation.</i>. ‘They are from my field<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">A field in Syria is subject to tithes only if it is the property of a Jew.</i>,’ he must tithe them. ‘They are tithed,’ one believes him because the mouth that forbade is the mouth that permits. If it is known that he has a field in Syria, he must tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, it is not his mouth which tells that he must tithe.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">In the Venice print, Halakhah 11.</i> a Jew and a Gentile bought a Gentile’s house and found there idols, libation wine, and coins, he should not tell him, take the idols and libation wine, and I shall take the coins.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, not only idols, libation wine, and coins, but even if there were two idols, one shaped as a tripod<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δέλφιξ “tripod” [e. g. “three-legged pot”, which might be dedicated to a temple.]</i> and one not shaped as a tripod, he should not tell him, take the one that is not shaped as a tripod and I shall take the one shaped like a tripod<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Both in the Tosephta and the statement of R. Joḥanan, the exchange would bring an illicit monetary gain through articles from which any usufruct is forbidden.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra said, that is correct, since even in the case of a <i>ḥaver</i> and an <i>am haäreẓ</i> who inherited from their <i>am haäreẓ</i> father and found there produce which might become impure and produce which might not, may he say to him, you take the produce which might become impure and I shall take that which might not<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">The produce that might become impure since it was in the hands of an <i>am haäreẓ</i>, must be assumed to be impure and unfit for consumption by a <i>ḥaver.</i> According to Rashi (Babli <i>Ḥagigah</i> 25b), the <i>ḥaver</i> would be guilty of transgressing the prohibition to put an obstacle before a blind person if he steered his brother the <i>am haäreẓ</i> to produce which, by the <i>ḥaver’</i> s rules, should not be eaten by a Jew.</i>? But our Mishnah disagrees: “Take the idols and I shall take money, take the wine for libations and I shall take produce.” But the end shows that there is no disagreement: “After they came into the possession of the proselyte, this is forbidden;” here, from the moment he acquired it it is as if it entered into his possession<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">In practical Jewish law, paying for real estate does not convey the title but only permits acquisition of the title. For actual possession, entering the property or some substitute symbolic action is required. However, since this real estate is acquired under Gentile law, concluding the contract has the force of conveying the title; hence, from the moment the title is transferred in Gentile law, the Jew can no longer try to give the unwanted articles to his Gentile partner. The implication is that a stipulation, contained in a transaction giving the idols to the Gentile and executed before the contract for sale of the real estate was drawn up, would be valid by the Yerushalmi’s standards. This inference seems to be denied in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 64a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.64a">Babli, <i>Avodah zarah</i> 64a</a>.</i>.
“Aquila the Proselyte<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 6:13" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).6.13">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 6:13</a>. Aquila was a student of Rebbis Eliezer and Joshua and the author of a very literal translation of the Bible. He did not avail himself of the permission given in the Mishnah but took his share of the idols (or their price) and threw them into the Dead Sea, which was the ultimate way of making sure that nobody would derive any benefit from them.</i> split with his brothers; he restricted himself and brought his gain to the Dead Sea.” Three Amoraïm; one said that he brought the price of the idols to the Dead Sea, another said that he brought the price of his part in the idols to the Dead Sea, and another said that he brought the idols themselves to the Dead Sea, in order to remove them from his father’s house.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> An <i>am haäreẓ</i> who said to a <i>ḥaver</i>, buy for me a bunch of vegetables, buy for me a loaf<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κόλλιξ, “roll, loaf of coarse bread.”</i>, if he buys anonymously, he is free<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">Since the produce or the roll never was the property of the <i>ḥaver</i>, he is not under the obligation to tithe before he delivers the goods. (Explanation of R. S. Lieberman.)</i>. If he says, this one is for me<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, if they became mixed up they become the <i>ḥaver</i>’s property before delivery and must be tithed.</i> and that one is for my friend, if they became mixed up he has to tithe, even if there are a hundred.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">Halakhah 12 in Venice print.</i>: There<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Peah</i> 8:2. There, everybody knows that the person is poor; hence, it is not the mouth which forbade the produce, asserting that it was not tithed, that could permit it without tithing as gifts to the poor. Nevertheless, the Mishnah asserts that the poor can be trusted.</i>, we have stated: “They can be trusted about gleanings, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i> at harvest time.” And here you say so<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">In Syria, nobody is trusted if his negative information can be verified by other sources. [All commentators here follow the different interpretation of R. Eliahu Fulda which, however, is based on a forced emendation of the text.]</i>? There, his assertion is for exemption, but here his assertion is for obligation. And we have stated so<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">This <i>baraita</i> is not found in parallel sources, but a similar statement appears in the quote from the Tosephta in the next paragraph.</i>: “If his assertion is for exemption, he may be trusted in restrictions.” But here, he can be trusted even if his explanation is to make it easier<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, he can only be trusted if his initial information is not available from other sources.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:2" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.2">Tosephta <i>Demai</i> 5:2</a>: “A Gentile who was calling out, ‘come and buy fruits, they are from Azeqa, from an <i>orlah</i> tree, from a vineyard in its fourth year,’ cannot be believed because he intends to advertise. But if he says, ‘I bought them from a certain Gentile,’ he is to be trusted for restrictions, the words of Rebbi. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, he cannot be trusted since the words of a Gentile are irrelevant.”</i>: “A Gentile who was calling out, ‘come and buy fruits from me, they are from an <i>orlah</i> tree<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">A tree in the first three years after planting, when its fruits are forbidden for all use (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:23" href="/Leviticus.19.23"><i>Lev.</i> 19:23</a>).</i>, they are from a vineyard in its fourth year<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Peah</i> 7:6.</i>,’ cannot be believed. If he says, ‘I brought them from a certain Gentile,’ he may be trusted for restrictions<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">He can be believed in that the produce is untithed; he cannot be believed that the produce is not subject to heave and tithes.</i>, the words of Rebbi. The Sages say, the words of a Gentile are irrelevant<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">As far as Jewish religious obligations are concerned.</i>.” Rebbi Judan asked, what if he calls out when he is uninformed<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">If he never heard of Jewish restrictions, or calls out in a place without any Jewish inhabitants. The questions are not answered, so they must be answered in a restrictive sense.</i>? Rebbi Judan asked, what if one holds that a Samaritan is like a Gentile, since they disagreed: “A Samaritan is like a Gentile, the words of Rebbi. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, a Samaritan is like a Jew in all respects<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Demay</i> 3:4, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 7:1:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.7.1.2-8"><i>Berakhot</i> 7:1</a>.</i>”?
Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, that is only if most of what he takes in comes from his own<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last statement in the Mishnah, that he will not be trusted for tithes if it is known that he has a field in Syria. If most of his fields are outside both Syria and the Holy Land, he still will be believed.</i>.
If a Jew had a sharecropper<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">An <i>am haäreẓ</i> who, in general, cannot be trusted in matters of tithes. But since in Syria most produce on the market comes from Gentile farmers who are exempt from tithes, it is to be assumed that the fruits come from a Gentile source and are permitted without tithing.</i> in Syria who sent him fruits and said that they were tithed; I say that he bought them on the market, if that kind is found on the market. Not only if he does not grow that kind on his field, but even if he does grow that kind on his field; since that kind is found on the market it is permitted.
Our Mishnah follows Rebbi Yose, as we have stated: “He who buys anonymously has to tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">This statement itself is not found in other sources, but there are two versions of the underlying <i>baraita</i> that explain the situation. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 8:1" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).8.1">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 8:1</a>: “If an <i>am haäreẓ</i> said to a <i>ḥaver</i>, buy for me a bunch of vegetables, buy for me a loaf, Rebbi Yose says the <i>ḥaver</i> does not have to tithe, Rebbi Jehudah says he does.” <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 37b" href="/Eruvin.37b">Babli <i>Erubin</i> 37b</a>: “If an <i>am haäreẓ</i> said to a <i>ḥaver</i>, buy for me a bunch of vegetables, buy for me a loaf, Rebbi Yose says the <i>ḥaver</i> does not have to tithe, but the Sages say he does.”</i>.” What are we talking about? If he said to him, go and buy for me, he is his agent<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">According to all opinions, he should not have to tithe since in all respects he represents the <i>am haäreẓ</i> who had commissioned him.</i>; go and buy for yourself, they are his property<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">According to all opinions he has to tithe before he delivers to the <i>am haäreẓ</i>. There seems to be no place for the disagreement between R. Yose and R. Jehudah.</i>. But in our case nothing was spelled out. Rebbi Jehudah says that the seller wants the person who buys to get the property rights<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">Since in handing over the merchandise, the seller also agrees to transmit the rights to the merchandise, it matters to whom the seller wants to transmit those rights.</i>, Rebbi Yose says that the seller wants the owner of the money to get the property rights. Therefore, if he added a piece<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">If the seller delivered a little more than was ordered for the price of the goods ordered, it depends on who is considered the recipient of the seller’s gift. This depends on our interpretation of the disagreement between Rebbis Jehudah and Yose. A slightly different version is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 98b" href="/Ketubot.98b">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 98b</a>. There, Rashi refers to Tosephta <i>Demay</i>; it should read: Yerushalmi <i>Demay</i>.</i>, Rebbi Jehudah says, it belongs to the buyer, Rebbi Yose says, it belongs to both of them. The argument of Rebbi Yose seems to be inverted. There he says that the seller wants the owner of the money to get the property rights, and here you say so<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">Why should the agent get anything if the property is given to the owner of the money? The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 98b" href="/Ketubot.98b">Babli, <i>Ketubot</i> 98b</a>, gives a different answer: If the unit price of the merchandise is fixed, then any addition is a gift and is jointly owned. If the unit price is not fixed, then the additional amount is part of the sale and totally belongs to the owner of the money.</i>? Here the transaction is through the money of one and the feet of the other, therefore they split.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 8:1" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).8.1">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 8:1</a>. If the buyer took the coin for himself, it becomes his property and he owes the original owner money. Hence, if he buys, he acquires the merchandise and as a <i>ḥaver</i>, he has to tithe.</i>: “Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, if he exchanged the coin he has to tithe.” Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">The Amora. The alienation means that the coins fall in unauthorized hands (that they are lost or stolen.)</i> said, this means that if somebody gives coins to another person to exchange them, when they are lost the recipient is answerable for their alienation.
Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last case in the Mishnah: he bought explicitly for himself and his partner but then the produce was mixed up.</i> says, if he distributes it himself<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">If the seller gave the two lots separately the <i>ḥaver</i> did not acquire the <i>am haäreẓ</i>’s part and might not have to tithe.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, only if he took his share last<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">In some situations, the <i>ḥaver</i> might have to tithe only his own share if he took it last.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, both statements are necessary<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">Samuel and R. Eleazar do not disagree but speak about different scenarios.</i>. If he<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">The seller; the next “he” is the <i>ḥaver</i> buyer who never acquired the produce destined for the <i>am haäreẓ</i> and is not responsible for it.</i> distributes it himself and took his share first he only has to tithe his share. If he<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">The seller did not divide but the <i>ḥaver</i> who bought resolved in his mind that all produce would be the <i>am haäreẓ</i>’s except the last bunch that would be for himself.</i> did not distribute it but took his share last, he only has to tithe his share.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:14" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.14">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 5:14</a>, <i>Avodah zarah</i> 8:10, quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 63a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.63a">Babli <i>Avodah zarah</i> 63a</a>, where, however, the second case is formulated so that the employer offers to pay the store for the purchases of the employee. The version discussed here, that the employer guarantees the employee’s expenses, is also in Yerushalmi <i>Qiddušin</i> 2:1 (fol. 62c), together with the interpretations of Rebbis Zeïra and Hila (Illa, La).</i>: “One may say to his worker<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">That the <i>am haäreẓ</i> worker would buy produce grown in the Sabbatical year which is forbidden for commercial transactions, or <i>demay</i> food and not tithe it. Since the money was given beforehand, it is spent on the responsibility of the worker alone.</i>: Here you have a denar, use it for food, here you have a denar, use it for drink, and not be worried about either the Sabbatical year<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">If the worker is a Gentile, one assumes that he pours out a small libation before he drinks and the wine is forbidden for usufruct since it was used for a pagan rite.</i>, tithes, or wine for libations<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">Since food and drink were bought on the employer’s credit, it is sinful for the employer to let the employee transgress religious laws on his behalf, or to have the Gentile worker use on his behalf wine of which all usufruct is forbidden to a Jew.</i>. But if he told him, go and buy yourself a loaf and I shall give you the money, go and buy yourself a <i>reviït</i> of wine and I shall give you the money, he has to worry<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">Since food and drink were bought on the employer’s credit, it is sinful for the employer to let the employee transgress religious laws on his behalf, or to have the Gentile worker use on his behalf wine of which all usufruct is forbidden to a Jew.</i> because of the Sabbatical year, tithes, and wine used for libations.” Rebbi Zeïra said<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">Why does the employer sin when the employee buys forbidden foods with his money?</i>, the grocer becomes the plenipotentiary of the employer to let the worker acquire<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, it is as if the employer himself gave the forbidden food to his employee and the employer is guilty of transgressing the prohibition to put a stone into the path of a blind man.</i>. Rebbi Hila said, the worker acquired for the employer from the grocer<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">In most cases, the opinion of R. Hila is not materially different from that of R. Zeïra, but in his opinion the grocer does not have any responsibility. (In the Babli version, the role of the grocer is the only question and it leads to rather forced solutions. The position of R. Hila is explicitly rejected in the Babli, <i>Qiddušin</i> 8b, as explained in חידושי הרשב״א <i>ad loc</i>.)</i> and then acquires it himself. What is the difference between them? If the grocer was deaf-mute. According to Rebbi Zeïra, he<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">The employer.</i> does not have to worry because a deaf-mute cannot become a plenipotentiary<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">The deaf-mute cannot act without a guardian.</i>; according to Rebbi Hila, he has to worry.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 6:4" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).6.4">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 6:4</a>: “A Jew should not say to a Gentile, a Samaritan, or to one who cannot be trusted with tithes, here you have 200 <i>zuz</i> and pay for me to the treasury, but he says to him, free me from the treasury. Similarly, a person should not say to his friend, here you have 200 <i>zuz</i> and pay the <i>leiturgia</i>, but he says to him, separate me from the <i>leiturgia</i>.” The parallel is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 71a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.71a">Babli <i>Avodah zarah</i>71a</a>.</i>: “A person should not say to his friend, here you have 200 <i>zuz</i> and pay for me to the treasury, but he says to him, free me from the treasury<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">The government requires taxes not in money but food and wine, where the Jew cannot appoint a plenipotentiary who for him buys untithed produce or, if he is a Gentile, wine for libations. He has to formulate it so that the other party (those described in the Tosephta) will not be appointed plenipotentiary. In the interpretation of R. Solomon ben Adrat (תשובות הרשב״א חלק א, תרפו), the king has wine which he forces the citizens to buy at fixed prices. Since this is all wine for libations, the Jew is unable to buy the wine for himself, so he gives money to his Gentile friend and tells him, not to buy for him, but to free him from his obligation. It is possible that the treasury will be satisfied with money and will not insist on delivery of the wine, but if it delivers, the wine will be the Gentile’s.</i>. Similarly, a person should not say to his friend, here you have 200 <i>zuz</i> and pay the <i>leiturgia</i><sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λειτουργία, ἡ. “public service”, offices and obligations forced upon private citizens by the government. Obligations included providing food and wine to officials or troups. The Jew himself could not satisfy his obligations with cheaper untithed produce and Gentile wine. (Cf. R. Solomon ben Adrat loc. cit.; בעל התרומות שער מו ח״ד יח in the name of Nachmanides.)</i>, but he says to him, free me from the <i>leiturgia</i>.”

Chapter 7



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody invites his friend for a meal<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">On the Sabbath, when tithing is forbidden.</i> but the latter does not trust him with tithes, the friend should say on Friday eve: That which I will separate in the future shall be tithe, the rest of tithe near to it<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The nine tenths of the tithe that one is not required to give.</i>. That which I made tithe shall be the heave of the tithe for everything, and the Second Tithe shall be to the North (or South) and be redeemed by coins<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">It is understood that the prospective guest must have some coins at home, reserved for redemptions. Each time some Second Tithe is redeemed, a <i>peruṭah</i>, one-eighth of an obolus, must be deducted from the value of the coin. If all value has been used, the coin should be destroyed.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Joḥanan said: The Mishnah deals with <i>demay</i>. Hence, not with certain produce<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Either <i>ṭevel</i> or produce from which heave was taken but one is certain that no tithe was taken.</i>? A Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 7:6. Since the possibility of tithing while he is out in his fields is discussed, that Mishnah must deal with weekdays. One discusses here only the possibility of tithing at a distance, which may apply also to produce which is certain. Tithing on the Sabbath is explicitly forbidden by Mishnah <i>Sabbath</i> 2:7, but the discussion here shows that the execution of a tithing plan already spelled out on Friday may be permitted.</i> says, even for certain produce, as we have stated there: “If he had <i>ṭevel</i> figs in his house and he was in the House of Study or on the field.” If you want to say that we deal with <i>demay</i>, you cannot do so, since it is stated<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 7, a direct continuation of Mishnah 6.</i>: “If it was <i>demay</i>.” What Rebbi Joḥanan said was that it deals with <i>demay</i>. He did not say, not with certain produce, but that this Mishnah deals with <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan asserted that this Mishnah deals with <i>demay</i>; he did not state that provisos can only be made for <i>demay</i>. Hence, the problem lies not in the possibility of a proviso but in its nature.</i>. What is the difference between <i>demay</i> and certain produce? For <i>demay</i>, a person may make a proviso for something that is not in his possession; for certain produce, he may only make a proviso for something that is in his possession.
Rebbi Yannai had made a proviso for certain produce<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">He had no time to tithe before the start of the Sabbath. (The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 93a" href="/Yevamot.93a">Babli, <i>Yebamot</i> 93a</a>, reports that his sharecropper used to bring him fruits every Friday afternoon, and that time he was so late that R. Yannai got nervous and made a proviso since the fruits would spoil if not eaten on the Sabbath.) Since provisos for certain produce were mentioned last, they are discussed first.</i>. He asked the Great Rebbi Ḥiyya, may one fix this on the Sabbath? He said to him (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:23" href="/Deuteronomy.14.23"><i>Deut</i>. 14:23</a>): “That you should learn to fear the Eternal, your God, all the days,” including the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">“You shall eat before the Eternal, your God, at the place that He shall choose to let His Name dwell there, the tithe of your grain, your wine, and your oil, the first-borns of your cattle and sheep, so that you should learn to fear the Eternal, your God, all the days.” Since the verse speaks of tithes, one may tithe on the Sabbath. {In the parallel version in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 93" href="/Yevamot.93">Babli <i>Yebamot</i> 93a/b</a>, the verse is taken to prove that one should make provisos so that certain produce on the Sabbath does not diminish one’s enjoyment of the Sabbath (interpretation of Rashi).}</i>. How did you see a way to be so lenient, my initiative<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The reading בלשי is attested to by all manuscript sources. The consensus of the commentators is that one should read בשלי, “it (a possible sin) hangs on me.” But then the final בי would be unnecessary. I am taking the word parallel to Arabic בלשׁ, “to initate, show initiative.” R. Yannai is reluctant to follow the lenient ruling of R. Ḥiyya, who praises him for his independent and careful position. [J. Levy reads מַקִּיל בַּלָּשֵי “the stick of the investigators”, the instrument of customs officials (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 15:4" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.15.4">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 15:4</a>) used to check whether a load of grain does not hide taxable goods.]</i> depends on me! He said to him, in the future you will carry leadership in Israel.
Rebbi Hoshaia had made a proviso for certain produce. One woman cooked vegetables she had forgotten to put in order. She came to Rebbi Hoshaia who sent Zavdi ben Levi<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">An Amora of the first generation, possibly a son of Levi ben Sissi; most of his original statements are in Aggadah.</i> to put it in order for him and for her. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal asked, would Zavdi ben Levi not have to give Rebbi Hoshaia property rights on these vegetables<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is forbidden on the Sabbath, how could Zavdi ben Levi possibly tithe on R. Hoshaia’s proviso, considering that for certain produce one may not make a proviso for produce not in one’s possession.</i>? Rebbi Zeïra asked, where do we hold? If his proviso was meant for him and for others, he does not have to give him property rights on the vegetable<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">What had been excluded was a proviso valid solely for another’s produce, as described in the Mishnah. It is a general principle accepted in all of Talmudic legislation that anything one can do for himself he can do for himself and others. Hence, a proviso valid for him and for all Jews of his locality will be perfectly legal even under the restriction outlined at the start.</i>. If his proviso was not meant for him and for others, he has to give him property rights on the vegetable. Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: It was answered, Zavdi ben Levi did not give Rebbi Hoshaia property rights on the vegetable<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, if one makes such a proviso (or, if the rabbi does,) it is advisable to make it for oneself and for all inhabitants of one’s town, as in the formula for an <i>eruv</i>.</i>.
Rebbi Yannai said, he must remember his proviso. Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, he must whisper with his lips<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Return to the discussion of the Mishnah. At the meal, the guest must remember that he tithes according to his prior proviso or whisper that he acts on that premise.</i>.
Rebbi Jeremiah<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">All his questions deal with the problem that the rabbis cannot institute rules which contradict Biblical prohibitions; they can only abrogate rabbinical rules under certain circumstances.</i> asked before Rebbi Zeïra: Is he not like somebody who makes repairs on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">To repair something otherwise unusable on the Sabbath is a capital crime under the heading of מכה בפטיש.</i>? He said to him, because of his proviso<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The essence of giving heave is to give it the name of heave, not the actual separation. Since the name was given on Friday, the actual separation is not the act that makes the food edible and is not Biblically forbidden.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah asked before Rebbi Zeïra: Is it not forbidden because of destruction of edibles<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The essence of giving heave is to give it the name of heave, not the actual separation. Since the name was given on Friday, the actual separation is not the act that makes the food edible and is not Biblically forbidden.</i>? He said to him, he crumbles a small amount and eats<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">It is a general prohibition, בל תשחית, to wantonly destroy usable things. In particular, foodstuffs may not be destroyed. The tiny amount the guest set aside for heave of the tithe must somehow be destroyed since under the circumstances it cannot be given to a Cohen.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah asked before Rebbi Zeïra: Is it not forbidden because of robbery<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">To take some of the host’s property without the latter’s knowledge is a violation of the host’s property rights and therefore should be forbidden (even if its value is less than a <i>peruṭa</i> and, hence, is not claimable in court).</i>? He said to him, the host desires the satisfaction of his guest<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Since without it, the guest would not eat at his place, the invitation implies the host’s acquiescence to the guest’s actions. Hence, it is not against the will of the host.</i>.
The words of the rabbis disagree since Rebbi Samuel bar Rebbi Isaac said: Rebbi and Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah were guests of a private person. Evil rumors told the latter, watch out, they are suspecting you<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">They do not trust you with tithes. It seems that they could not have refused the invitation, in particular as R. Yose ben R. Jehudah follows the teachings of his father, cf. Halakhah 2:2, second paragraph, even though he does not follow him in his opposition to anybody fixing the food which is another’s property, as in the next paragraph.</i>! He sat near them and was watching them<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The guests noticed that they were watched whether they would behave as if the host was an <i>am haäreẓ</i>.</i>. They pretended to throw food against each other and in that way were putting the food in order<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">They threw little pieces of food against each other. These little pieces were not eaten but served as heave of the tithe without the host’s knowledge.</i>. Does he not desire their satisfaction? He does, but he does not want to be suspected<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The produce probably was tithed but since the host was not publicly part of the circle of the trustworthy persons, his food must be considered <i>demay</i>.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 8:5" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).8.5">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 8:5</a>: “R. Jehudah said, can a person make a proviso about something not in his possession? But Rebbi Jehudah agrees that he may go and buy from the place from which his host bought.” Since the argument is Amoraic in our text, the Tosephta must represent a Babylonian edition.</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah forbids<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">He forbids these provisos even on weekdays and requires tithing by the guest before going to the party.</i>.” What is the reason of Rebbi Jehudah? Can a person make a proviso about something not in his possession? “But Rebbi Jehudah agrees that he may go, buy from the place from which his host bought, and tithe.” Can a person give for something that is not his? Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: they considered him as one who sold <i>ṭevel</i> produce to another person, as was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Maäser Šeni</i> 3:7, in slightly different language but identical meaning. R. Jehudah’s restriction that the tithing must be from the same source as the <i>ṭevel</i> is not mentioned.</i>: “He who sells <i>ṭevel</i> produce to another person has to run after him and put the produce in order. If he does not find him, if it is known that the produce still exists, he has to tithe for him<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Also for something that is not in the tither’s possession. The case discussed by R. Jehudah is parallel to the doubtful case here.</i>; if not, he does not have to tithe for him. If it is doubtful whether the produce exists or not, he tithes for it and gives a name to the tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Since all tithes have to be named even if only the heave of the tithes actually has to be separated and given.</i>.”

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> When<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">This Mishnah is a continuation of the first and refers to the proviso he makes both on Friday and at the meal.</i> they mixed him a cup, he says: What I shall leave at the rim of the cup shall be tithe, the rest of tithe near to it. What I made tithe shall be the heave of the tithe for everything, and the Second Tithe shall be on its top and be redeemed by coins.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> So is the Mishnah: Concerning the mixing of the cup<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Not that the proviso would be spelled out when they mix the cup, which would be on the Sabbath and forbidden; it is the proviso which has to be spelled out on Friday and then repeated, in a whisper, on the Sabbath.</i>.
Where are we at? If he says it beforehand, the drink is mixed<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since fluids move, if he declares the place of the heave at one moment, its molecules will have wandered away by the time he drinks, and he also drank of the heave, a deadly sin.</i>. If he says, when I shall have drunk, before that he drank <i>ṭevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">If he declares after he drank, he drank only <i>ṭevel</i>.</i>. What we are dealing with is that he says beforehand: When I shall have drunk<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">He must make the declaration beforehand in order to avoid drinking <i>ṭevel</i>. On the other hand, he cannot designate heave before he finished, in order to avoid drinking heave. Hence, he has to designate heave on Friday to be separated after he finished drinking.</i>. But does he then not move impure heave on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is eating at an <i>am haäreẓ</i>’s, all the food is considered impure. Hence, the heave also is impure and must be destroyed. But this cannot be done on the Sabbath; the impure heave is therefore of no use on the Sabbath and under the rules of <i>muqẓeh</i> cannot be moved.</i>? Rebbi Eleazar said, he leaves some profane<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">When he declares that the wine near the designated tithe should be tithe, he really does not want it to be all tithe but some drops of profane non-tithe must also remain. Since the profane wine may be moved, and since we have a principle that anything usable may be moved, the impure heave also can be moved.</i>, as what we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 21:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.21.1.1">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 21:1</a>: “One moves impure heave with pure and with profane food.” It seems that the switch impure/pure in our text is a scribal error since the following argument requires the order of subjects in the Mishnah. In the parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 3:3:2-11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.3.3.2-11"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 3:3</a> (fol. 30a), the quote of the Mishnah is correct. {Because of that switch, successive editors have mutilated the text in newer editions.}</i>: “One moves pure heave with impure one and with profane food.” But that does not compare. There, the impure is for the needs of the pure<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">There is no Yerushalmi on the Mishnah, and a parallel quote in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 3:3:2-11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.3.3.2-11"><i>Pesaḥim</i>3:3</a> (fol. 30a) has exactly the same text. The explanation of the Yerushalmi seems to be the same as that of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 142a" href="/Shabbat.142a">Babli (<i>Šabbat</i> 142a</a>), that impure heave can be moved on the Sabbath only if it is necessary to remove it in order to get at usable food, be it pure heave or profane.</i>, but here the profane is for the needs of the impure<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, there is no need to move the impure heave and it should not be permitted.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, when he sets down the cup it will be holy<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since we have to stipulate from the start that wine should become heave only when the guest has finished drinking, one may also stipulate that this should happen only when the guest has put down the cup and does not move it again. Then the question does not arise.</i>.
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 7:5. “He who buys wine from the Samaritans, since he buys wine which certainly is not tithed, he may declare that he will give designated amounts in the future and drink immediately.” In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 8:7" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).8.7">Tosephta, <i>Demay</i> 8:7</a>, this is R. Meïr’s opinion, restricted to somebody who bought on Friday and had no time to tithe it before sundown.</i>: “Is somebody buys wine from the Samaritans.” It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 8:7" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).8.7">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 8:7</a>. R. Meïr’s position is he never had a case of a broken wine bag; he would worry if a bag broke and was emptied without some drops remaining that could be designated as heave of the tithe.</i>: “Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Simeon forbid it, for the wine bag may spring a leak and it will turn out retroactively that he drank <i>ṭevel</i>.” Is our Mishnah not following Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The guest may drink before the heave was declared. Since the cup is filled at the place where one drinks, nothing bad can happen since even if something is spilled, the heave is in the drops clinging to the wall.</i>? Rebbi Jacob bar Idi said, all agree that the cup is at its place.

Halakhah 3
