<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A worker<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">An agricultural worker who is a <i>ḥaver</i> and has to be fed by his employer during the day.</i> who does not believe his employer takes one dried fig<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Of those supplied by his untrustworthy employer. Again, the tithes have to be named but only the heave of the tithe has actually to be taken out and separated for <i>demay</i>.</i> and says: This one and the next nine shall be tithe for ninety that I shall eat. This one is made heave of the tithe for them. The Second Tithe is at the end and it is redeemed by a coin<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">One <i>peruṭah</i> in a coin.</i>.<br>He saves one fig<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The worker of Mishnah 3. Since he has declared one fig to be heave of the tithe, that fig must be given to the Cohen. The anonymous Tanna requires the worker to give the fig from his allotment. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel requires the worker to give a fig, but one he buys, not one given to him by his employer since he is required to eat all the food he receives from his employer in order to be strong. Rebbi Yose requires the employer to give a fig to the Cohen if the worker so requires since that is a stipulation of the Court and automatically incorporated in all work contracts.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, he should not save because he reduces the work he will be able to do for his employer. But Rebbi Yose said, he should not save because that is a stipulation of the Court.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph does not belong here but in <i>Terumot</i> 2:4, where the text is identical except for בטילה instead of בטיבולה. The Mishnah in <i>Terumot</i> assumes that somebody gives a <i>seah</i> to a Levite and a <i>seah</i> to the poor; according to R. Meïr then he, or his worker, can eat eight <i>seah</i> based on the tithes he gave. The Sages permit only to eat according to the percentage of the tithe that still exists. Maimonides and R. Simson give two possible interpretations, one that the Mishnah there is a corollary to the Mishnah here and deals with a worker who receives food from his employer and sees the employer give to the Levite and the poor but does not know whether he gave as tithes or as gifts. R. Hoshaiah declares that the position of the Sages in that Mishnah is identical with the position of the Tanna in our Mishnah.</i> in the name of Rebbi Hoshaiah: They considered him a worker who does not trust his employer. The following is obvious: If the produce was burned, the heave is <i>ṭevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Since heave is taken to permit the rest to be eaten, if nothing can be eaten there is no heave, and heave and tithes have to be separated anew from the remainder. This refers to the case where the produce burned before the heave was delivered to the Cohen. If the heave burned after he declared it, the farmer did his duty.</i>. If the heave is burned, when the produce is eaten the heave will have been sanctified retroactively.
What did he save? From his food he separates the heave of the tithe.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text appears in Tosephta <i>Baba Meẓia</i>‘ 8:2: “A worker is not permitted to work for himself at night and rent himself out during the day, or plough with his cow in the evening and rent her out in the morning; he should not starve himself, torment himself, or give his food to his children, because he robs his employer of the work he will be able to do.”</i>: “A man should not plough with his cow at night and rent her out during the day, he should not work for himself at night and rent himself out during the day, he should not starve himself and not torment himself because he reduces the work he will be able to do for his employer.”
Rebbi Joḥanan went to some place where he found the schoolteacher weak<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simson reads אטימוייס, <i>Hagahot Maimuniot</i> on Maimonides <i>Śekhirut</i> 13:6 reads אטימוס, ἄθυμος “feeble” (M. Sachs), in <i>Cafṭor Waperaḥ</i> Chap. 39, אטמיאס. The translation follows <i>Hagahot Maimuniot.</i></i>. He asked them, what has happened<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that he suspected the school board of not paying a living wage.</i>? They said to him, he is fasting. He said to him, that is forbidden to you. If one says that it is forbidden for the work one does for flesh and blood, so much more for the work of the Holy One<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The elementary school teacher, who turns illiterates into literates and uncivilized into civilized, finishes the work of creation and therefore does God’s work.</i>, praise to Him!
Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi in the name of Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 8:6" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).8.6">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 8:6</a>: “R. Yose says that it is a stipulation of the Court that the heave of the tithe should be from the employer and the Second Tithe from the worker.” R. Simson reads the text of the Tosephta in the Yerushalmi. <i>Cafṭor Waperaḥ</i> Chap. 39 quotes the Tosephta, not the Yerushalmi. Maimonides <i>Maäser</i> 9:11 follows the Tosephta as current practice. It seems that the Yerushalmi gives the position of the anonymous Tanna, in contrast to the Tosephta which explicitly refers to R. Yose; the reading in the Yerushalmi should not be changed for the (probably Babylonian) Tosephta. Rav explains the position of the anonymous Tanna as practice; he must assume that Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel and R. Yose disagree, so both are single opinions that are disregarded in face of a majority.</i>: It is a stipulation of the Court that the heave of the tithe should be from the worker and the Second Tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>peruṭah</i> needed to redeem the second tithe.</i> from the employer.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If someone buys wine from Samaritans<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">If one accepts the Samaritans as Jews, the wine is kosher but it certainly is not tithed.</i> he may say: Two <i>log</i> that I shall separate in the future are heave, ten tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">It really should be only 9.8 <i>log</i> per one hundred, but one does not require him to go into details. Similarly, Second Tithe should be 10% of the remaining 89.2 <i>log</i>.</i>, and nine Second Tithe; then he redeems<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">He redeems the Second Tithe with a <i>peruṭah</i> from a designated coin; the redemption is effected by his declaration.</i> and drinks.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 8:7" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).8.7">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 8:7</a>: “If someone buys wine from Samaritans on Fridays and he forgot to tithe, he may say: Two <i>log</i> that I shall separate in the future are heave, the next ten tithe, and the next nine Second Tithe; then he redeems and drinks immediately; the words of R. Meïr. R. Jehudah, R. Yose, and R. Simeon forbid it. They said to R. Meïr, do you not agree that if his wine bag would spring a leak he would drink <i>ṭevel</i>? He said to them, if it would spring a leak.” R. Jehudah is not mentioned in the Halakhah; since he is the senior among those who disagree, the argument with R. Meïr is his and does not obligate the other two authorities who agree with R. Jehudah on the practical rule but not on the theoretical reason. Note that in the version of the Tosephta, the wine bag is already transported to the buyer’s house and, therefore, the risk of the bag springing a leak is minimized.</i>: “Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Simeon prohibit to do so, maybe the wine bag will spring a leak and it turns out retroactively that he drank <i>ṭevel</i>.” But the reason of one is not the reason of the other. The reason of Rebbi Yose is that maybe he will pour<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">At home, he will pour out the contents of the leather wine bag into a clay amphora for storage. Then the text of his declaration is no longer applicable and the buyer drank <i>ṭevel</i>.</i>. The reason of Rebbi Simeon is that maybe he will forget and drink the remainder<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Then he actually drank heave and heave of the tithe!</i>. As it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">A similar statement is in Tosephta <i>Maäser Šeni</i> 3:17: “If somebody said, the Second Tithe in this container should be redeemed by that <i>as</i>, and he did not define its place, R. Simeon says, he gave it its rightful name {and it is redeemed.} The Sages say, {it is not redeemed} until he says, at the North or South end.” The next Tosephta shows that the “Sages” here follow the opinion of R. Yose. The problem is that without a clear definition, the owner may spend the redemption coin on other things. Therefore, one requires him to spell out where the coin will be taken and, since coins move around in a wallet, it is clear that he has to remove the coin from the wallet immediately and separate it.</i>: “The Second Tithe in this container should be redeemed by the tetradrachma in that wallet, it is redeemed. Rebbi Yose says, it is not redeemed.” What is the reason of Rebbi Yose? “<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Maäser Šeni</i> 4:12: “If somebody says, the Second Tithe shall be redeemed by a tetradrachma that will come out of this wallet, or the denar that will come out of that wallet, R. Yose says, he did not redeem, but the Sages say, he did redeem. R. Yose agrees with the Sages that if he says that the Second Tithe shall be redeemed by the new tetradrachma which will come out of this wallet, or the new denar that will come out of that wallet, he did redeem. The Sages admit to Rebbi Yose that if he said [it shall be redeemed] by the tetradrachma that was in my son’s hand, the denar that was in my son’s hand, it is not redeemed since perhaps it was not in his hand at that time.” We have to assume that R. Yose agrees only in case the owner had only one new coin of that type at the moment of the declaration.</i> If he said, the old tetradrachma or the old denar, he did redeem. The Sages admit to Rebbi Yose that if he said, the tetradrachma I shall take from my son’s hand, or the denar I shall take from my son’s hand, it is not redeemed since it was not in his hand at that time.”
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.8.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 3:8</a>: “If somebody puts aside produce to serve for heave or tithes, or coins to serve for Second Tithe, he refers to them {to redeem produce or to put produce in order with the intention that all sanctity should devolve on produce and coins set aside} based on the prior knowledge that they still exist. If they are lost, he worries for 24 hours, the words of R. Eleazar (ben Shamua‘.)” In both the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 31" href="/Gittin.31">Babli (<i>Giṭṭin</i> 31a/b</a>) and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:8:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.8.2-5">Yerushalmi (<i>Giṭṭin</i> 3:8</a>), there is a discussion whether the tithing is questionable for the 24 hours preceding the discovery of the loss or whether the tithing is not questionable only for the first 24 hours after produce or coins were set aside. In any case, there is no opposition noted by either R. Yose or R. Simeon. Since transporting wine from Samaria presumably takes less than 24 hours, such opposition would have been expected.</i>: “If somebody puts aside produce … to serve for Second Tithe.” Does<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">This and the next sentence are copied from <i>Giṭṭin</i>. Therefore, our <i>baraita</i> is referred to as “there”, and the Mishnah as “here.”</i> that Mishnah disagree with Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Simeon? Rebbi Zeïra said, there they become spoiled retroactively, but here they become spoiled only starting from the moment of loss<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the heave was lost, since it was legal heave at the time it saves the produce from ever being <i>ṭevel</i> again.</i>.
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Meїlah</i> 6:6: “If a <i>peruṭah</i> of Temple property fell into somebody’s wallet, or if somebody said, a <i>peruṭah</i> in this wallet shall be Temple property, then the moment he spent the first coin he committed larceny, the words of Rebbi Aqiba. But the Sages say, not until he spent the entire wallet. Rebbi Aqiba agrees that if he said, ‘a <i>peruṭah</i> from this wallet shall be Temple property,’ he may go and spend the entire contents of the wallet.” מעל refers to the particular case of larceny involving Temple property, whose private use is forbidden even if it does not impair its value (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:15" href="/Leviticus.5.15"><i>Lev</i>. 5:15</a>). Our text is a <i>baraita</i> that combines the Mishnah with Tosephta <i>Meїlah</i> 3:1: “If a <i>peruṭah</i> of Temple property fell into somebody’s wallet, or if somebody said, a <i>peruṭah</i> in this wallet shall be Temple property, then the moment he spent the first coin he must bring an <i>ašam</i> sacrifice for a possible sin, for the second coin an <i>ašam</i> sacrifice for a certain sin, the words of Rebbi Aqiba. But the Sages say, an <i>ašam</i> sacrifice for a possible sin is brought only for an offense which if deliberate is punished by being cut off (כרת, cf. J. Milgrom, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1-16" href="/Leviticus.1-16">Leviticus 1–16</a>, New York 1991, pp. 457–460) or, if in error, must be atoned for by a sin-offering.”</i>: “If a <i>peruṭah</i> of Temple property fell into somebody’s wallet, or if somebody said, a <i>peruṭah</i> in this wallet shall be Temple property,” it was stated that “with the first coin he committed probable larceny, with the second one certain larceny<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of the Mishnah, that with the first coin he already committed certain larceny, is easier to understand. The difference between possible and certain larceny is in the kind of sacrifice that must be brought for atonement, as stated in the Tosephta. R. Moses Margalit explains the argument of R. Aqiba that the first coin, which was on top of the coins in the wallet, is not really “in” the wallet,” but the second coin certainly is “in the wallet.”</i>, the words of Rebbi Aqiba. But the Sages say, with any of them he committed probable larceny, except that with the last one he committed certain larceny.” Rebbi Yudan, the father of Rebbi Mattania said, that is, if there were ten coins. But if there were only two, with the first coin he committed probable larceny, with the second one certain larceny. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish asked before Rebbi Joḥanan: What is the difference between him who says “in the wallet” and him who says “from the wallet?<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the Mishnah and the distinction which R. Aqiba makes, that in the second case there is no larceny unless all coins are spent.</i>” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, even if he spent the entire wallet he would not have committed larceny; just as you say ‘that he drank <i>ṭevel</i> retroactively,’ here also ‘retroactively he spent profane money.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">If he separated the coin after he spent some of the money, the stigma of possible larceny should be removed from the rest he spent earlier.</i>’ But here it depends on the act of separation<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody agrees that <i>ṭevel</i> can be removed only by an actual declaration fixing place and modality of heave and tithes.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, in both cases it depends on the act of separation; it is as if he said, this wallet should not be freed from a <i>peruṭah</i> of Temple property<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The remaining coins cannot become profane except by either removing the dedicated piece from the wallet or designating it unequivocally as old or new, as in the preceding paragraph. In the Babli (<i>Meїlah</i> 34a), this answer is given by R. Joḥanan. But the thrust of the Babli is about retroactive clarification that is excluded here and in the entire discussion of the Yerushalmi, as recognized by <i>Sefer Nir</i>.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish’s argument is inverted. There<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Babli <i>Meїlah</i> 13b, in explanation of Mishnah <i>Meїlah</i> 3:7: “From water {drawn from the water canal flowing through the Temple courtyard on Friday afternoon of the festival of Tabernacles, to be used next morning for the water libation} in the golden barrel {that was not a sanctified vessel and in which the water would not become forbidden by standing in a vessel overnight} one may not have any use, but use is no larceny. Once it is poured into the pitcher {the holy vessel in which the water was transported to the altar} its use becomes larceny.” R. Simeon ben Laqish holds that there is no larceny from the water of the golden barrel if one uses the excess over the three <i>log</i> that are to be filled into the holy pitcher. The interpretation of the Yerushalmi seems to be that if the barrel contained <i>x log</i> and somebody drew <i>y log</i>, even if <i>x-y</i> &gt; 3 one would commit larceny in the amount of the cost of 3y/x <i>log</i> of water.</i>, he says that one commits larceny with a <i>peruṭah</i> corresponding to the computation of three <i>log</i>. And here, he says so? After he asked, he returned and gave the simple answer<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">His statement was a question and not a declaration; he himself gave the same answer later given by R. Jonah.</i>.
Rebbi Isaac ben Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Probably, this and the following paragraph refer to Mishnah 6. The same text in its main context appears in <i>Šabbat</i> 18:1.</i> said, a person can say on Friday, this shall be heave tomorrow<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Mishnah below, a person may not be pure for heave on Friday but he may be pure on the Sabbath. If his impurity (or that of his vessel) is derivative he contaminates holy things but not profane food. Hence, he must keep his food in profane state until nightfall. But at nightfall it will be Sabbath and he will not be permitted to change the status of food and separate heave and tithes. He must make a declaration on Friday to separate heave on the Sabbath automatically.</i>, but nobody can say on the Sabbath, this shall be heave tomorrow. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, nobody can say on Friday, this shall be heave tomorrow. A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tevul Yom</i> 4:4. The exact text of the Mishnah reads: “If a vessel which was immersed that day {and will be pure only after sundown} was filled from an amphora with <i>ṭevel</i> tithe, if he said that its contents should be heave of the tithe after nightfall, then this is heave of the tithe. If he said that it should be <i>eruv</i>, he did not say anything.” Tithe can be <i>ṭevel</i> only if the heave of the tithe was not taken; hence, ‘heave’ in our text should always mean ‘heave of the tithe’ as in the Mishnah.</i>: “If a vessel<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>lagena</i>,<i>lagaena</i>,<i>lagona</i>,<i>lagoena</i>, -<i>ae</i>, <i>f</i>. “large earthen vessel with neck and handles; flask, bottle”; same as Greek λάγηνος, perhaps identical with Κνίδιον, τὸ, “a measure of wine.”</i> was immersed that day and somebody filled it from an amphora with <i>ṭevel</i> tithe, if he said<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">On Friday.</i>, its contents should be heave of the tithe tomorrow, i. e., after nightfall, then this is heave. If he said, that should be <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Cf. Peah</i>, Chapter 7, Note 56. The <i>eruv</i> must be deposited on Friday afternoon and be at the right spot at sundown, so that he can acquire the Sabbath rest at that spot. But in our case, at sundown the wine is still <i>ṭevel</i>; since it may not be eaten it is not food, and the <i>eruv</i> is invalid.</i>, he did not say anything.” Explain it if he transgressed<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose ben R. Abun would agree that if someone incorrectly followed R. Isaac ben Eleazar’s prescription the declaration was valid.</i>. But Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: “He says<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">There is a <i>baraita</i> in which R. Ḥiyya presents the text of the declaration to be recited in the case considered by R. Isaac ben Eleazar. R. Ḥiyya unconditionally permits the declaration.</i>,” and you say, if he transgressed? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun retracted that. 

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody had <i>ṭevel</i> figs in his house but was in the House of Study or on the field<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">On Friday afternoon.</i>, he says: Two figs that I shall separate in the future shall be heave, ten tithe, and nine heave of the tithe. If they were <i>demay</i>, he says: That which I shall separate in the future shall be tithe tomorrow, the rest of tithe near to it. That which I made tithe shall be heave of the tithe for all, and the Second Tithe shall be to the North (or to the South) and be redeemed by coins<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>demay</i> declaration is identical with that of Mishnah 1 and all the rules explained there apply here also.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> What is done<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">What is the text of R. Ḥiyya’s declaration?</i>? ‘From before I shall separate it<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">When I shall separate it (on the Sabbath), the separation shall be valid from the moment of declaration (on Friday).</i>.’ Think about it, if it was pure heave, ‘from before I shall eat it<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">If a Cohen makes the declaration, he may say so since then he does not have to separate once it was declared to be heave. If another person makes the declaration, he may say: From the moment that a Cohen eats it.</i>.’ Think about it, if it was impure heave, ‘from before I shall deposit it in a corner<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Since impure heave must be burned, which is forbidden on the Sabbath, the only thing one can do with impure heave on the Sabbath is putting it away so that it should not be used.</i>.’
We have stated: “One may move<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">On the Sabbath. Since impure heave cannot be used, one would expect that it could not be moved.</i> both pure and impure heave.” Rebbi Zeïra said, it means that one is permitted to move <i>ṭevel</i> that has a condition on it<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">That heave is taken retroactively on the Sabbath to make the <i>ṭevel</i> edible. In that case, the <i>ṭevel</i> is food and certainly may be moved on the Sabbath.</i> on the Sabbath. What does he do<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the next Mishnah, or to the declaration implied by R. Isaac ben Eleazar, that a person may say on Friday that a certain part of food shall become heave or heave of the tithe on the Sabbath without actually separating heave and profane food on Friday.</i>? He thinks about a part of it<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The part destined to be heave has to be kept in mind all the time while the rest is consumed.</i> and eats the rest.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he had before him two baskets of <i>ṭevel</i> and he said, the tithes of one are in the other and those of the other in the one, the first one is tithed<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">At the moment when he said that the tithes of the first basket are in the second one, the first basket was in order and is fully profane food. Hence, when he comes to tithe the second basket, he cannot use the first one since profane food never can become tithe. Hence, what he says of the first basket is valid; what he says about the second basket is invalid, it is as if he never said it. He is in trouble with the second basket since he must separate the Second Tithe before he tithes the second basket, which is forbidden. In addition, Second Tithe must be given from tithed produce; hence the tithe of the second basket has to include the produce taken for Second Tithe from that basket. How to compute the amount of tithe to be taken for the first basket is discussed in the Halakhah.</i>; (if he said) their tithes are tithes of each basket in the other one, he gave it a name<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, everything is simultaneous. Hence, the designations of tithes are valid and he must separate the tithe of the first basket from the second, those of the second basket from the first, and he may not change his mind.<br>Most Mishnah codices (including the Rome ms. of the Yerushalmi but excluding the quote of the Mishnah in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 25b" href="/Temurah.25b">Babli <i>Temurah</i> 25b</a>) have a longer text of the Mishnah which, however, brings nothing new.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> 8<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Halakhah 6 in the Venice print.</i>: We have stated for this<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 8:15-16" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).8.15-16">Tosephta, <i>Demay</i> 8:15–16</a> has a similar wording but cannot possibly be the basis of the quote here and either represents a different tradition from that of the Yerushalmi or is hopelessly corrupt. The latter is likely since the second part of 8:16, discussed here in Halakhah 7, is corrupt, as noted by all commentators from R. M. Margalit to R. S. Lieberman. The relevant part of the Tosephta reads: “If he had before him two baskets of <i>ṭevel</i>, in each one a hundred, of which heave was taken. ‘The tithes of one are in the other one,’ the first one is tithed. ‘The tithes of one are in the other, and those of the other in the one’ the first one is not tithed. He takes two figs and two tenths and a tenth of a tenth. ‘Their tithes are tithes of each basket in the other one,’ he gave it a name.”</i>: “He takes from the second one two figs and two tenths and a tenth of a tenth<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">How does one tithe the first basket out of the second one? First he takes two figs (assuming 100 figs are there in all) for heave. Then he takes two tenths for the two tithes. But tithe should be taken from the produce itself; after tithe was taken, the profane remainder is nine times the tithe. Hence, ten figs of the second basket free only 90 figs of the first basket; 1/10 from above is 1/9 from below. The Tanna then requires one fig to be taken in addition to free the remaining 10 figs. Samuel protests, since 1/9 = 0.1111…, if one includes tenths, the tithing will never end since one always has to add another tenth of the preceding amount.</i>.” Samuel says, you cannot state that; when he takes one in ten he has to take one in a hundred, when he takes one in a hundred he has to take one in a thousand, when he takes one in a thousand he has to take one in ten thousand.
Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina said, for the first basket he transgresses a commandment, for the second a commandment and a prohibition. For the first basket he transgresses a commandment since he gave both names simultaneously<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 3:7 states that the First Tithe is called “beginning” since it contains heave of the tithe, but the Second Tithe is never called “beginning.” Hence, the beginning should precede the non-beginning.</i>, and for the second a commandment and a prohibition since he gave both names simultaneously and he put the Second Tithe of the first basket before the First Tithe of the second basket<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 4" href="/Temurah.4">Babli <i>Temurah</i>4a/b</a>, both R. Yose bar Ḥanina and R. Eleazar agree that declaring the Second Tithe before the First is a violation. R. Yose bar Ḥanina holds that transgressing a prohibition by words alone is not punishable in criminal law while R. Eleazar declares it punishable.</i>.
Rebbi Eleazar said, this is from Rebbi Meïr since Rebbi Meïr says that you accept only the very first version. There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 5:4" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.5.4">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i> 5:4</a>: “This one {animal} shall be a substitute for a burnt offering {another animal already dedicated} and a substitute for an offering of well-being, then it is a substitution for a burnt offering, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Yose said, if this was his original intention then his words stand since it is impossible to pronounce two names at the same time. But if he changed his mind after he said, this one shall be a substitute for a burnt offering, and said, this one should be a substitute for an offering of well-being, then it is a substitute for a burnt offering.” Our Mishnah cannot be R. Yose’s since he would accept both the first and second versions if the original intention was the same. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 25b-26a" href="/Temurah.25b-26a">Babli, <i>Temurah</i> 25b/26a</a>, the difference between R. Meïr and R. Yose is reduced to the stylistic nicety whether there is a difference in meaning between “a substitute for a burnt offering and a substitute for a peace offering” and “a substitute for a burnt offering and an offering of well-being.” It is difficult to square that approach with that of the Yerushalmi.</i>: “This one shall be a substitute<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">While substituting one sacrifice for another is forbidden (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:9-10" href="/Leviticus.27.9-10"><i>Lev.</i> 27:9–10</a>), nevertheless once done it is a valid dedication.</i> for a burnt offering and for an offering of well-being, then it is a substitute for a burnt offering, the words of Rebbi Meïr.” Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The Amora. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Temurah 3:3" href="/Tosefta_Temurah.3.3">Tosephta (<i>Temurah</i> 3:5</a>) there are three opinions, that of R. Meïr (who takes only the first statement) and R. Yose (the Tanna, who asks to investigate what was in the mind of the speaker) as in the Mishnah, and that of the Sages who say that the animal should be put out to graze until it develops a bodily defect (and no longer could become a sacrifice) when it should be sold and the money received be split 50-50 to buy burnt and well-being offerings.</i> said, we were of the opinion that Rebbi Meïr and the Sages disagree only after the time for speech<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">The measure of “time of speech,” the unit for a simple sentence, is defined in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:1:2-20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.1.2-20"><i>Berakhot</i> 2:1,</a> fol. 4b (Notes 50–51).</i>, but not within the time for speech. Since Rebbi Eleazar said, the Mishnah is Rebbi Meïr’s, it means that even within the time of speech he cannot retract.
This is the Mishnah: “Their tithes are tithes for each basket in the other one, he gave it a name,” because he declared both names at the same time. One after the other you accept only the very first version. Rebbi Abuna<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Usually called Rebbi Abun.</i> said, the Sages agree with Rebbi Meïr about him who says “this heave substitutes for Second”<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Since heave cannot become tithe; if he first declares it to be heave and then tithe, the second declaration is void. The words in parenthesis seem to be a scribal error induced by the next clause.</i> (that he is guilty). “This animal instead of well-being offerings,” he is obligated for two since he declared two names<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">By using a plural. An unspecified plural always means two, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.3.4.1"><i>Peah</i>, Chapter 3, Note 78</a>.</i> at the same time; hence, one after the other you accept only the very first version. Not so, said Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac who said in the name of Rav Huna: “This one shall be a substitute for a burnt offering,” he may retract that within the time for speech. That is, if he wants to retract<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Huna does not refer to our case but to a person making a substitution and retracting it. In our case, the speaker makes a substitution and then adds to it; he compounds his sin, and the rule of “time for speech” does not apply.</i>, there if he wants to add. If one said, “this is heave,” even within the time of speech he cannot retract. A person can be asked about his dedication<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">All dedications of sacrifices are vows, which are subject to annullment by a court if the circumstances change in which the vow was made. Hence, there is reason to allow a time of reflection. Heave is holy by declaration, not by vow, and no retraction is possible.</i> but nobody can be asked about his heave.
How does one act<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last part of Mishnah 8, if he has two baskets and says, “their tithes are tithes of each basket in the other one; he gave it a name.” The parallel is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 8:16" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).8.16">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 8:16</a>, in badly garbled form: “Their tithes are tithes of each basket in the other one, he gave it a name. One takes 20 figs from the one he chooses. In one 100, in one 200, if he takes from the small one, he takes 11, if from the large one, he takes it all and it is not enough. In one 100, in the other 1000, if he takes from the small one he takes 16, if he takes from the large one he takes all of 60. In other cases, by this computation.” In the computation, the second number in either case was switched.</i>? “One takes 20 figs from any basket one chooses<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Since the name of tithe was given for both simultaneously, the tithes have to be taken together. If one took tithes for one of the baskets alone, then the rest of the baskets would be profane and could no longer be used to tithe anything.</i>.” And in any 10 one takes there is one of heave of the tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">In all these computations of the preceding Halakhah and the current one, one separates all the tithes but in case of <i>demay</i> only heave of the tithe has to be given away; the Second Tithe is redeemed by a coin. In the current <i>baraita</i>, only First Tithe is considered.</i>. That is, if they were equal. “If in one basket there were 100 and in the other 200, if he takes for the one with a hundred, he takes 11<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">If he takes the tithe of the smaller basket out of the larger basket, he has to take 11 since there remain 100 profane and not 90 as there would be if he tithed the basket for itself. (To be mathematically exact, he should take 11.1111… figs, but if at all possible, tithing other than whole fruits is avoided.)</i>. If for the one with 200 he takes 30<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, the smaller basket is treated as appendix of the larger one, and the two are tithed together.</i>. “If in one basket there were 100 and in the other 1000, if he takes for the one with a hundred, he takes 15<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">This is incomprehensible (as is the explanation of R. Moses Margalit.) R. Eliahu Fulda, R. Eliahu Wilna and R. S. Lieberman emend and read 11 instead of 15, as in the previous sentence.<br>The last case is again clear; he would need 110.</i>. If for the one with a thousand, even if he takes the entire basket he does not complete taking.”
“<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The text is very close to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 8:17" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).8.17">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 8:17</a>. In all these cases, it is supposed that heave was already taken and only tithe with its heave has to be taken.</i> If someone has a basket and wants to tithe it, he takes two ninths and a ninth of a ninth, corresponding to ten tithe and nine Second Tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">Since 2/9 + 1/81 = 19/81, one takes 19 figs for any 81 remaining. For all other numbers of figs, one has to multiply by this ratio.</i>.” Why does he not say 19? Rebbi Zeïra said, the words of the Sages and their riddles. He who wants to bring a hundred figs in order into his house takes for ever single fig two ninths and a ninth of a ninth, 23 figs in all, four called ninth of a ninth<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">By the preceding computation, for a remainder of 100 one has to take 19×100÷81 = 23.4568 figs. Hence, the interpretation of the final statement must be “23 whole figs, four tenths, and 4/81 = 23.4494” The word אמר was emended by all commentators but the text is confirmed by the arithmetic.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A hundred <i>ṭevel</i> in a hundred profane, he removes 101<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The basis of these computations is Mishnah <i>Ḥallah</i> 3:9, where it is stated that if tithed and untithed produce are mixed together, it is preferable to tithe the untithed part from other produce, but if that proves impossible the whole turns into titheable produce as far as heaves are concerned, but not for other tithes. Since we are dealing with <i>demay</i>, it is always assumed that the first heave was taken and the only problem is heave of the tithe. Hence, in our case, one has to remove the 100 as equivalent of the <i>ṭevel</i> to be tithed, and, since the profane returns to the status of untithed as far as heave of the tithe is concerned, another 1% for heave of the tithe.</i>. A hundred <i>ṭevel</i> in a hundred tithe, he removes 101<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">The tithe here is First Tithe, of which heave of the tithe has been taken. First Tithe of which heave has been taken is fully profane in the hands of the Levite. Hence, the rules are the same as for profane produce.</i>. A hundred profane fully in order and a hundred tithe, he removes 110<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously, the tithe here did not have its heave removed, otherwise it would be profane. Hence, the status of the profane now equals that of tithe and heave of the tithe must also be removed from the profane. It turns out that he removes 100 for tithe and another 10 for heave of the tithe, so that the entire heave of the tithe will be 20.<br>In this Mishnah, “profane” means produce tithed according to the rules of <i>demay</i>, and “profane fully in order” is produce of which it is known that both tithes were actually removed according to the rules of certain produce.</i>.<br>A hundred <i>ṭevel</i> and ninety tithe, ninety <i>ṭevel</i> and eighty tithe, he did not lose anything. That is the rule: Anytime that <i>ṭevel</i> is more, he did not lose anything<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Maimonides explains that the general rule of taking out a replacement for heave from any produce into which it fell needs a minimum of 101 to be taken out. Since here the tithe is less than the <i>ṭevel</i>, an application of that rule would make the tithe forever <i>dema‘</i> .</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">In the Venice print, Halakhah 8.</i>: Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, one has to specify a stipulation and say: If <i>ṭevel</i> is in my hands, it shall be tithe, but if tithe is in my hands, it shall be heave of the tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the case where <i>ṭevel</i> and tithe were mixed together and one takes 101 parts for the <i>ṭevel</i> of which the one part in excess is to be addititional heave of the tithe. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish states that heave can only be taken if it is specified from where it is to be taken. In our case, heave of the tithe can only be taken if the produce was first designated to be tithe. Hence, it is necessary to stipulate that it should be designated tithe if part of the grain originated from what was <i>ṭevel.</i></i>. Rebbi Jonah said, one has to add the converse stipulation and say, otherwise I did not do anything<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jonah’s remark is a technicality. Since the rules of stipulations in Jewish law are derived from the stipulation Moses made with the tribes of Gad and Reuben (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 32:29-30" href="/Numbers.32.29-30"><i>Num.</i>32:29–30</a>) in which it was spelled out first what would happen if the conditions were met and second what would happen if they were not, any stipulation in Jewish law is valid only if it is formulated both in the positive and the negative sense. So R. Jonah simply means that the stipulation has to be legally valid.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, one does not have to add the converse stipulation<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">One does not need a stipulation at all; a simple declaration is sufficient.</i>. Either one of them follows his own opinion, since they disagreed<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Terumot</i>, Chap. 3. The obligation of heave starts when grain, after threshing, is formed into heaps or put into a silo. If somebody has two heaps and he says, the heave of both heaps should be in one of them, without specifying in which one, then R. Joḥanan is of the opinion that both heaps are potentially profane mixed with heave, and both of them fall under the strict rules of <i>dema</i>‘ until heave is taken. But R. Simeon ben Laqish holds that heave can only be taken from a designated spot; hence, the declaration is invalid and nothing has changed. It follows that, in our case, R. Simeon ben Laqish must require a declaration which fixes the exact part from which heave of the tithe may be taken, but R. Joḥanan holds that any produce that is potentially heave may be turned into heave by simple declaration.</i>: Two heaps of grain, in one of them. Rebbi Joḥanan said, they are both forbidden as <i>dema‘</i>, but Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, they are not forbidden.
Rebbi Mana said, they differ with Rebbi Eleazar ben Arakh<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">One of the foremost students of Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai, whose influence on the development of the Mishnah is minimal since after the fall of Jerusalem he did not go to Jabneh but to Emmaus in the foothills of the Judean mountains. The problem here does not come from the position of R. Eleazar but from the <i>baraita</i> in which he is mentioned.</i>. As he stated<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 5:15" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).5.15">Tosephta <i>Terumot</i> 5:15</a>: “<i>Ṭevel</i> mixed with profane produce makes it forbidden even in the tiniest amounts. If it can be provided from another place, one takes it out in proportion. Otherwise, Rebbi Eliezer and Rebbi Eleazar ben Arakh say, he gives a name to the heave of the tithe and takes it out by 101; the same holds if it falls into tithe.” The <i>baraita</i> in the Yerushalmi expresses the same but from another source. The assertion in the Tosephta that one always tithes in proportion contradicts the Mishnah since no penalty is incurred in form of heave of the tithe of the profane produce, as in the Mishnah.</i>: “<i>Ṭevel</i> mixed with profane produce, if it can be provided from another place, one takes it out in proportion. Otherwise, Rebbi Eleazar ben Arakh says, he gives a name to the heave of the tithe and takes it out by 101.” Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, explain it with kinds one cannot take out<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Special kinds of nuts and pomegranates and a few other kinds, enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 3:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.3.5.1">Mishnah <i>Orlah</i> 3:7</a>; they are always sold by the piece and cannot become absorbed by anything into which they fall. Hence, no substitute can be taken out from any other produce and R. Eleazar ben Arakh himself precribes for them the remedy explained in the Mishnah.</i>. Rebbi Hillel ben Pazi<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">A Galilean Amora of the fourth generation, usually quoted in discussions with R. Jonah and R. Yose. His son, R. Simeon ben R. Hillel ben Pazi, is quoted once in the Talmud.</i> asked: Why can he not crack them? Rebbi Jonah said, is one permitted to crack them<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 3:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.3.6.1">Mishnah <i>Orlah</i> 3:8</a>: If the nuts were cracked, or the pomegranates split,…, they may be taken out (like any other produce.)” In reference to tithes, the same is stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 5:10" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).5.10">Tosephta <i>Terumot</i> 5:10</a>.</i>? Did we not state: “If the nuts were cracked,” in the past. That means, not to start out with<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">If nuts are forbidden because of heave nuts fallen among them, one may not shell all the nuts to reduce them to regular produce.</i>.
Rebbi Jonah said, so is the Mishnah: Anytime that <i>ṭevel</i> is more than the tithe, tithe does not lose anything<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the preceding Mishnah, the rule given here applies only to tithe, not to profane produce.<br>R. Simson reads: “Whenever the <i>ṭevel</i> exceeds the tithe by at least 10, one does not lose anything.” Maimonides and R. Abraham ben David clearly read the Yerushalmi as given in the text.</i>.
Rebbi Levinti<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">All that is known about him is that he was a fifth generation Amora, student of R. Jonah.</i> in the name of Rebbi Jonah: Any <i>ṭevel</i> mentioned here is <i>ṭevel</i> for first and second tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">All <i>ṭevel</i> mentioned in the entire chapter refers to produce of which heave was taken and the question is only about tithes and heave of the tithe.</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu said, Rebbi and Rebbi Dositheos the son of Rebbi Yannai<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna of the fifth generation, probably a student of R. Meïr, chiefly prominent as a preacher. It is possible that his father was the Tanna quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pirkei Avot 4:15" href="/Pirkei_Avot.4.15"><i>Avot</i> 4:15</a>.</i> disagree. One says, he takes the second tithe from another place, but the other one says, he makes all of it second tithe and takes the first tithe from another place<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">This cannot refer to the Mishnah, since there the tithe is reconstituted as it was before. It must refer to the <i>baraita</i> quoted in the following sentences, in which the Tanna disagrees with the Tanna of the Mishnah and applies the rule of Mishnah 9 also to our case. If tithe returns to the state of untithed, then Second Tithe also is due, but it cannot be taken out here without disturbing the entire computation. Hence, it must be taken, and then redeemed, from other sources.</i>. What are we talking about? “A hundred <i>ṭevel</i> and ninety tithe, he takes 92 minus one tenth<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">If he takes 90 out of the container, it might be that all he takes was originally <i>ṭevel.</i> From this, he can legally only take 9 for heave of the tithe. But he has to take in all 10.9 by the rules of the previous Mishnah which the Tanna here also follows in case tithe is less than <i>ṭevel.</i> Hence, he has to take another 2-0.1 and then give 10.9 as heave of the tithe. If <i>ṭevel</i> is 100 and tithe 80, he has to take 8 + 2.8 as heave of the tithe in order to put tithe and <i>ṭevel</i> in order. In general, for 100 <i>ṭevel</i> and <i>n</i> tithe, he takes <i>n +</i>(1000 - 9n)/100. (Explanation by R. Z. Frankel, the only rational explanation of this paragraph.)</i>. A hundred <i>ṭevel</i> and eighty tithe, he takes 83 minus two tenths.<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:17-18" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.17-18">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 5:17–18</a>, which certainly is garbled but might in addition represent a Babylonian tradition opposed to that of the Yerushalmi: “100 tithe and 80 <i>ṭevel</i>, he takes 91.9. 100 tithe and 90 <i>ṭevel</i>, he takes 92.8. Other quantities proportionally.”</i> Other quantities proportionally.”

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody had ten rows of ten wine jugs<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 27a" href="/Bava_Kamma.27a">Babli, <i>Baba Qama</i> 27a</a>, notes that the Mishnah starts with “jugs” and continues with “amphoras,” to indicate that Biblical כד corresponds to rabbinic חבית.</i> each, and he said that one from an outer row was tithe but he does no longer know which one, he takes two diagonally<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λοξός, ή, όν “slanting, crosswise”.</i> opposite amphoras<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Let the rows be numbered by <i>i</i>= 1, 2,…,10 and the amphoras in each row by <i>j =</i> 1, 2,…,10. Then the position of each amphora is uniquely described by a couple (<i>i</i>,<i>j</i>) and the amphoras are filling a square. Also, let * denote any number from 1 to 10. The outer rows are (1,*), (10,*), (*,1), (*,10). Since the problem is not the tithe but the heave of the tithe, if one takes both amphoras (1,1) and (10,10) [or (1,10) and (10,1)] he will have taken one amphora from each boundary row and have valid heave of the tithe. From the last sentence of the Mishnah it becomes clear that in all cases, only one amphora has to be given as heave of the tithe. Hence, the interpretation here is that one takes half of each amphora chosen and pours into a new one, so that the full new amphora will be the heave of the tithe.</i>. One of half an outer row was tithe but he does no longer know which one, then he takes the four amphoras at the four corners<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">Since each semi-row ends at a corner, if he takes one quarter of any of the four at the corners, one of them will be the right one.</i>. One row was tithe but he does no longer know which one, then he takes one diagonal row<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">If he takes either (<i>i</i>,<i>i</i>) for <i>i</i> = 1,…, 10 or (10-<i>i</i>+1,<i>i</i>) for <i>i</i> = 1,…, 10 then he will have taken one amphora from each row counted in either direction. Here, he takes one tenth out of each amphora chosen.</i>. Half a row was tithe but he does no longer know which one, then he takes from the two diagonal rows<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">The rows <i>(i</i>,<i>i)</i> and (10-<i>i</i>+1,<i>i</i>) for <i>i</i> = 1,…, 10. Then one amphora for each half-row starting at the border will have been taken. Here, one takes one twentieth of each amphora chosen.</i>. One amphora was tithe but he does no longer know which one, then he takes<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">He takes 1% of the volume of each amphora, then he gets the required amphora out of the 100 given and from each amphora some wine is in the new barrel.</i> from each amphora.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Why does he not take two<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the second sentence in the Mishnah, when he made half a row tithe on 50 of the 100 amphoras. Why can he not declare that the other half is also tithe for the remaining 50 amphoras and reduce this case to the preceding one where he has to take only from two diagonally opposed amphoras? The answer is that the remaining amphoras are already profane, not <i>ṭevel</i>, and the method proposed is impossible. (Explanation of R. Eliahu Fulda.)</i>? Rebbi Cohen in the name of the rabbis of Caesarea: When he separated half of the heave from another place.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">There is a similar Tosephta, <i>Demay</i> 5:22–23, which first explains how to take two pitchers and the declarations to make, as in the <i>baraita</i> here. But then there is no disagreement reported but it is stated that the Cohen who drinks must immerse himself not only before drinking but between the drinks and after them, combining the rules of Rebbi and R. Eleazar ben R. Simeon. That Tosephta explains the practical rule and not the background as does the <i>baraita</i> here. Nevertheless, the text of the Tosephta cannot stand as it is in manuscript and print: “The pitchers, one of impure <i>ṭevel</i> tithe, the other of pure <i>ṭevel</i> tithe. He drinks from one and immerses himself, and drinks from the other and immerses himself, and drinks from both of them together, and needs an immersion at the end.” All commentators of the Tosephta feel compelled to rearrange the Tosephta according to the Yerushalmi.</i>: “If there were two amphoras before him, one of pure tithe which was <i>ṭevel</i> and one of impure tithe which was <i>ṭevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Only pure heave may be consumed; impure heave of oil may be used as fuel but impure heave of wine cannot be used at all. Hence, if the Cohen is the owner of the tithe, he wants to take heave so that he can drink everything.</i>, he brings two vessels and takes from each of them heave of the tithe for both of them. He then turns and lifts one vessel and says: If this one is pure, I am making it heave of the tithe; else I did not do anything. Then he turns, does the same for the second vessel, immerses himself and drinks, the words of Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">Since no Cohen may eat or drink heave without first immersing himself, it is obvious that here the Cohen did immerse himself first. Hence, the immersion required by Rebbi is between the drinks from both vessels.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says, he takes it<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">A scribal error for טובל “he immerses himself.”</i> only at the end.” What is the reason of Rebbi? I say, maybe he started drinking the impure one first<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">Then he has to purify himself again for the heave since the first pitcher was profane drink.</i>. And what is the reason of Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon? Maybe he drank the impure one at the end. Everybody agrees that he who drinks impure liquids may not even eat heave that is in doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody drinks impure fluids (in a minimum of a <i>reviït</i>, about 135 cm<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup>) then, by rabbinic decree (<i>Zavim</i> 5:12), his body becomes impure even if the impurity was only secondary. (Biblical impurity is transferred to a person only by original impurity.) If the impurity is certain, then by rabbinic decree he is impure even if it is in doubt whether the food is heave or not. But if the drink is only suspected of being impure there is no rabbinic decree and impurity is imparted only by original impurity. The discussion here assumes that all impurities discussed are rabbinic in character.</i>. If the liquids were possibly impure, everybody agrees that he may eat what certainly is heave. But here we deal with another Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zavim</i> 5:12.</i>. What is the reason of Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon? Since according to both the impurity is certain, he needs immersion<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">At the end, the contents of one of the vessels certainly was impure and made his body impure.</i>. What is the reason of Rebbi? That he should not be led into drinking a <i>reviït</i><sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">If he drinks less the immersion is not really necessary; it is a reminder not to make one’s body impure by drinking a <i>reviït</i> of a questionable liquid.</i>. According to the reason of Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon, he drinks less than a <i>reviït</i><sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, no immersion at the end would be necessary. Hence, the argument is faulty because otherwise Rebbi and R. Eleazar would not quarrel, one speaking about a person drinking less than a <i>reviït</i>, the other about one who drinks more.</i>. Is Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon not afraid that he would be led into drinking a <i>reviït</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, the only explanation you have is our first one. Rebbi is afraid that he drank the impure liquid first and Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon is afraid that he drank the impure liquid at the end<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, the practical rule is the one given in the Tosephta.</i>.
